                                 Books by the Author
1. 3D Unconditional Security—graphical overview of principle perspectives regarding unconditional security.
2. 4D Forgiveness—examines four levels of biblical forgiveness and explains how it is possible for God to
    forgive and forget our sin in one stratum while not forgiving or forgetting our sin in another stratum.
3. Breaking the Rocking Horse—introductory book regarding the distinction between having eternal life as a
    gift versus obtaining it as a reward in relation to the outer darkness and the parable of the soils.
4. Carnal Corinth—an affirmation of the categorical existence of carnal Corinthian Christians.
5. Destined for Salvation—evaluates five major models for interpreting 1Thess 5:9-10 and takes the passage
    as promising an unconditional rapture (and thus unconditional security) to all believers in Christ, even to
    those who are morally asleep. However, an incentive for being morally awake is provided by making
    salvation (in the form of millennial and eternal rulership) contingent upon being morally alert.
6. Fallen from Grace but Not from Perfection—an advanced defense of unconditional security which affirms
    that believers can fall from grace but not fall from perfection.
7. Is God a Communist?—by comparing Paul’s teaching of the workmen with Jesus’ parable of the laborers,
    this book refutes communistic views of Heaven which think that Jesus is building heavenly mansions in
    New Jerusalem for all believers regardless of the way they live.
8. John in Living Color—a color-coded translation and reference manual of the Gospel of John that focuses
    on the grammatical and aspectual indications of unconditional security.
9. Like a Tree Planted by the Water—expounds upon the OT background for the outer darkness by examining
    the precursor texts in Psalms, especially Ps 1-2.
10. Logic Tables and Diagrams—illustrations of logic with scriptural implications pertaining primarily to the
    soteriological and misthological arenas.
11. Mere Christianity and Moral Christianity—a more comprehensive discussion regarding perseverance (and
    other performance-related themes) as being needed for rewards in heaven rather than for entrance into
    heaven. This book provides both introductory material and advanced treatments.
12. Monogamous Sex in Heaven!—at the same time in which the present author was reading books by Marrow
    and Kreeft posing that sexual intimacy continues in Heaven, he discovered that his wife had stage-3 breast
    cancer. As a therapy to help him deal with this news, he poured himself into research to see if Marrow and
    Kreeft might be correct, thereby enabling him to believe that no matter what happened, the best was yet to
    come. This is the conclusion and comfort of this book.
13. New Birth by the Sovereign Wind—refutes the Calvinistic interpretation of Jn 3:16 that limits God’s love
    to the elect. An excursus deals with the Calvinistic claim from Jn 3:3 that one must be born again before
    one can see (i.e., believe) the gospel. An additional excursus entertains a possible duality to what it means
    to see the kingdom.
14. Predestination and the Open View—a discussion of predestination that focuses on free will and the book
    of life from an open view.
15. Rewards are Eternal—is a defense of the eternal nature of biblical rewards against the proposition that
    Rev 22:5 indicates that all believers will rule in the eternal state and against the premise that Rev 21:4
    proves that misthological death will no longer exist in the eternal state.
16. Sealed and Secure—a thorough defense of a popular argument for unconditional security that also defends
    the premise of unconditional promises.
17. The Race of Grace—introductory book in which performance-related themes (such as perseverance) are
    considered essential for rewards in heaven. Entrance into heaven, in contrast, is regarded as free. A strong
    defense of unconditional security derived from believers being given to Jesus is a strength of this book.
18. The Outer Darkness—a magnum opus that ties together the author’s arguments for unconditional security
    and conditional rewards.
19. Trichotomy—an advanced holistic dichotomous discussion of trichotomy with an integration of nikology.
20. Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep—a rejection of the notion that the profession of salvation is proven
    genuine by the way a person lives. This book focuses on the parable of the sheep and the goats and shows
    the inadequacy of conditional security in interpreting this parable.

                                       Purpose Statement
       The first two chapters of Dual Sealing and Security present most of the original discussion Sealed and Secure,
which was an intermediate-level demonstration that being sealed by the Spirit is an unconditional promise to anyone
who has ever believed savingly in Christ for eternal life. Specifically, remaining sealed is not conditioned on the
believer persevering in the faith. Soteriological security was thus affirmed. However, Dual Sealing and Security
advances beyond that original discussion by providing two appendixes which pose that there is a secondary sense
to the sealing that may, in certain cases, be conditioned on the believer’s subsequent perseverance in faith and
faithfulness. Misthological contingency, as a secondary vantage point, is thus affirmed as well, resulting in an
advanced discussion of soteric-mistholic duality. Two supporting excurses are also added.

                                          Chapter 1.
                                      Guaranteed Security
                                                    Introduction
      The biblical affirmation regarding the sealing of the believer in Christ is a particularly strong affirmation of
unconditional security.1 This book will explore the arguments both for and against this understanding of this biblical
theme. Just how secure is the believer in Christ? The present chapter will focus on texts related most directly to the
believer’s sealing. The next chapter will dig beneath the surface to consider whether implicit conditionality might
be found beneath the surface of the explicit security found in these texts.
                                                        Text
      In Him, you also, after listening to the message of truth, the gospel of your salvation—having believed
      (pisteuo), you also were sealed (sphragizo) in Him with the Holy Spirit of promise, who is given as a
      guarantee (arrabon) of our inheritance, with a view to the redemption of God’s own possession, to
      the praise of His glory. (Eph 1:13-14; TM)
                                                   Tense of Believe
       According to conditionalists,2 one must continue to believe in order to continue to be classified as a believer.
If one ceases to believe, then one ceases to be sealed. Therefore, conditionalists frequently will appeal to the present
tense of believe and assert that one must presently believe in order to remain presently sealed. A linear aspect (£)
thus is perceived as being necessary for the present tense in such contexts by conditionalists. From their perspective,
a person must continue to believe in order to continue to be a believer and thus continue to be sealed.3 The security
conveyed by being sealed is conditioned on continuing to believe. Some conditionalists would add that the person
must also behave.
       Securitists4 take an opposing point of view: Security is not conditioned on continuing to believe.
Consequently, once a person believes in Christ for eternal life as a free gift, that person is unconditionally secure in
Christ. At the punctiliar point in time () a person believes, that person is granted unconditional security in terms
of reaching Heaven. This security is no longer contingent on the person believing and never was contingent on the
person behaving.
NOSAS Perspective of Seal
       Securitists consider Eph 1:13 to be a very strong affirmation of unconditional security, more popularly known
as eternal security or OSAS.5 Once a person has been sealed, that person cannot become unsealed even if that
individual fails to persevere in the faith.
       Conditionalists, in contrast, will agree that the promise applies to regenerate believers but insist that if a
person stops believing, then the promise no longer applies to such a person, and his or her regeneration,
consequently, is lost. Indubitably, the promise is not made to unbelievers. So Ashby’s exegetical critique is that the

with my section on trichotomous classification. See “Categorical Retention of Eternal Life” in Believe: An
Aspectual and Metaphorical Analysis from the Gospel of John. After all, not even all Marrowists are trichotomists.
Therefore, the present discussion will focus primarily on the supporting text found in Eph 1:13-14 to solidify that
position with an expanded treatment.
2
  Conditionalist refers to an advocate of conditional security—the conviction that once a person believes in Christ
for eternal life, that person is conditionally secure in Christ and must persevere in order to reach heaven.
3

John.
4
  Securitist refers to an advocate of unconditional security—the conviction that once a person believes in Christ for
eternal life, that person is unconditionally secure in Christ and is assured of unconditionally reaching heaven.
5

not the same thing as unconditional security in that many proveitists believe in OSAS and yet insist that one must
persevere in order to reach heaven. Thus, reaching heaven is conditioned by the proveitist camp on perseverance.
This proveitist hybrid is not a valid form of OSAS. The only form of OSAS considered legitimate herein is the
unconditional affirmation of OSAS even if one does not persevere.
Page 2                                                                                    Dual Sealing and Security
purpose of the passage is not to affirm the existence of saved unbelievers. From the NOSAS7 perspective, the
                                                                               6

passage knows nothing of sealed unbelievers. Therefore, Arminians such as Ashby will appeal to the fact that we
are sealed while we are believers, but then such conditionalists will stipulate that we become unsealed if we stop
believing. The present book will interact with this Arminian perspective.8
                                        Illustration 1. When Doubt Speaks




Verbal Aspect
      As will be shown below, since the pledge is a down payment, this NOSAS assumption by conditionalists is
an unnatural inference. To the person who has believed, the down payment has been given as a guarantee that this
person will receive the inheritance. Therefore, if the person who has believed does not get the inheritance, the down
payment would still be his or hers even if that individual no longer is a functional believer.



6

ed. Matthew J. Pinson (Grand Rapids: Zondervan, 2002), 166-167.
7

not the same thing as unconditional security in that many proveitists believe in OSAS yet insist that one must

the present book.

  Even so, it should be noted that Calvinists believe practically the same thing: If you were to cease to believe, you
would lose your seal. Calvinists, however, simultaneously conclude that if you cease to believe, this proves that you
never were sealed. This tension in the Calvinistic argument weakens their argument. Thus, the present book will
focus more so on the Arminian version of conditionalism.
Chapter 1. Guaranteed Security                                                                                 Page 3
                                           Illustration 2. Children of God




         As discussed more fully in The Outer Darkness, being born of God is a static category ontologically. This
status as a child of God does not change or fluctuate. Functionally, however, not all those who are born of God are
children of God. They are dysfunctional children. Nevertheless, they remain children, and in that sense they still
may be classified as believers, even if they cease to believe. Just as the members of a dysfunctional family actually
are still part of that family, so dysfunctional family members of God’s family are still part of His family.
        The promise of being sealed is not made to the person who continues to believe but to the one who has
believed. Therefore, the promise conveys unconditional protection to the one who has believed regardless of
whether that person continues to believe. Accordingly, the focus of the immediate response herein will be on the
tense of the verb (pisteuo) itself in Eph 1:13, translated above as having believed. This tense is antithetical to
Ashby’s exegetical critique. The verb in question is an aorist participle, not a present participle. As such, it refers
to the person who has believed.
        The Arminian sense of the tense is nonsense. Had Paul meant to say that we are sealed as long as we believe
(as in classical Arminianism), he easily could have done so with chronos. Paul certainly uses this word elsewhere
when he wishes to express temporal duration:

•   The law has jurisdiction over a person as long as he lives (Rom 7:1).
•   A wife is bound as long as her husband lives (1Cor 7:39).
•   As long as the heir is a child, he does not differ at all from a slave (Gal 4:1).

Or if Paul had meant that we are sealed as often as we believe (as in contemporary Arminianism), he certainly could
have said so with hosakis:

•   As often as you drink it in remembrance of Me (1Cor 11:25).
•   As often as you eat this bread and drink the cup, you proclaim the Lord’s death (1Cor 11:26).
Page 4                                                                                      Dual Sealing and Security
       As it is, the Arminian argument in Eph 1:13 puts words in Paul’s mouth that are not there. Paul is not saying
people are sealed as long as they believe or as often as they believe. Instead, he clearly is affirming that the person
is sealed who has believed. Paul simply uses an aorist participle to show that the condition of faith which he has in
mind is a punctiliar response which is contingently antecedent to being sealed. Such aoristic participles normally
are translated as some form of having, after, or when. For example, akouo (the first verb in the verse) is also an
aorist participle and is typically translated as after listening (NAS) or after you heard (KJV, NKJ, DRA, ERV, NJB,
RWB, TNT, WEB). But it is also just as easily translated as having heard (ASV, DBY, RSV, YLT) or when you
heard (CSB, NET, NIB, NIV, NRS). Likewise, although the second verb (pisteuo) most frequently is translated as
having believed, it could be translated just as well as: after you believed (KJV, RWB, WEB) or when you believed
(CSB, NET, NLT).
       So let us not think that the translation of having believed is a perfect tense denoting a faith in the past that
continues to sustain a state of faith into the present so that sealing is contingent on this lingering affect. Paul does
not use the perfect tense on this occasion. He is not referring to a state of faith which necessarily continues into the
present (▬ ). Likewise, Lenski’s comment that hearing and believing are ingressive aorists (̈) makes no sense.
Paul is not referring to an act of faith which necessarily continues into the present. Even Lenski has to admit: “The
moment [] we come to faith the sealing occurs.”9 Our sealing is not conditioned on our continuous believing
anymore than it is contingent upon our continuous hearing. Paul is not saying that as long as or as often as we hear
the gospel then we believe and are sealed. Continuous hearing is not his point at all. He is simply using a series of
three aorist participles to give a sequential chain of punctiliar events:
                                         you heard → you believed → you were sealed

        Lenski acknowledges, “The aorist ‘were sealed’ fits one act of sealing.”10 Paul, as even Lenski has to admit,
is not saying that protracted hearing or believing results in sealing. Since the sealing occurs instantaneously the very
moment we first believe, the sealing must be conditioned on punctiliar faith (), not on linear believing (£). In
other words, Paul does not say, “He who hears continuously and believes continuously is sealed continuously.”
        Paul uses this aoristic chain to show his ordo salutis: hearing precedes believing, and believing precedes
sealing.11 Paul, therefore, is assuring: “When you heard and subsequently believed, you were consequently sealed.”
The sealing is the consequence of a () punctiliar response of faith. Although these events are temporally
simultaneous, they are logically sequential.
Sealing is Not Filling
        Barth endeavors to lend some scholastic muscle to the conditional notion by claiming that sealing is “as little
completed, as much dependent upon continuous renewal, as that of ‘filling’ with the Spirit.” In other words, sealing
is just as linearly contingent as filling. Supposedly, believers are just as liable to continuous sealing as they are to
continuous filling. Yet Barth himself admits that his interpretation clashes with the tense:

             The Greek tense used in Eph 1:13 is an aorist ‘you have been sealed.’ This tense, above all in the
      indicative form, denotes—except in the rare cases when it occurs in proverbial utterances (gnomic
      aorist)—a unique, complete, past action. The aorist seems to preclude the interpretation offered above.12

How can Barth make what appears to be such a contradictory assessment? By appealing to aorists used earlier:

      But as little as the aorists “he has blessed us,” “he has chosen us” in 1:3-4 will be used to prove that
      God has ceased to bless and to choose after a given moment, ought the sealing to be considered
      restricted to one instant only in the man’s life.13

9
  R. C. H. Lenski, The Interpretation of St. Paul’s Epistles to the Galatians, to the Ephesians, and to the Philippians
(Minneapolis: Augsburg Publishing House, 1961), 382.
10
   Ibid., 383.
11
   Incidentally, the Calvinistic notion that faith is a gift that comes after regeneration in Eph 2:8-9 is proven false
by the Pauline sequence of events in Eph 1:13.

Bible (New York: Doubleday & Company, 1974), 141, n. 372.

       Evidently, Barth is trying to set the stage for his later assessment in which he takes Eph 4:30 as a threat of
believers grieving the Holy Spirit and, in so doing, finding their seal to become a seal unto eternal death. Yet even
Lenski, also an insecuritist, acknowledges the punctiliar nature of the sealing.
       If one carefully notes the interchange of ideas in the movement of Barth’s argument, it is not difficult to see
where unconditional security was removed in his shell game. As one looks under Barth’s conclusion, the con
becomes immediately apparent in that punctiliarists do not claim that the aoristic sealing proves that those once
sealed have ceased to be sealed. To the contrary, those who were sealed (at that previous point [] in time) remain
sealed. This is the whole point from the punctiliaristic perspective. Thus, Barth has attempted to attribute a false
cessation to the punctiliar position by confusing completion with termination. God does not have to continue to
perform the act of choosing us in order for us to be chosen, nor does God have to continue to perform the act of
sealing us in order for us to be sealed.
       The text itself that Barth cites clearly states: “He chose () us in Him before the foundation of the world”
(Eph 1:4). The action is completed, and we remain chosen. The thought certainly is not that we were chosen in the
past but no longer are chosen in the present. Nor is there any suggestion in that text that we can be unchosen. Barth
is being oxymoronic to suggest that God must continue to perform His act of choosing us in the present in order for
us to remain chosen in the present when the text asserts that the action was performed before the foundation of the
world and that we remain chosen in the present!
       Even school children on the playground realize that when they were chosen () at the beginning of recess to
be on a team that they remain on that team throughout the course of play. The team captain does not have to keep
choosing them every minute for them to be chosen to be part of that team. They remain in a state of being chosen.
The captain is not actively choosing them at every moment in order for them to remain on the team.
       Similarly, believers remain in a chosen state as a result of God’s past choice. Believers were chosen () to
be in Christ before the world began. They then were sealed () in Christ at the temporal point in time when they
came to faith in Christ. These irrevocable blessings are not (£) linearly contingent. Our state of being sealed,
chosen, and blessed continues even if our faith does not. In such texts, believers are not called upon to be concerned
about being unchosen, unsealed, or unblessed.
Linear Classification
       Objections are easy to anticipate: How can our aoristic sealing have a lingering effect if our aoristic faith
does not have a lingering effect? How can I stress the resulting state of the benefit when I have rejected the resulting
state of the contingency? How can securitists claim that the aoristic sealing results in an ongoing state when they
deny that the aoristic faith upon which it was conditioned results in a lingering state?
       Such objections misunderstand the comments above about the aoristic nature of sealing faith. The faith which
results in one being sealed does not linger from the standpoint of contingency, but such faith necessarily must be
linear in terms of the resultant seal. That punctiliar () act of faith lingers on in the resultant state (▬) of being
sealed. In terms of lingering result, one remains a believer even if one ceases to believe.14 One may still be labeled
a believer in terms of the result that came about from that completed action. Therefore, the Arminian objection that
there is no such thing as sealed unbelievers begs the question as to whether the term believer is being used in terms
of result or requirement.15 The aoristic sealing used to describe the believer as sealed refers to the result rather than
the requirement.
       With that clarification in mind, securitists themselves may well respond to conditionalists by remarking, “Of
course there is no such thing as a sealed unbeliever.” God does not seal unbelievers. The Lord does not seal a person
until that person becomes a believer. At that point in time, the Lord then marks that person as a believer with His
seal. From that point forward, that person is a marked man. From the perspective of that mark, the person is
henceforth a believer in terms of that mark. In terms of result, the person remains a believer. The classification of a
person as a believer is linear, even if the action of believing is not.
       In the OT we find a similar picture: “And the Lord set a mark upon Cain, lest any finding him should kill
him” (Gen 4:15; KJV). In an utterly remarkable expression of grace, the Lord marked Cain for protection from
those who would be inclined to slay him. By no means did Cain deserve that mark of protection—he received it
despite the fact that he had just killed his brother! Clearly, the Lord only had to mark him graciously one time for

14
     See “Substantival Categorization” in Believe: An Aspectual and Metaphorical Analysis from the Gospel of John.
15
     See “Popular Classification” in Believe: An Aspectual and Metaphorical Analysis from the Gospel of John.
Page 6                                                                                       Dual Sealing and Security
him to remain marked.
       Some punctiliar actions result in permanent perspectives; some do not. One act of tithing does not make one
a tither. One act of dieting does not make one a dieter. On the other hand, some actions clearly result in a linear
classification. By one act of sin, Adam had become a sinner. By one act of sin, his son, Cain, had become a murderer.
The determining factor is the perspective of the one doing the labeling. Cain was still regarded as a murderer even
though he was no longer committing murder.16 The Lord expresses His own perspective on the matter quite vividly:
“The voice of your brother’s blood is crying to Me from the ground” (Gen 4:10). That shed blood was still crying
from the ground, “Murderer! Murderer!” even after the murder was over and done. Similarly, the shed blood of our
Lord is still crying from the cross, “Believer! Believer!” even long after a sealed believer may cease to believe.
       Paul has just stated in this context: “In Him we have redemption through His blood” (Eph 1:7). Next, he
states that we are sealed in Him with a view to redemption (Eph 1:13-14). Paul then picks up these themes of
redemption and sealing once more in his epistle and combines them: “You were sealed for the day of redemption”
(Eph 4:30). The text does not say, “Your faith was sealed.” Rather, it says, “You were sealed.” The person himself
or herself is sealed. The person is protected and redeemed by the blood and the seal, not by the person’s faith. The
person is protected by being regarded as a blood-bought and Spirit-sealed believer even if he or she ceases to believe.
                                                          Seal
Protection
       Corner rejects the securitist position, as expressed by Dillow, that the seal denotes ownership or protection.
But Corner’s counter is very poorly chosen as a repudiation of protection. Corner claims: “That seal [in Mt 27:66]
didn’t protect the tomb from being opened by God’s angel when Jesus rose from the dead!” (Emphasis his.)17 Of
course, it did not. But the question is, “Was the seal intended to protect the tomb from being tampered with by the
disciples?” Certainly! Those who requested the seal on the tomb wanted to make sure that Jesus’ tomb was not
tampered with; therefore, the nature of their request leaves no doubt as to the intended nature of the seal: “Therefore,
give orders for the grave to be made secure until the third day, lest the disciples come and steal Him away and say
to the people, ‘He has risen from the dead,’ and the last deception will be worse than the first” (Mt 27:64)! The
response to their request for security nullifies any legitimate room for doubt as to the designated nature of the seal:
“Pilate said to them, ‘You have a guard; go, make it as secure as you know how. And they went and made the grave
secure, and along with the guard they set a seal on the stone’” (Mt 27:65-66). According to the Bible, the seal was
placed there for security—to make the tomb “secure…secure…secure”!
       How did Corner miss this triple affirmation of security concerning the intended nature of the seal in the very
text he chose for a prooftext? From the joint vantage points of those requesting and giving the seal, the seal was
placed on the tomb for security. The old adage that a prooftext is a text taken out of context certainly seems to be
true of Corner’s usage. Perhaps this is why Corner actually chose not to quote this text. Without a doubt, it is much
easier for Corner to tell his readers that the text does not denote protection when he does not quote the three
affirmations of security embedded within the text.
       From the very text that Corner chose to cite (without quoting), to illustrate the alleged lack of inherent
protection in sealing, that of the sealed tomb in Matthew, one should gather that the purpose of the seal was for
security—if one would just read the text! If the sealed tomb is a picture of the intended nature of what it means to
be sealed in Eph 1:13, as Corner supposes, then the seal is a beautiful picture of eternal security. The purpose of the
seal was to make the tomb as secure as possible by the one placing the seal. The seal was even guarded to make
sure it was protected from tampering. If this is what our sealing means, then God has made us as secure as He
possibly can in Christ by setting His army as a guard over us so that no one can get into Christ to tamper with us.
What is more, God has placed a stone over us that we cannot possibly move to get out of Christ on our own. We
are tamper resistant to the max! As a securitist, I love the text that Corner has chosen to illustrate the intended nature
of the seal. It destroys his argument.
       In Resurrection Factor, McDowell cites Robertson as saying that the seal “could be placed on the stone only
in the presence of the Roman guards who were left in charge….The purpose of this procedure was to prevent
anyone from tampering with the grave’s contents.”18 Contrary to Corner, the purpose of the seal was not merely “to

16
   See “Philosophical Classification” in Believe: An Aspectual and Metaphorical Analysis from the Gospel of John.
17
   Daniel D. Corner, The Believer’s Conditional Security: Eternal Security Refuted (Washington, PA: Evangelical
Outreach, 2000), 446.
18
   Josh McDowell, The Resurrection Factor (San Bernardino: Here’s Life Publishers, 1981), 59.
Chapter 1. Guaranteed Security                                                                                Page 7
                                                        19
show that it wasn’t tampered with by Jesus’ disciples.” Much more, it was intended to prevent the disciples from
tampering with the tomb. The seal was intended to make the tomb tamper proof, to protect it from tampering, to
make it as secure as humanly possible. In Evidence that Demands a Verdict, McDowell quotes Robertson verbatim,
“The sealing was done in the presence of the Roman guards who were left in charge to protect this stamp of Roman
authority and power.”20 Corner’s inability to detect the protecting purpose of the Matthean seal throws his ability
to see the purpose of the Pauline seal into question. Corner’s illustration gives his own argument a coronary.
Identification
       Rather than dwell on Corner’s blunder in failing to note that the textual evidence clearly shows that the
purpose of the Matthean seal was for protection, let us give credibility to what Corner rather awkwardly was
attempting to do, which is to show that the word seal has a semantic range. Some of those questioning Eph 1:13
from an Arminian perspective have noticed that the Greek dictionaries regard seal as referring to identification
rather than security in Eph 1:13. So they have questioned the legitimacy of reading the idea of protection into this
passage. This question, at least superficially, has more credibility than Corner’s crude objection.
       After all, BDAG defines the Matthean usage of seal in Mt 27:66 as to provide with a seal as a security
measure, which is in harmony with my analysis above concerning the tomb. This Arminian argument refutes
Corner’s Arminian appeal to Mt 27:66 by acknowledging that the sealing of the tomb does indeed show security
(which is obvious when one reads the text). But then this Arminian argument maintains that this security does not
apply to Eph 1:13 because lexicons put the sealing of these two passages into two different categories. Sure enough,
the definition BDAG provides for the Pauline seal in Eph 1:13 is to mark with a seal as a means of identification.
       For sake of argument, let us content ourselves for the moment with these distinctions in definition for
sphragizo (seal) as making the Arminian lexical argument plausible that the sealed tomb pictures security but the
sealed believer only depicts identity and move our attention to arrabon (pledge, down payment, guarantee) for
consideration as to the significance of this identification. In doing so, it will become apparent that a thoroughgoing
distinction between protection and identification is not the lexical intention.
                                                      Deposit
Pledge
       Arminians, of course, have assumed that believers can lose the pledge. After all, by the sheer nature of the
case, pledges can be returned. As a case in point, the singular LXX example (that of Gen 38:17-20) dealing with
pledges shows that the pledged items in question were given with the intent of their being returned. The Arminian
bubble bursts quickly, however, when one examines the differences between the texts. As Woodcock notes, this
acknowledgment that a pledge could be returned in no way impinges upon the eternal security expressed by the
texts under consideration:

          Like the earnest, the pledge functions as a guarantee of the completion of a business transaction.
      Unlike the earnest, however, a pledge consisted of an article or articles unrelated to what was being
      purchased and the payments for it. The pledge was to be returned when all payments had been made.21

      Unlike a pledge, the Spirit is related intrinsically to the believer’s redemption. Moreover, the transaction
already has been completed. The transition rather than the completion of the transaction is what we await on the
day of redemption.22 Even if the imagery were pressed to mean that the transaction will not be concluded until the
day of redemption, the evident fact still remains that God did not give us the Spirit with the intent of our returning
the Spirit to Him at the completion of that transaction.23 Yet, for sake of argument, even if it were assumed that God

19
   Corner, 446.
20
   Josh McDowell, Evidence that Demands a Verdict: Historical Evidence for the Christian Faith, revised edition
(San Bernardino: Here’s Life Publishers, 1979), 222.
21
   Eldon Woodcock, “The Seal of the Holy Spirit,” BibSac 155:618 (April-June 1998): 151.
22
   See Sealed or Saved, 18.
23
   A pledge was a unilateral guarantee by one party to fulfill an obligation. The pledge was not part of obligation
but was returned at the completion of the obligation. An earnest (i.e., down payment), on the other hand, was a
bilateral guarantee in which both parties had to fulfill their respective parts of the obligation to complete the
transaction. In the case of a seller and buyer, the seller makes the down payment, and the buyer gets to keep the
Page 8                                                                                       Dual Sealing and Security
did give us the Spirit with the intent of the Spirit leaving us after the completion of the transaction, the return of the
pledge would not be demanded before the completion of our redemption at the future day of redemption (Eph 4:30).
By the time that future day of redemption arrives, many of those believers who have died in the Lord already will
have been in heaven for a considerable period of time because of the pledge. Will God throw believers out of Heaven
into Hell after those believers have been in Heaven for thousands of years? Of course not! Not even moderate
misthologists are daring enough to think that God will cast believers out of Heaven into Hell for their failures that
took place on Earth thousands of years before. Is there any Arminian who would dare suggest such an unthinkable
thing?24 If not, then it must be concluded eschatologically that the giving of the pledge to the believer results
soteriologically in unconditional security.
       Furthermore, as Woodcock notes, even in the case of these LXX pledges, “These items were her security
that she would be paid. When he delivered the goat, the objects were to be returned to him.”25 Even the purpose of
the returnable pledge was security, not probation. Additionally, even though the pledge might be returned
theoretically, it could not be taken morally. That is, Judah (the giver of the pledge) did not have the right to take the
pledge from the woman (the recipient of the pledge) until he paid her.26 Morally, God does not have the right to
take the pledge from us either—certainly not until He completes our redemption since He has given us the pledge,
at least until the day of redemption. Once the Lord completes our redemption by giving us glorified bodies, we can
rest assured that God will not throw us into hell afterward. Would an Arminian dare suggest otherwise?
Misthologists have enough trouble convincing FG soteriologists that carnal believers can be cast into the outer
darkness after having been in Heaven for thousands of years while they await the bema. Those in the Arminian
camp who might argue that God will cast believers into Hell after being allowed in Heaven for thousands of years
certainly would compose a small circle indeed. Surely, no reasonable person will disagree. Even if (strictly
hypothetically speaking) all believers are required to return the Holy Spirit to God after receiving their redeemed
bodies (Rom 8:23), none of them will be thrown soteriologically into Hell after having lived in Heaven.
       Finally, theoretical speculation aside, it is impossible for believers to return this pledge. A signet-ring (used
to make seals) was one of the items the woman received as a pledge. Believers, in contrast, do not receive a ring as
their pledge. Our seal is not placed on our fingers but in our hearts by God because only He can perform this heart
operation (2Cor 1:22). If the pledge had been placed in the woman’s heart, she would not have been able to return
the pledge even if she had so desired. As it is, she kept the pledge. As believers, we likewise have no choice but to

down payment (as part of the full payment) once the goods being sold have been delivered. Some translations prefer
to render arrabon as pledge; some as earnest; some prefer to enjoin the two by translating it as bilateral-unilateral
guarantee.
       Obviously, God does not sell us the Holy Spirit. The Holy Spirit is a gift; therefore, the preference on the part
of some translations for pledge is understandable. Moreover, if we have been given the Holy Spirit because we have
believed (), then an ongoing bilateral contingency is ruled out. The unilateral fulfillment of the obligation rests
solely upon God. However, because we get to keep the Holy Spirit, the preference herein will be for the earnest
motif with the clarification that keeping the Holy Spirit is conditioned on punctiliar faith. God has obligated Himself
to give full soteric redemption as a gift to those who believe in Christ for eternal life and has given them the gift of
the Holy Spirit as a down payment on His fulfilling that transaction. Because we have fulfilled our bilateral part of
the transaction by believing in Christ at the moment () of saving faith, the fulfilling of the soteric obligation is
now unilateral and rests solely upon God. Hence, it is a bilateral-unilateral guarantee.

secure once they reach heaven. Nevertheless, I do not wish to attribute Duty’s detestable opinion to Corner without
documentation that Corner does indeed deny OSAS in heaven. As the rule of thumb, the Arminian affirmation of
once in Heaven always in Heaven is herein assumed. Even so, the potential exception represented by Duty has been
noted and refuted. See “Substantival Categorization” in Believe: An Aspectual and Metaphorical Analysis from the

   The soteriological argument from SAS is being left largely unchanged in DSAS in the first two chapters of the

the possibility of losing the misthological down payment, but not allow the possibility of losing the soteriological
pledge. As to the pledge given by Judah to Tamar (Gen 38:16-20), the pledge was not a gift, but part of a business
transaction. Therefore, this OT commercial usage of the pledge does not have applicability to the NT soteriological
nature of the seal given as a pledge.
Chapter 1. Guaranteed Security                                                                                  Page 9
keep the pledge because it is our hearts which are sealed with the pledge. Heart transplant surgery was not an option
for the woman. Only God has the power to perform heart transplant surgery at the ontological level of regeneration
and has provided us with a new heart (Eze 36:26). The best we can do is imitate this action at the phenomenal level
(Eze 18:31). But just as we cannot birth ourselves at the ontological level, so we cannot return this seal or replace
it with an old heart at the ontological level.27 Only God has the theoretical power to reverse this heart transplant,
yet even He does not have the right to do so.28 At the very least, God has given us the pledge to keep until He
completes our redemption. Therefore, even if the arrabon were translated as pledge in all three NT passages
(2Cor 1:22; 2Cor 5:5; Eph 1:14), it would still result in unconditional security. Consequently, since the nature of
such an internal pledge is that it cannot be returned or taken and thus results in unconditional security, pledge is not
the best translation for arrabon in the NT. Better translations include earnest, down payment, guarantee. In
summary, arrabon in these passages is an earnest given in the form of a down payment as a guarantee.
Guarantee
        Whether the believing is impermanent, the sealing is permanent. How do we know this? Well, in addition to
the sense of the tense and the absence of words such as chronos and hosakis (in Eph 1:13) to imply linear faith, the
presence of arrabon (down payment, guarantee) in the next verse (Eph 1:14) provides us with a commercial usage
that is easy to understand. If you make a down payment on your house to the mortgage company to guarantee that
you will finish paying off the house but fail to finish paying off the house, you lose the down payment, and the
mortgage company gets to keep it. The giver of the down payment loses the down payment to the recipient of the
transaction if the giver of the down payment fails to complete the transaction. In this case, God loses the Holy Spirit
to us if He does not make good on His promise to complete our redemption on the specified day. God is the one
making the promise in this context; we are not the ones making the promise.
        To argue that we can lose our salvation is tantamount to claiming that we are the ones making the down
payment rather than God. It is as if God sold us His house (i.e. Heaven) and is requiring that we make the down
payment in frontend LS (Lordship Salvation) and monthly payments in backend LS. The Calminian will respond
that we do so with God’s aid. This is like saying that we make co-payments. Such a transaction does not picture a
gift, but a contract. This picture is false because God is the one making the payment, not us.
        Woodcock rightly rejects the contract for services understanding of arrabon in the NT texts. A contract for
services fits the LXX example in which the woman received the arrabon as a contract for her services as a harlot.
Most assuredly, however, Jesus is not giving us the Holy Spirit as a contract for our services. The Spirit is given to
us as a gift, not as a result of our service. As Woodcock goes on to explain, regardless as to whether arrabon is
translated as pledge or down payment, there are shortcomings in each term.

           In describing the indwelling Holy Spirit as the believer’s ἀρραβών Paul did not intend to convey
      all nuances of that term. For in this context the ἀρραβών was not merely a partial payment as part of
      a contract obligating the debtor to pay all he owed. Nor was it a pledge that would have to be returned.
           Rather, the believer’s ἀρραβών is an unsolicited and gracious gift by the perfectly holy and
      completely faithful God, who always fulfills His promises. Since His integrity and faithfulness are
      beyond question, this ἀρραβών was of course unnecessary as a device to discourage God from
      changing His mind or not taking His obligations seriously. God graciously led Paul to use imagery that
      would assure believers of the reality of His guarantee.29

       Well said. The purpose of the arrabon is to give us assurance, not to force God to follow through on His
promise. Nevertheless, the nature of this promise is clarified by thoughtful consideration of the contextual imagery
so as to make down payment a vastly superior translation over pledge. The promise of a completed redemption
should be understood as unconditional to those who have received the down payment. This unconditional security
logically results in absolute assurance.
Non-returnable
      Still, the Arminian immediately will respond that the believer might return the down payment. According to

27
   See Metaphysical Union, 25.
28
   Might ≠ Right. For a more technical distinction between power and ability, see Power versus Ability, 17.
29
   Ibid., 153.
Page 10                                                                                        Dual Sealing and Security
the Bible, however, God’s gifts are not so easily returned. In fact, they are non-returnable. God will not repent of
the gifts He has given us (Rom 11:29). He will neither take them away from us nor allow us to return them. We
cannot return our justification to God because God would have to unjustify us. God is our justifier (Rom 3:26;
11:33), not our unjustifier; hence, we cannot become unjustified. Nor can we unsave ourselves because to do so we
would have to get God to unsave us and thereby cause Him to go back on His promise to give us these gifts freely
and irrevocably. To return the down payment to the owner, the owner would have to receive it. To receive it, He
must take it. To take it, He must revoke on His promise that the reception and retention of the down payment were
conditioned only on our initial acceptance. The down payment is ours to keep, not ours to return.
       It is incumbent upon God to make the payments.30 If He does not, He loses His down payment—His security
deposit. The purpose of the verse is not to entertain the hypothetical possibility that we can lose the deposit, which
was given to us as a gift, but to describe what God hypothetically would lose if He did not follow through on the
completion of what He has started. His deposit made to us, not ours to Him, is at stake. His veracity is at stake, not
ours. The Lord is the one who stands to lose the deposit, not us. In making the assertion that we can return the down
payment, the Arminian is standing the verse on its head by suggesting that we can lose the deposit. This is not the
point of the verse. The intention of the verse is to impart a sense of security, not insecurity. The only hypothetical
loss that the text entertains is the possibility that God might lose the deposit. The point of the verse is that the deposit
is ours to keep even if God does not fulfill the promise. If God fails to deliver, for whatever reason, even if it is
because of some moral failure on our part (such as not wanting the deposit anymore), God is not at liberty to take
the deposit from us. It is ours to keep.
                                                      Seal/Deposit
        There can be no room for reasonable doubt as to the fact that the Holy Spirit is the down payment (i.e.,
earnest) in the NT passages. In addition to describing the Spirit as our down payment in Eph 1:14, Paul also repeats
this illustration in 2Cor 5:5, where he uses arrabon to say, “Now He who has prepared us for this very thing [i.e.,
for having an immortal body] is God, who also has given us the Spirit as a guarantee” (NKJ). Is the guarantee that
we will always want what God has prepared for us? No. The guarantee is that we will get what God has prepared
for us. His fidelity, not ours, is guaranteed. Therefore, it is He “who guarantees our inheritance until we come into
possession” (Eph 1:14; CJB). Even in the case of this translation, which might be taken as suggesting that it is our
possession which awaits realization, the guarantee is that God will do what is necessary for us to come into
possession of this inheritance.31 If we fail to inherit it, then it is because God has failed. He is the one who made
the guarantee, not us. He is the guarantor; we are the guaranteed. God has given us the Holy Spirit as His security
deposit to guarantee our security. The Lord “put His seal on us and gave us his Spirit in our hearts as a guarantee
for the future” (2Cor 1:22; TM).
        L-N defines arrabon as “the first or initial payment of money or assets, as a guarantee for the completion of
a transaction.” FB and the UBS also define it as a guarantee. Strong’s (SEC) definition is still appreciable: “Earnest
(a part payment in advance for security).” The seal is a down payment that guarantees our security. Paul conjoins
the seal with the down payment in both 1Cor 1:22 and Eph 1:13-14. Consequently, in Paul’s mind, the seal must
convey a guarantee of security. Now, to be sure, unlike the word arrabon, the word seal does not mean down
payment, but there must be an overlapping somewhere of the semantic domain between seal and down payment for
the parallelism to make sense. Could it be that both words denote protection in the way Paul is using them? Perhaps
we should reconsider whether the Arminian restriction of the Pauline usage of seal to mean identification has
overlooked a link that Paul believes exists with protection.
                                             Identification is Protection
       As noted, BDAG does not define the Matthean usage of seal in Mt 27:66 as falling in the same section as the
Pauline usage in Eph 1:14. The former is defined in the first section of BDAG’s definition as meaning to provide
with a seal as a security measure, but the latter is defined in the third section as to mark with a seal as a means of
identification. So according to the standard biblical Greek dictionary of our day, the sealing of the tomb denotes
security, but the sealing of the believer denotes identification. Thus, I have been willing to entertain the Arminian
lexical argument that the Pauline usage might be limited to identification and not convey a sense of protection.

30
   I am speaking of multiple payments only by way of illustration. Actually, it was a singular, completed payment
in which the transaction has been completed. What we await is not the completion of the payments or transaction
but the realization of the transition in the day of redemption. See Possession, 14.

   God’s inheritance/possession of us awaits realization. See Transaction versus Realization, 17.

       Now, it is time for the rest of the story. BDAG goes on to say that the purpose of the seal in this latter section
pertaining to the believer is to mark with a seal as a means of identification…so that the mark denoting ownership
also carries with it the protection of the owner (bold mine). BDAG gives the sealing of believers in Rev 7:3 as the
reference. Yet no reason exists to suppose that John’s imagery of believers being sealed on their foreheads is
significantly different than Paul’s statement that believers are sealed in their hearts (2Cor 1:22). Therefore, in
contrast to Corner, BDAG regards the third category (in which it places Eph 1:13) as denoting both ownership and
protection. Dillow was right all along in regarding this seal as a picture of OSAS.
       Now, as to Corner, who likes to pit one proponent of OSAS against another, he should realize that this
procedure is a two-edged sword. OSAS can do the same with NOSAS. Corner says that the seal in Eph 1:13 does
not denote ownership; Lenski says that it does. Which NOSAS proponent is correct? Lenski obviously. The seal
denotes the protection by the owner. As for those Arminians who point to the Greek lexicons and claim that
Eph 1:14 is listed under identification rather than security, they are making a false dichotomy. No such sharp
distinction between sealed for identification versus security is intended lexically. The identification shows
ownership. We belong to God. We are under the protection of our owner. We are under God’s protection. This
identification results in protection. This Pauline identification results in a guarantee of security.
       Thayer (TY) also puts Eph 1:13 in the third section of his definition. Like BDAG, Thayer starts off this
section by pointing out that seal is used in Rev 7:3 to stamp his servants on their foreheads as destined for eternal
salvation, and by this means to confirm their hopes. Naturally, Thayer places Eph 1:13 in this same section as
respecting God, who by the gift of the Holy Spirit indicates who are his. Ownership and the completion of our
salvation and the confirmation of our expectations of living with Christ in heaven are being affirmed. Woodcock
concludes, “What He owns, He also protects.”32 “Since believers are God’s inviolable property, He will protect
them. As a result, believers can enjoy complete security in their relationship with the Lord.”33 This relational
security is guaranteed by the seal. Constable summarizes: “God sealed them in Christ. This provided a guarantee
of their eternal security.”34
                                                    Spherical Security
       This additional confirmation of security within this passage is worthy of exploration. Paul affirms that we
are sealed in Christ. Translations vary, but even those translations which place the second in Him (en ho) as the
object of believe (so that it is translated as believe in Him) are almost uniform in acknowledging that we are sealed
in Him by virtue of the first en ho. For example, the ESV reads: “In him (en ho) you also, when you heard the word
of truth, the gospel of your salvation, and believed in him (en ho), were sealed with the promised Holy Spirit”
(Eph 1:13). In other words, when you believed in Him, you were sealed in Him. The RSV likewise reads, “In him
you also, who have heard the word of truth, the gospel of your salvation, and have believed in him, were sealed with
the promised Holy Spirit.” You not only believe in Christ; you are sealed in Christ. Believers are not only sealed,
they are sealed in Christ. They are identified as belonging to God. That is security. We saw that lexically. Now,
we further see contextually that believers are identified as being in Christ. This double security is reminiscent of
Jn 10:28-29, in which believers are doubly secure because they are in Christ’s hand and in the Father’s hand.
       Yet Lenski claims, “The idea that Christ is the container into which we were placed, and that this container
is then sealed up, will scarcely find acceptance.”35 On the contrary, why should we not accept the container imagery?
Undoubtedly, we are not merely sealed in connection with Christ as Lenski claims. Christ is not sealed; we are.
Taken together, Eph 1:13-14 and 2Cor 1:22 certainly justify the children’s object lesson in which you take a picture
of a child, draw a picture of a dove on the back of it to represent the Spirit’s indwelling presence, place the picture
in an envelope labeled Christ, and then seal it. There is a double sealing, a sealing of the Holy Spirit in our hearts
and of us in Christ. Of course, in other texts Paul talks about our being baptized into Christ (Rom 6:3; Gal 3:27) by
the Spirit (1Cor 12:13) so that by God’s doing we are in Christ (1Cor 1:30), new creatures in Christ (2Cor 5:17),
created in Christ (Eph 2:10), and clothed with Christ (Gal 3:27). But for the purpose of my illustration, I will just
picture the double sealing.

       A variation of this object lesson will allow us to have fun with the Arminian insistence of limiting the seal to
identification and connection. Take a picture of the child, or a Xerox® copy of it, and smear superglue all over the
back and front of it. Then drop it into a clear Zip Lock® container labeled Christ, or better yet, Our Identification
In Christ. After all, it is a photo ID. Then superglue the container shut. The picture will not only be in the container,
but it will also be connected to the container. The picture will not only retain its own identity, but also that of the
container. You can then show a picture of your driver’s license (or a heated ID badge) to the children and explain
that the making of a driver’s license works something like this procedure. Your picture is permanently made part
of the container. You cannot remove the picture without doing violence to both the container and the glue keeping
it within the container. The glue represents the Holy Spirit, of course, and the plastic container represents Christ.
The only way to take the picture out of the container would be to cut it out, in which case the picture gets to keep
the glue and the plastic.36 If you cut the picture out to illustrate this point, you could burn the picture to show the
dark conclusion to which Arminianism leads: The only way for you to burn in hell is for the Holy Spirit and Christ
(the glue and plastic) to burn there with you.37 We have been sealed in the sphere of God’s love in Christ in such a
way as to make soteriological separation from Christ impossible.

fallacy.”38 Perhaps I should direct Corner to the latter pages in his book where he approvingly quotes Wesley: “The

love of God is everlasting life. It is, in substance, the life of heaven.”
       Wesley seemingly equates “the love of God” with “everlasting life” since he says one is the other. In Corner’s
defense, Wesley apparently is talking about our love for God rather than God’s love for us, since Corner extends
this quote from Wesley as saying, “Now, everyone believes, loves God, and therefore, hath everlasting life.”40
According to Wesley our love for God results in our soteriologically having eternal life. This is terrible soteriology.
Nevertheless, since Corner espouses this perspective, might we be so bold as to suggest that if Arminians are going
to equate our love for God with the life of God, then they are certainly being very shortsighted in criticizing their
opponents for equating God’s love for us with the life of God! If our love for God allows us to experience the life
of God, then certainly it is because God’s love for us provides us with the life of God!
       Securitists agree with Corner that God loves every lost person in the world (Jn 3:16). But does this mean that
God will love every lost person in hell? Do the lost not cease to be objects of God’s love once He casts them into
Hell?41 If so, then the logical outcome of Paul’s declaration is that no believer will be thrown into Hell. On the other
hand, for the sake of argument, suppose that God does love those in Hell so that even Hell will not separate them
from His love (cp. Ps 139:8). Does Corner intend to reduce Paul’s crescendo to a tautological whisper in which Paul
is merely pointing out that he is convinced that nothing will be able to separate the lost or saved from the love of
God? Of course not. To ask the question is to answer it. Paul makes a polarization in this context between being in
Christ versus being in the flesh (Rom 8:8-9).
       Obviously, a differentiation exists in this context between the love which God has for His children versus
those who are not His children. Paul makes this crystal clear in the verse itself. Paul is not describing God’s love
for those in hell or even for the lost people still alive in the world. No! It is “the love of God, which is in Christ
Jesus” (Rom 8:39). It is the love of God for those in Christ since it is the love of God which is located in Christ. It
is a love for those who are in the Spirit as opposed to being located in the flesh and thus do not belong to Christ
(Rom 8:9).

       In the above illustration, my younger son, Jonathan, is entrapped in a sphere of God’s love from which there
is no escape. The sphere represents Christ. Believers have been sealed permanently in Christ (Eph 1:13). Likewise,
according to Paul, when believers are baptized by the Spirit into Christ, they are clothed with Christ (Gal 3:27).
Illustration 3 depicts this truth as being sealed in a picture of Christ, but for present purposes, the sphere in
Illustration 4 will serve nicely for this dative of sphere as long as it is kept in mind that Christ is the soteriological
sphere in which we are located.42 Paul uses this phrase in Christ Jesus our Lord twice in Romans.
       On the first occasion that Paul uses this phrase, he assures us that the free gift of God is eternal life in Christ
Jesus our Lord (Rom 6:23). On the second occasion, Paul affirms that nothing can separate us from the love of God
which is in Christ our Lord (Rom 8:39). In both instances, Christ is the sphere. Both eternal life and this specialized
dimension of God’s love are located in Christ Jesus our Lord. The lost can no more have this love outside of Christ
than they can have eternal life outside of Christ. If a person is outside of this sphere, then that individual does not
have the love of God which is in Christ our Lord, and such a person does not have eternal life in Christ Jesus our
Lord. Conversely, if you are in Christ, then you have both the love of God and eternal life which are in Christ our
Lord.

     1. Nothing can separate the believer from the love of God in Christ.
     2. The love of God in Christ is limited to those in Christ.
     3. Therefore, nothing can separate the believer from being in Christ.

       Since nothing can separate the person who has been justified by faith from the love of God which is in Christ
(and since this love only exists in Christ), nothing can take the justified believer out of Christ. Death is separation.
Since the believer cannot be separated soteriologically from Christ, the believer cannot die soteriologically.
Moreover, since eternal life is in Christ and since the believer cannot be taken out of Christ, the believer cannot be
taken out of this soteriological sphere of life.
                                                       Possession
Ownership
       That which is sealed in Eph 1:13 is identified as God’s possession by virtue of the lexical meaning of the
word seal. The sealing denotes ownership in this context. One does not have to know Greek to deduce this fact. The
very next verse says that the pledge “is given as a guarantee of our inheritance, for the redemption of God’s own
possession” (TM). In denying that the seal denotes ownership in v. 13, Corner has ignored v. 14 which says that
we are God’s purchased possession (peripoiesis; NKJV). Does the fact that we are God’s purchased possession not
mean that He owns us? Of course, it does! Corner has ripped the seal out of its context in claiming that it does not
indicate God’s ownership in this context. How can he be so blind as to deny that the seal refers to ownership when
the very next verse explicitly says that we are God’s own possession (peripoiesis)?
       Is this a linearly contingent possession? In a far different context, there is no question that the preserving
(peripoiesis) of our soul requires persevering faith: “But we are not of those who shrink back to destruction, but of
those who have faith to the preservation of the soul” (Heb 10:39; TM). This preservation requires perseverance.
The salvation of the believer’s soul is a misthological concept—a reward based on performance in the form of
perseverance. We preserve our souls (for ourselves in terms of rewards) by persevering. The sealing, in contrast, is
a soteriological concept—and thus a free gift—regarding the redemption of our body. All believers will have
redeemed bodies(see AELT). Nevertheless, there is no reason that we could not follow the example of those who,
like Wuest, refer to the general idea of peripoiesis as preserving for one’s self in the Ephesian context. We are
God’s purchased possession which He preserves for Himself. Unlike Heb 10:29, here in the Ephesian passage God
is the preserver. Moreover, what is preserved belongs to Him, not to us. We are not preserving our purchase in this
context. Our identification as God’s possession guarantees God’s protection. If an Arminian cannot find security in
his identity in Christ in this passage, then he needs to have his exegetical eyes examined.



42
  The dative of sphere is also known as the locative of sphere. The former refers to its form; the latter to its case.
Those using the eight-case Greek classification will use the latter term while those using the five-form Greek system
will use the former term. Regardless, both terms are referring to the same syntactic relationship. For discussion of
how this sphere relates to the conditional sphere of God’s love in Jude 1:21, see The Outer Darkness.
Chapter 1. Guaranteed Security                                                                                Page 15
Misthological Possession
       I have demonstrated elsewhere at length that peripoiesis can be used to denote conditional security when
used misthologically. In fact, at least three out of five times this word is used in the NT, it is used misthologically
(1Thess 5:9; 2Thess 2:14; Heb 10:39).43 Lest there be an undiscerning reader like Corner who thinks that the
conditional security in one passage necessarily carries over into a separate context because of certain similarities, I
will pause long enough to harmonize my findings on both sets of passages. My misthological defense of conditional
security regarding peripoiesis should not be misperceived as necessarily44 carrying over into the remaining two
passages using this word (Eph 1:14; 1Pet 2:9). Even limiting the semantic range of peripoiesis to possession imposes
no insurmountable difficulty for security:
       •   God has destined us for the possession of salvation (1Thess 5:9).
       •   God called us for the possession of glory (2Thess 2:14).
       •   We believe for the possession of the soul (Heb 10:39).
       In each of these verses, the expression for the possession (eis peripoiesis) denotes the intended goal of
possessing something we do not yet have. We do not yet have this future, misthological salvation. This
misthological glory is also future. Just as we can forfeit our future possession of our crowns (Rev 3:11), so we can
forfeit our future possessions of our souls. Our possession of these items, not our being possessed, is at stake. Paul
uses the verbal form (peripoieomai) when he likewise promises: “Those who have served well as deacons purchase
for themselves a good standing and great boldness in the faith which is in Christ Jesus” (1Tim 3:13; TM). Like
Jesus, Paul is teaching us to lay up treasures for ourselves in heaven. The KJV is to be commended for translating
peripoieomai in this verse as purchase. But who does the purchasing? God? No! The deacons do. How? By
believing? No, by serving well.
       Is this purchase complete? No, not until their service as deacons is complete. Whose possession is at stake?
God’s? No, theirs. These deacons are purchasing this possession for themselves, not for God. The Lord is neither
the one doing the purchasing nor the one for whom the purchase is being made. Is the purchase of the possession
completed? No. One could translate peripoiesis in each of the above verses in the same manner. God has destined
and called us for purchasing these benefits for ourselves through ongoing faith and faithfulness. Peripoiesis refers
to the incomplete purchasing of a future possession within these contexts.
       Our possession of these future benefits (salvation, glory, and our souls) is in question in these misthological
passages, not God’s possession of us. As to the reference to soul in the Heb 10:39, although the preceding verse
refers to God’s soul (Heb 10:38), no one believes that it is God’s soul that is at stake in v. 39! Just as clearly, our
purchasing our soul (not God’s possession of our soul) is at stake. Jesus is clear when He warns of the danger of
losing one’s soul as to whose possession is at stake: “For what will it profit a man if he gains the whole world and
forfeits his soul? Or what will a man give in exchange for his soul?” (Mt 16:26). The future salvation of our souls
in terms of our possessing of our souls is a reward based on our works, as Jesus explains in the next verse: “For the
Son of Man will come in the glory of His Father with His angels, and then He will reward each according to his
works” (Mt 16:27).
God’s Possession
      God’s possession of our souls (as well as His possession of our glory and our salvation) is not the subject
matter in any of the three bulleted verses above. Our possession is contingently in question, not God’s unconditional
possession. Some translations very accurately bring out the implicit contrast between what we possess and what
God possesses with the remaining two uses of peripoiesis:

       •   The Spirit is given as a pledge of our inheritance, with a view to the redemption of God’s own possession,
           to the praise of His glory (Eph 1:14).
       •   You are a people for God’s own possession (1Pet 2:9).

43
   See discussions of peripoiesis in “Experience,” “Earn,” “Active/Passive,” “Active is most Common,” and “NT
Usage” within The Outer Darkness.

   The possibility of peripoiesis having a dualistic soteric-mistholic meaning in Eph 1:14 and 1Pet 2:9 is not denied.

dual capacity in Eph 1:14. This dualistic perspective has been developed more fully in the appendixes of DSAS and
expanded to potentially include Eph 1:13.
