        Peter is not saying that we are a people for our own possession. We do not belong to ourselves.
Soteriologically, we are God’s possession. Likewise, Paul does not intend to be understood as saying that we are
our own possession as a result of God’s redemption. The Lord paid the price; we belong to Him. Paul himself
clarifies that we are not our own for we have been bought with a price (1Cor 6:19-20). Wayne Watson has effectively
captured this truth in his song We Belong to Him. (It is even downloadable as a ringtone.) God Himself expresses
this reality with emotional force when He exclaims: “Do not fear, for I have redeemed you; I have called you by
name; you are Mine!” (Is 43:1). David’s background as a shepherd comes forth in the manner in which he pictures
this reality: “We are His people and the sheep of His pasture” (Ps 100:3). We are His sheep, not our own sheep. We
need not think of this shepherd imagery as being unable to stand up against the fierce attacks of unconditional
security:

      David said to Saul, “Your servant was tending his father’s sheep. When a lion or a bear came and took
      a lamb from the flock, I went out after him and attacked him, and rescued it from his mouth; and when
      he rose up against me, I seized him by his beard and struck him and killed him. Your servant has killed
      both the lion and the bear” (1Sam 17:34-36).

       Will Jesus do less than David in defending His sheep? The loving Shepherd is also a fierce Shepherd. Will
those trying to pluck Jesus’ sheep out of Jesus’ hand or seeking to bring soteriological charges against His Father’s
elect suffer any less a fate than the lion and bear who attempted to take David’s sheep? (Jn 10:28-29; Rom 8:33)
The logical impression that one should derive from being kept as God’s possession and guarded as His child might
be conveyed by the imagery of a father warning someone trying to take his child away from him, “Over my dead
body!” God has more than a sling or a gun at His disposal when it comes to protecting His child and warning the
conditional securitist, “You can have my child when you pry him from My cold, dead fingers.” The only way to
take us out of Jesus’ nailed-scarred hand would be to cut off His hand. No Arminian argument is going to be able
to amputate us from the body of Christ.
       Jesus is not only our defense attorney, but He is also the executioner of those who seek to take His sheep
from Him. Lions and bears were attacking David’s flock; Arminian wolves are attacking ours. They try to rob the
flock of the assurance that comes from being the Lord’s own purchased possession. As the Lord will kill the
soteriological accusers of His flock by throwing such attackers into the Lake of Fire, so securitist pastors should
shepherd the flock by killing the conditional arguments of the wolves attacking the flock. Paul uses the verbal form
(peripoieomai) when he urges, “Be on guard for yourselves and for all the flock, among which the Holy Spirit has
made you overseers, to shepherd the church of God which He purchased with His own blood. I know that after my
departure savage wolves will come in among you, not sparing the flock” (Acts 20:28-29). Like the apostate who
tramples the precious blood of the Lord underfoot in denying its saving efficacy, the conditionalist spurns the power
of the blood in preference for salvation by works.
Dualistic Protection
       Peter apparently is affirming unconditional security by means of shepherd imagery when he unconditionally
assures believers that in the aoristic moment they were converted, Jesus became “the Shepherd and Guardian of
their souls (1Pet 2:25). Granted, Peter refers dualistically to the soul: at times misthologically (1Pet 1:9; 4:19) and
at times soteriologically (1Pet 1:22; 2:25). But would we expect otherwise? The Lord, who is able to unconditionally
guard His possession of our soul, is certainly able to conditionally guard our possession of our soul. The fact that
the Lord guards our souls in two aspects should not be overlooked.
       Will not the father who encourages his daughter to put her money in her little piggy bank not only guard the
bank but also guard his daughter? God encourages us to entrust Him misthologically with our pennies by doing
what is right. Still, His promises to guard our spiritual pennies are contingent on our entrusting these pennies to
Him. If a child fails to put her pennies in the piggy bank, preferring instead to spend them on ice cream, she cannot
expect her father to guard her spent pennies. But does this mean that the child must live in fear that her father will
not guard her? Perish the thought! Arminianism leads to unthinkable conclusions.
       God’s promises to conditionally guard the aspects of our lives/souls that we misthologically entrusted to Him
for our possession should not be misconstrued as conditional promises to guard that aspect of our lives/souls which
He has immutably guaranteed as His possession. We remain His child as a result of trusting Him for eternal life
even if we fail to entrust Him with our daily lives.
Chapter 1. Guaranteed Security                                                                                  Page 17
Power versus Ability
        At the ontological level, God has given us eternal life by imparting it to our spirits. Not even God has the
power or ability to snuff out eternal life. Eternal life is God’s kind of life. As such, it cannot be snuffed out because
God cannot die. To be sure, Jesus (even as God) died on the cross. Yet He could not die in the sense of loose eternal
life as His divine attribute.
                                    Illustration 5. Did Jesus Lose Eternal Life?




       Jesus’ qualitative experience of eternal life could not drop below the threshold of ontological possession.
Neither can ours.45 God cannot take His own life spiritual, so how can He take our life spiritually when He has
given us the same kind of spiritual life that He has? God cannot spiritually kill us or Himself. God’s theoretical
ability to kill Himself or to kill us spiritually is not a function of power. As Murphree explains, “Even infinite power
cannot do what cannot be done….infinite power does not entail infinite ability.”46 There are logical, moral, and self-
imposed limitations on God’s ability. God does not have the ability to kill Himself. Suicide is not an option for God.
       Pertaining to the gift of eternal life that God has given us, homicide is not a divine option either. Even if God
had the power to kill us by taking eternal life away from us, He does not have the ability to do so because it would
run counter to His own immutability. Our experience of that life may fluctuate temporally and misthologically.
Even God’s experience of eternal life is not impassable; nevertheless, His possession of eternal life is immutable.
In the same way, our soteriological possession of eternal life is grounded unflinchingly in the Lord’s unconditional
possession of us. What gives an Arminian the right to teach that a believer (who irrevocably has been given God’s
kind of life) can die in the sense of losing the gift of eternal life (that has been imparted to him or her by a God who
cannot lie or die)? Eternal life is not only life with God, but it is also the life of God. Having the latter guarantees
the former.
Transaction versus Realization
       As deduced elsewhere, the nominal and verbal forms of peripoiesis mean to gain and keep as a possession
by paying the necessary purchase price, thus to achieve and win by much effort. Sometimes it is the Lord who puts
forth the effort and who pays the price, even to the extent of laying down His life and shedding His blood for the
sheep. In such cases, He is the active agent who has purchased us for Himself as His own possession. He is the
purchaser, we are the possession. Not only are we the passive possession in such cases, but it must also be noted
that the purchase has been completed; we are the purchased possession. The transaction has been consummated.
No further price waits to be paid. We have been marked as paid in full. Paul uses the aorist in Acts 20:28 in harmony
with the completed nature of the transaction. The action is punctiliar: He purchased () us with His blood.
       Those translations that bring out the completed nature of the transaction are to be commended. The full price
45
  These types of graphs are explained more fully in other of my writings.
46
  Jon Tal Murphree, Divine Paradoxes: A Finite View of an Infinite God (Camp Hill, PA: Christian Publications,
1998), 60.
Page 18                                                                                      Dual Sealing and Security
of our redemption already has been paid for by the blood of Christ. That payment was made for us, not to us. The
Holy Spirit, in contrast, is given to us. To be sure, an overlapping of the word payment occurs in both cases. But
the blood of Christ by which we were redeemed was paid to God’s justice. The Spirit, by whom we are sealed, was
given to us (as a down payment), not to God’s justice.
       Although we still wait for the unconditional realization of the transition, the transaction has been completed.
The manner in which peripoiesis is used in such cases (Acts 20:28; Eph 1:14; 1Pet 2:9) logically conveys
unconditional security. It is a done deal.
       At other times, however, we are the active agent. We are the ones making the purchase. By paying the
necessary purchase price, in order to achieve and win the benefit by much effort, we are currently in the process of
purchasing the misthological salvation of our souls. These misthological transactions (Lk 17:33 CT; 1Thess 5:9;
2Thess 2:14; 1Tim 3:13; Heb 10:39) are not complete. And in them, we are not passive. As a result of them, we
will be the owners rather than what is owned. In failing to give due consideration to the completed versus
uncompleted nature of the purchase, to the divine versus human agents making the purchase, to the agent versus the
object of the purchase, and to the means of the purchase (as to whether it is by Christ’s blood versus our service),
it is not surprising that conditionalists have failed to discern the unconditional versus conditional nature of the
purchase. One can only derive conditional security from such texts by ignoring a list of details within the texts. The
security is in the details. Too bad Corner did not take time to examine the details in his writing.
                                                    Sealed or Saved
       Corner asks, “Does the Bible say Christians are sealed unto the day of redemption or saved unto the day of
redemption (Eph 4:30)?” (Emphasis his.)47 Fair enough. Securitists have a couple of questions of their own. (1) Does
the Bible say Christians are sealed unto the day of redemption or probated unto the day of redemption (Eph 4:30)?
(2) Does the Bible say Christians are sealed unto the day of redemption or sealed until the day of redemption
(Eph 4:30)?
       With his question, Corner evidently hopes to interject a dichotomy between being sealed and being saved.
But does he provide any examples where the unsaved are sealed or the sealed are unsaved? No. Does not the fact
that they are sealed mean that they are saved? Yes, soterically. Does that mean that securitists are speaking
equatively? Not necessarily, but it does mean that in failing to deduce the semantic overlap between the sealed and
saved symmetry, Corner has implied a false dichotomy. To speak substantively of being sealed (as the Bible does
in Rev 7:4), the sealed are saved, and the saved are sealed. At the very least, this is the case in the Pauline passages,
which are the passages in dispute. So what does Corner hope to accomplish with his question? In terms of security,
there is no substantial difference between having been sealed with the Spirit in Eph 1:13 and having been saved by
grace in the immediate context, fourteen verses later (Eph 2:5,8).
       If anything, in examining the broader biblical spectrum, being sealed is a stronger affirmation of security
than is being saved. Misthologists are well aware of cases where being saved by God is linearly contingent upon
the performance of the person who is saved. Both sanctification and co-glorification salvation are contingent upon
the performance of the person who already is saved soteriologically. However, we know of no cases where being
sealed by God is linearly contingent upon the action of the one who is sealed.48 One may take the mark of God or
the mark of the beast, but one cannot take both. Nor can one take one and then the other. One may not take the mark
of God and the mark of the beast simultaneously or interchangeably. Both God and the beast use a permanent
marker.
       Cases where seals are broken do exist. God’s seals on the scroll in Revelation were broken at God’s
appropriate time. Nonetheless, the seals were not broken because of some defect in the scroll’s performance. The
breaking of the seals was not due to an unfilled linear contingency on the part of the scroll. Nor was the scroll able
to break its own seals. Far to the contrary, the cry goes out: “Who is worthy to open the book and to break its seals?”
(Rev 5:2)
       Yet what if the Arminian, in hopes of finding some example of an object becoming unsealed, wishes to ignore
such details and still insists that seals may be broken? Then let it be noted that even the scrolls were not unsealed
in the sense of having the seal removed. These seals were broken rather than removed. The wax signet still would
have remained on the scroll. The broken wax seal was not scraped off the scroll. Does Corner think the Holy Spirit
can be broken? Let us hope not. However, even if he does want to appeal to this broken imagery, it should be noted

47
     Corner, 541.
48
     This SAS statement is open to modification in DSAS. See Dual Seal in 2Tim 2:19, 68.
Chapter 1. Guaranteed Security                                                                                  Page 19
that the broken seal remains on the object that receives the seal. Likewise, the Holy Spirit would still remain with
the believer. This truth is not only conveyed by the imagery but also by the fact that Paul affirms that this seal is
given as a down payment to the object that is sealed. To be sure, the sealed object belongs to God as God’s
possession. However, the seal itself belongs to the object. The Holy Spirit is ours to keep.49
       Apparently, Corner wishes to use his question to create a blank in the reader’s understanding of the text’s
meaning so that he can import his meaning into the text. So to proceed to my first counter question, let it be restated
in such a manner so as to supply Corner’s unspoken interjection.

•    Does the Bible say that Christians are sealed unto the day of redemption or ___________________ (probated,
     protected, secured) unto the day of redemption (Eph 4:30)?

       Lexically, the blank may be filled in with either of the latter two choices: protected or secured. BDAG’s
definition of seal in this type of passage has been noted already: the mark denoting ownership also carries with it
the protection of the owner. Its citation of Is 44:5 as an eschatological and conceptual parallel is noteworthy: “Write
on his hand, ‘Belonging to the Lord.’” Regarding the meaning of seal used to secure the tomb in the very passage
to which Corner alludes for support, BDAG defines sealing as to provide with a seal as a security measure. L-N
likewise defines it: to provide with a seal as a security measure. UBS: secure with a seal. One only has to read the
passage in question (Mt 27:65-66), which I quoted previously, in order to see that security is the apparent meaning.
Seemingly, the transparent reason that Corner did not quote the passage in question is because doing so would have
refuted his position. To what lexicon (or for that matter, to what text dealing with sealing) will Corner appeal to
show that to be sealed by God means to be put on probation rather than under God’s protection? As demonstrated
herein, the purpose of this seal is protection not probation, which leads to my second counter-question: Does the
Bible say Christians are sealed unto the day of redemption or sealed until the day of redemption (Eph 4:30)?
       Opinions vary as to whether eis is best translated as for or unto in this verse, and until is yet another popular
option for eis in Eph 1:14. So to restate the question in terms of the most popular candidates,

•    Does the Bible say Christians are sealed __________ (for, unto, until) the day of redemption (Eph 1:14; 4:30)?

Unlike the previous question, in which one has to throw out Corner’s interjection of probation (since it has no
textual or lexical support), all three of these answers are correct in this verse. Believers are sealed irrevocably until

49
   Following the CT, the NAS mistranslates 1Thess 4:8 as: “Consequently, he who rejects this is not rejecting man
but the God who gives His Holy Spirit to you.” Contrastively, following the MT, the NKJ appropriately translates
it as: “Therefore he who rejects this does not reject man, but God, who has also given us His Holy Spirit.” Better
yet, “Consequently, he who rejects this is not rejecting man but the God who indeed has given His Holy Spirit to
you” (TM). God is not in the process of giving the gift of the Holy Spirit to individual believers. Rather, He has
already given His Holy Spirit to the individual believer.
      Even the reading of the CT collaborates elsewhere. In Acts 10:45, the perfect tense is used. The gift of the
Spirit has been poured out (▬ ) upon those who have believed, not is being poured out (£). The perfect aspect in
Acts 10:45 should be understood in the aoristic tense used above in 1Thess 4:8 and in the following aoristic verses
as well concerning those who “have tasted of the heavenly gift and have been made partakers of the Holy Spirit”
(Heb 6:4). God “has sealed us and has given us the Spirit in our hearts as a guarantee” (2Cor 1:22; TM). “He who
has prepared us for this very purpose is God, who has given to us the Spirit as a guarantee” (2Cor 5:5; NKJ).
Although the same underlying Greek text is being used in each case, the NKJ is superior to the NAS in these texts
as well. The believer has been sealed, not simply was sealed, much less is being sealed, with the Spirit. Eph 1:13
likewise should be understood as a perfective aorist. For additional examples, see Believe: An Aspectual and
Metaphorical Analysis from the Gospel of John.
      Nevertheless, for those who cannot bring themselves to accept the aorist of the MT but feel some compulsion
to adopt the present tense of the CT in 1Thess 4:8, one might do so with a distributive: . As people become
believers, they are given the gift of the Holy Spirit. The argument of this book is not only that they aspectually have
been given (▬ ) the Spirit but that contextually they permanently have been given (▬▬ ) the Spirit. Believers
have been permanently and unconditionally guaranteed this security. Unconditional security is strongly being
affirmed by the biblical text. See “Eschatological Perfective Aorist” in Believe for additional examples.

the day of redemption. As long as until is not taken to mean that they return the Holy Spirit (i.e., lose the Holy
Spirit) after their redemption is completed, the nuance of unconditional, interim security may be fully appreciated
by the use of this translation.50 The feasibility of the other two alternatives (for, unto) may be used to help explain
that sealing of the believer’s heart until the day of redemption does not mean that his or her heart becomes unsealed
after the day of redemption. Rather, the words combine to make an unconditional guarantee of eternal security.
                                                Corner’s Lack of Logic
        Corner’s implicit interjection of a false dichotomy with his question between being sealed and saved is bad
enough, as if one could be sealed but not saved. Even Ashby, Corner’s fellow Arminian, decries the thought of
saved unbelievers! So what does Corner hope to accomplish by implying that one can be sealed unsaved? Would
not his own fellow Arminians roll their eyes at his suggestion?
        Corner uses some very questionable examples to try to advance his argument. For example, on the same page
in which Corner seeks to ask a question intended to suggest a complete disparity between being sealed and saved
(as if one could be sealed but unsaved), he, in like manner, implies that since Judas lost his salvation, Judas was not
unconditionally sealed unto the day of redemption. This assumption begs a few questions.
        Was Judas ever saved? There are many reasons to think that Judas was never saved. Were the disciples sealed
before Pentecost? Many think they were not. Even if Judas was saved and lost his salvation (and in doing so lost
his seal), as Corner’s question concerning Judas seems to suggest, does this equation between simultaneously losing
one’s seal and salvation not refute Corner’s previous question in which he tried to drive a wedge between being
sealed and saved? If to become unsaved means to become unsealed, then would to become sealed not mean to
become saved? Alternatively, if being sealed does not mean that one is saved, then does this mean that Corner thinks
there are sealed unbelievers? Ashby’s eyes surely would roll at such a ridiculous suggestion. It becomes distracting
to Corner’s thesis when the answers which he himself seems to suggest to his own questions refute his own
argument.
        Corner also tries to make inherit equal sealed. But this is an apples-to-oranges comparison. A conditional
inheritance should be contrasted with (not equated with) an unconditional seal. If one wishes to compare seal to
inheritance, then the soteriological seal should be compared to the soteriological inheritance. A soteriological seal
should not be compared to a misthological inheritance. When comparing an unconditional seal with an
unconditional inheritance, truer similarities may be found. Being a sealed heir is contingent upon being a child of
God. Being a co-heir is contingent upon being a faithful child of God (Rom 8:17). To simplify the matter, consider
a couple of passages that soteriologically link such matters:

•    Present redemption → adoption → Spirit in heart → heir (Gal 4:5-6)
•    Present redemption → seal → heir → future redemption (Eph 1:7-14).

       Our present redemption guarantees our future soteriological redemption by means of having the Spirit in our
hearts as a seal. These are unbreakable, unconditional chains of events set in motion by a moment of faith.
                                                 Day of Redemption
       The NLT equates being sealed with being saved: “Remember, he has identified you as his own, guaranteeing
that you will be saved on the day of redemption” (Eph 4:30). I have no quarrel with such a paraphrase as an
explanation of the text; although, it misses some of the lexical richness of the passage. Still, it does pose an
interesting question: “What does it mean to be saved on the day of redemption as a result of having been sealed
unto the day of redemption?”
       The eschatological nature of this day of redemption is self-apparent. Notwithstanding, the more exact timing
of Paul’s reference is provided by Paul elsewhere: “And not only this, but also we ourselves, having the first fruits
of the Spirit, even we ourselves groan within ourselves, waiting eagerly for our adoption as sons, the redemption of
our body” (Rom 8:23). The day of redemption is the day when our bodies will experience redemption. It is the day
that we, as church-age believers, are waiting eagerly. Without a doubt, the day of redemption is the rapture. Paul
elsewhere clarifies that believers will be delivered unconditionally from the wrath of the tribulation by means of
50
  Alternatively, even if one interprets until to mean that we cease to have the Holy Spirit in this capacity once we
reach the day of redemption, this is sufficient to demonstrate the superiority of the securitist model over the
conditionalistic model. For if you have retained this ministry of the Spirit until that future point in time, then you
are assured of being in heaven.
Chapter 1. Guaranteed Security                                                                                  Page 21
the rapture (see DFS).
       If one simply puts two and two together, one derives the conclusion that being sealed for the day of
redemption is an unconditional guarantee of being raptured. Walvoord concludes his article combating the partial
rapture position with this very verse. And although Walvoord marshals many ill-founded remarks and arguments
against the partial rapture position, his deduction from this citation appears to be right on target.51 Believers are
unconditionally guaranteed the glorified redemption of their bodies. The sealing of all believers guarantees a full
rapture. All believers will have redeemed bodies.
                                                  Grieve unto Death
       In his earlier failed attempt at making sealing by the Spirit a linear contingency equatable with filling by the
Spirit,52 Barth apparently was trying to set the stage for his later assessment that Eph 4:30 is

      an unmistakable threat which is reminiscent of the Synoptic words about the “sin against the Holy
      Spirit” (Mark 3:28-30 par.).…“grieving the Holy Spirit” may well be a synonym of “sinning against
      the Spirit”; nothing but death can be its consequence.”

       Barth means eternal death since this is the standard conditionalistic equation in which grieving the Spirit is
taken to be synonymous with committing the unpardonable sin and thus being placed “in danger of eternal
damnation” (Mk 3:29; KJV). Even more unequivocally, Barth states that those believers who grieve the Holy Spirit
can expect God to become their enemy “just as an OT prophetic voice (Isa 63:10) had affirmed that God himself is
hurt and becomes his people’s enemy when his Holy Spirit is grieved.” As the verse warns, “They rebelled and
grieved his Holy Spirit, therefore he turned to be their enemy and himself fought against them.” Corroboration is
sought by Barth in a NT example of grief: “No saint can take the risk of introducing sadness into the realm of joy
created and guaranteed by God (cf. Heb 13:17).”53 Not surprisingly, Barth also ties lying to the Spirit with death
(Acts 5:3). Grieving the Holy Spirit can be a sin unto death. For a conditionalist, this passage (and others like it) is
not limited to physical death but extends over into spiritual death as well.
       With the wave of the scholastic wand and a few words of exegetical hocus pocus, the promise of being sealed
unto redemption is turned instead by conditionalists into a threat of being sealed forever in the Lake of Fire. The
devil will be sealed in the Lake of Fire until the appointed time is fulfilled (Rev 20:3). In like manner, Barth should
have concluded that believers would be sealed until the day of their redemption is fulfilled. How is it that Arminians
think that believers can break God’s seal when even Satan himself cannot? Nevertheless, rather than dismiss Barth’s
comments with a wave of my hand, it will be more prudent to strengthen his arguments so that I may respond to the
Arminian position at its full strength. On behalf of those Arminians, therefore, who understandably wish to see their
position defeated when that position is presented at full power, I will link Acts 5:3 with other texts that insecuritists
generally perceive as dealing with premature death—which they, in turn, believe results in eternal death. And I will
not be satisfied with stock answers from my fellow securitists.
       Securitists, as a rule, readily will admit that a host of texts refer to premature physical death as a punishment
for sin. Among the more well-known are: Acts 5:3; Jam 5:15; and 1Jn 5:16. Insecuritists certainly will add, and I
deem correctly, Eze 3:20 and its related passages to this list:

      When a righteous man turns away from his righteousness and commits iniquity, and I place an obstacle
      before him, he shall die; since you have not warned him, he shall die in his sin, and his righteous deeds
      which he has done shall not be remembered; but his blood I will require at your hand.

       In my judgment, this text may well represent genuine believers (at least they should not be excluded) who
die in their sins.54 Ananias and Sapphira were NT believers who died prematurely because of their sin, and they
most certainly died with unconfessed sin. Contrary to some securitists, I insist that 1Jn 1:9 makes the forgiveness

of such sins dependent upon confession. Thus, believers who die with sins they were not willing to confess, die in

       Moreover, contrary to most securitists, my assessment is that believers can commit the unpardonable sin.56
Accordingly, such believers would die in their sin. Although some securitists readily would agree with my
assessment that believers can die because of their sins and in their sins, acknowledging that believers can commit
the unpardonable sin or at least reach an unpardonable state is not necessarily standard fare among securitists.
       Thus, not only do I concur to a considerable degree with the opposing point of view expressed by some
insecuritists, but I do so to a degree that demonstrates that I am open to conditionalistic arguments when they are
in accord with exegetical evidence. Pointing out my divergences from some within my own camp on such matters
opens up the dialogue to give due consideration to the counterclaims by conditionalists to stock securitist answers.
       Nevertheless, despite my concurrence with insecuritists on a number of such points, I find more than a few
problems with their insecurity. For one thing, soteriological insecurity fails to take into account the different types
of vertical forgiveness pictured in Illustration 6.57 For an insecuritist to assert that believers who die in their sin
must die in a soteriological state of being unforgiving simply begs the question as to which type of vertical
forgiveness is in view: judicial forgiveness, filial forgiveness, temporal forgiveness, or misthological forgiveness.
       The knee-jerk reaction by some insecuritists, such as that of Wesley as quoted by Corner, which remarks that
premature death cannot be meant because we will all die sometime hardly deserves comment.58 Premature death is

bad enough, but a premature death that guarantees that a believer will spend eternity in the outer darkness is even
worse and ought not to be scoffed at by insecuritists.
       A more mature Arminian viewpoint would argue that since believers who die in their sin have none of their
previous righteous acts remembered, such believers must be thrown into Hell because if they had been allowed into
Heaven they would have at least received some rewards. Thus, from this perspective, since Jesus promises believers
that they would not lose their rewards in Heaven, apostate believers must not be considered believers any longer
and must not be in Heaven. The perceptive Arminian could reinforce such a conditional perspective by appealing
to Johannine parallels. Those who die in their sins cannot go to Heaven (Jn 8:21). Dying in one’s sin is a
soteriological issue equated with being an unbeliever (Jn 8:24).
       A mature misthological counter is readily at hand, however. Such righteous acts are not remembered in regard
to the subject matter at hand, namely, escaping temporal death and, by logical implication, misthological death.59
As to the Johannine parallels, the soteriological nature of these warnings is not in dispute. Soteriological forgiveness
is but one plane of vertical forgiveness. The way to receive forgiveness in that plane is to believe in Christ for the
soteriological forgiveness of one’s sins. Jesus explicitly links this type of forgiveness with simply believing in Him:
“I said therefore to you, that you shall die in your sins; for unless you believe [] that I am He, you shall die in your
sins” (Jn 8:24).
       This belief is punctiliar. The use of an aorist subjective denoting punctiliar faith as the means to escape
soteriologically dying in ones’ sins is counterproductive to a NOSAS argument for linear contingency. Even aside
from appealing to the punctiliar aspect of the Greek verb, the Johannine parallels base this soteriological forgiveness
on simple faith. The texts pertaining to premature death do not. Simple faith is not enough to save one from
premature death. Instead, deliverance from premature death is conditioned on one’s performance and thus on works.
Since soteriological forgiveness is not conditioned on works, it makes little sense for conditionalists to appeal to
such texts as examples of soteriological forgiveness. Unbelievers die soteriologically in their sins and go to Hell.
Believers die misthologically in their sins and go to the outer darkness. By Jesus’ own testimony in the Johannine
passages, believers do not share the same fate as unbelievers.
       Working my way up the list of objections I have provided from Barth, I, likewise, may respond that Heb 13:17
need not be taken to mean more than it says. Sin does not pay. Sin is unprofitable, even for the believer. Even if
alusiteles is taken to indicate the infliction of harm, which is certainly possible, it certainly would be in accordance
with misthology to affirm accountability and, therefore, acknowledge that it poses no problem for soteriological
security.
       In connection with the OT text in Is 63:10, whereby Barth merely implies that God can become the enemy
of sealed believers, I will clearly make this connection for him from the NT. Sealed believers can become enemies
of God (Jam 4:4).60 However, Barth has failed to provide any reason to think that such believers necessarily become
unsealed as a result of this estrangement.
       Paul, on the other hand, with his choice of verb tense, the imagery of down payment, and teleological
fulfillment, has furnished every reason to believe that such believers remain sealed. In light of these very positive
affirmations, Barth’s assessment is most unreasonable. The seal is the person of the Holy Spirit, not the emotions
of the Holy Spirit. Just because the Spirit is not impassible does not mean that He is not immutable. Just because
we can lie to the Holy Spirit and grieve Him does not mean that He can get mad and lie to us. Misthologists would
have no theological objection to accommodating the Arminian by picturing God as getting mad at unfaithful
believers and throwing them into the outer darkness as a result of grieving the Holy Spirit. But as interpreters
seeking to rightly divide the word of truth, we must admit that it seems highly unlikely that this is Paul’s point in
this context. Barth claims that Eph 4:30 is “an unmistakable threat.” Actually, Barth’s postulation is very dubitable.
       Indisputably, when a brother pleads, “Don’t break your mother’s heart,” he is not, thereby, indicating that
your mother may get mad at you and disown you if you break her heart. Your brother is simply reminding you that
you do not want to hurt someone who loves you so deeply, has done so much for you, and will continue to love you
unconditionally even if no one else will. Your brother is underscoring her love, not her anger. Surely, this is the
point brother Paul is making. Saying that the Spirit will leave us if we grieve Him is nothing less than slanderous.
What mother would want it said of her that she would leave her children if they grieve her? You may break her
heart, but you will not break her love. Breaking the Holy Spirit’s heart is not equatable to breaking the seal. The
59
  See my other writings for discussion of misthological death.

seal is the person of the Spirit, not the emotional state of the Spirit. One can no more equate breaking the Spirit’s
heart with breaking the seal than one can assume that breaking your mother’s heart is the same as breaking your
mother’s back. This distinction breaks the back of the Arminian argument.
       A difference of opinion exists as to whether the prohibition in Eph 4:30 should be translated as stop grieving
the Holy Spirit. Wuest believes the negation of the present imperative in this verse should be translated stop; Lenski
does not. In my assessment, Wuest has the stronger argument.61 The context certainly would collaborate with this
assessment. As Bond points out a few verses earlier: “Paul charges [these] believers to no longer live as unbelievers
[4:17]….The believers in Ephesus were once like that (2:1-5). They were now to stop living that way.”62
       If so, then an argument can be made immediately that these believers still were sealed by the Spirit for the
day of redemption despite the fact that they were grieving the Spirit. This would be an implicit affirmation of eternal
security in and of itself. Regardless, 1Cor 6:17-20 is a strong conceptual parallel to these soteriological passages
regarding sealing and the applicability of the word stop.
                                             Illustration 7. Sealed Temple

           Eph 4:30                                               1Cor 6:17-20
    And do not grieve the Holy     But the one who joins himself to the Lord is one spirit with Him. Flee immorality.
    Spirit of God, by whom you     Every other sin that a man commits is outside the body, but the immoral man sins

•     Stop grieving the Spirit ≈ flee immorality.
•     Sealed with Spirit in heart ≈ body is temple of Spirit who is in you.

       Whether it is admitted that the believers in Ephesus needed to be told that they needed to stop grieving the
Spirit who had sealed them, there can be no doubt that the believers in Corinth were, in fact, grieving the Spirit who
indwelt them and needed to stop doing so. In their case, the exhortation undoubtedly could be worded: “Stop
grieving the Holy Spirit of God, by whom you were sealed for the day of redemption.” The fact such believers need
to stop grieving the Spirit who was indwelling them proves that the Spirit does not stop dwelling within believers
when He is grieved by them. They had been sealed and were still sealed even though they presently were grieving
the Spirit. Likewise, they had been redeemed/bought and still were redeemed/bought even though they were not
living accordingly. To refer back to the song by Watson Wayne, we are: “Bought with a price through one sacrifice.
We belong to Him.” Even when we grieve the Holy Spirit, we still belong to Him.
       Barth appeals to Hebrews to question the association between sealing with permanence. I will appeal to
Hebrews to prove that we belong to Him permanently: “For by one offering He has perfected for all time those who
are sanctified” (Heb 10:14).63 Profitability, in contrast, is a rewards concept and is conditioned upon our works:
“Obey your leaders, and submit to them; for they keep watch over your souls, as those who will give an account.
Let them do this with joy and not with grief, for this would be unprofitable for you” (Heb 13:17).
       A security that comes from being perfectly sealed is a grace-based concept and thus dynamically opposed to
dependency on our works. To link soteriological security conditionally with misthological contingency is an
atrocious incorrigibility.64 Barth’s theology is theological barf. Like the guards who sealed the tomb and were
instructed to “make it as secure as you know how” (Mt 27:65), Paul has made our seal as secure as he knows how.
Those, who, like Barth, are trying to steal away that security by breaking the seal are acting at cross-purposes to
Paul’s literary imagery.

                                                 Metaphysical Union
       Paul specifies the location of this sealing pledge as being in our heart: “Who also sealed us and gave us the
Spirit in our hearts as a pledge” (2Cor 1:22). In Modern Greek, arrabon (pledge) is used of an engagement ring.
But in the Koine Greek in which Paul is expressing our security, he does not locate the seal on our finger but in our
hearts. Engagement rings can be returned, sealed hearts cannot. Even in the physical arena, you cannot cut out your
own heart. Many might object that believers could climb up on the operating table and let the devil cut out their
transformed heart and in this way become separated from Christ. However, Paul has ruled out anyone receiving
such an assisting hand from the devil in Rom 8:35-39. The devil does not have a license from God to perform this
kind of surgery:
      35
        Who shall separate us from the love of Christ? Shall tribulation, or distress, or persecution, or famine,
      or nakedness, or peril, or sword? 36 Just as it is written, “For Thy sake we are being put to death all day
      long; We were considered as sheep to be slaughtered.” 37 But in all these things we overwhelmingly
      conquer through Him who loved us. 38 For I am convinced that neither death, nor life, nor angels, nor
      principalities, nor things present, nor things to come, nor powers, 39 nor height, nor depth, nor any other
      created thing, shall be able to separate us from the love of God, which is in Christ Jesus our Lord.

        Some might respond that Paul is not referring to our physical heart, so this operating illustration is too literal.
To be sure, Paul is referring to our heart metaphysically. As a matter of fact, he refers to it as our spirit on other
occasions. For example, in 1Cor 6:17 he says, “But the one who joins himself to the Lord is one spirit with Him.”
In Rom 8:10 he likewise states, “And if Christ is in you, though the body [experientially] is dead because of sin, yet
the spirit [ontologically] is alive because of [imputational] righteousness.” As shown elsewhere, Rom 8:10 proves
that Rom 8:13 is not saying that we can die spiritually. Christ remains in a believer’s spirit even if the believer
experientially is dead in sin.65
        The point in the present discussion though is that having the Spirit indwelling us is equated with having the
Spirit of Christ in our spirit and with belonging to Christ (Rom 8:9). Metaphysically speaking, we would have to
open up our spirit and take out the Spirit of Christ if we wished to unseal ourselves. However, we do not have the
tools by which to perform such an operation. We did not give birth to ourselves, and we cannot un-birth ourselves.
We can commit physical suicide, but we cannot commit spiritual suicide (in the sense of depriving ourselves of the
gift of eternal life once we have received it). We can only kill the body (Lk 12:4). The literal illustration above
regarding having a sealed heart well illustrates the metaphysical impossibility facing us if we contemplate spiritual
suicide at the ontological level.
        Paul gives us a very easy means by which to understand the point he is making concerning being spiritually
joined to the Lord in 1Cor 6:17 since Paul compares it to sex: “The one who joins himself to a harlot is one body
with her” because “the two will become one flesh” (1Cor 6:16). As Hunt comments, “The word joined denotes the
closest of ties, and it literally means glued” (emphasis his).66 You cannot crawl into bed with a harlot and then claim
to be unaffected by it when you crawl out of bed. That experience becomes a part of you (and you will be judged
for it). Paul’s indication is that there is something lasting about the experience. He is also informing us through
various illustrations, such as seals and pledges, that there is something lasting about our spiritual union with the
Lord. You cannot go back and become a spiritual virgin after your spiritual union with the Lord any more than you
can go back and become a physical virgin after your physical union with a harlot.67 You cannot become a spiritual
65
   See “Static Versus Dynamic Life” in The Outer Darkness.
66
   Dwight L. Hunt, 1 Corinthians, GNTC, ed. Robert N. Wilkin (Denton, TX: Grace Evangelical Society, 2010),
731.
67
   Of course, believers are spiritual virgins in one sense of the word as a result of their sealing (2Cor 11:2). Paul
desires to present the Corinthian believers to Christ as pure virgins misthologically. This presentation contingently
anticipates a future misthological union with Christ as His Bride. The Bible also describes believers as virgins in
the parable of the virgins (Mt 25:1-13). Some of these virgins will be wise in Matthean terms (or chaste in Pauline
terms); some will not be. At the experiential level and misthological level, being a chaste\wise virgin (overcomer)
morally is conditioned on spiritual faithfulness to the Lord. At both the experiential level and misthological level,
being a chaste\wise virgin (overcomer) morally is conditioned on spiritual faithfulness to the Lord.
      Paul is using the imagery differently in 1Cor 6:17, however, to refer to a union that already has taken place
between the believer and the Lord when the believer becomes a member of the Lord’s body. All believers already
Page 26                                                                                      Dual Sealing and Security
virgin in the sense of being disjoined with the Lord (as if the experience and result of the experience never happened)
once you have become enjoined with the Lord. Spiritual death at the ontological level is impossible once one has
been made spiritually alive with eternal life.
                                                     God’s Glory
       Paul concludes Eph 1:14 by saying that this guaranteed redemption is to the praise of His glory (doxa). Our
redemption is not to the praise of the glory of our perseverance but to the praise of the glory of His preservation.
Contrary to Calminianism, our perseverance cannot be a condition for the retention of our redemption; otherwise,
we would get to share the glory for our redemption. The exclusive nature of this doxology rules out our active
cooperation in retaining this redemption. Sure, we may work in coming to saving faith, but the punctiliar response
of faith itself has no meritorious value and is not a work. Moreover, our salvation is not conditioned theologically
on preconversional works.
       In contrast to the passive response of our saving faith, our subsequent faith does have meritorious value and
can result in our praise (1Pet 1:7). Therefore, our sealing cannot be conditioned on our subsequent faith. Sealing is
based on our initial faith, not our persevering in faith. Otherwise, our sealing would be conditioned on our merit
and result in our praise. Our possession of life was, and remains, conditioned on our singular punctiliar passive
response of saving faith so that God’s grace solely may be glorified in that our redemption was, and is, not
conditioned on anything meritorious that we do.68
                                                       Devil’s Glory
       God is the obvious sealer in Eph 1:13-14. The Lord’s promise prevents Him from taking the Spirit from us
or from unsealing us. Yet the claim is often made that we can unseal ourselves and send the Holy Spirit away by
means of our free will. Suppose for the moment that everything I have said above about this passage is wrong and
that we can, in fact, stand the verse on its head with our perverted minds, find a loophole that Paul did not anticipate,
unseal ourselves, and force the Holy Spirit to leave. Who gets the credit? We do. But not exclusively. As we burn
in Hell, we will have to share credit with Satan for our achievement of overpowering the Holy Spirit and breaking
God’s unconditional promise.
       All things considered, once we meet the condition of punctiliar faith, it is incumbent upon God to keep His
promise. No more conditions remain for us to keep. No strings are attached. Nevertheless, assume for the moment
that we can take this engagement ring (a contemporary translation for arrabon) and throw it back in God’s face.
After all, from the conditionalistic viewpoint, it might be said that even though God does not have the right to take
it, we might have the audacity to return it.
       Will we be able to take sole responsibility for our action? No. Satan will be able to step up to God and say,
“See what I made him do. Throw that ring right back in Your face!” This is not an affirmation of the-devil-made-
me-do-it philosophy. It is simply an acknowledgment that Satan will be able to claim at least partial credit for
influentially (rather than irresistibly) causing us to throw the ring back in God’s face. Satan will be able to say, “I
put him up to it. I get partial credit for his rebellion. I had a hand in it.” It does not matter if the stimulus was from
our old sin nature. The devil can take considerable credit for that also. We did it with the devil’s helpful influence.
We were acting under his influence. If we can squirm out of Christ, then the devil can boast to Christ that he had a
part to play in it. Can this happen? Absolutely not!
       We cannot separate ourselves from Christ without giving the devil room to boast. By cutting off any room
for the devil to boast in Rom 8:35-39, Paul cuts off any avenue by which we can separate ourselves from Christ.
Paul does the same thing in Eph 1:13-14 and its related passages pertaining to the seal and pledge. God has pledged
to take the entire responsibility upon Himself to see to it that we bodily reach immortality and safely arrive home.
God has a life grip on us that will not let go (2Cor 5:4-8).
                                                Chasing Rabbits

says.69 Sure, Christians can be cut off in Rom 11:22 and severed from Christ in Gal 5:4. Hunting such Calminian

       Basically, I have contented myself in this present discussion of sealing with the affirmations that Paul makes
concerning the sealing. I will not pull any further OSAS rabbits out of the hat. Nonetheless, a couple of NOSAS
rabbits are worthy of further response. In contradistinction to myself, Woodcock finds that “Paul’s language in

Covenant, under which God has saved, sealed, and guaranteed the inheritance of the redeemed.”72 In Is 63:10, God
redeemed Israel, but Israel rebelled and grieved the Holy Spirit, so God became their enemy. Granted, that
possibility still exists in the NT (Jam 4:4). Yet a new dimension exists in the New Testament. In the NT, believers
are now sealed with the Spirit as a down payment. He cannot leave us. However, the Spirit could leave OT believers
(Ps 51:11).
       Corner brings up a strawman version of the OSAS as protesting: “God would never give his Spirit to later
take it away from a person.”73 Corner brushes away this caricature of OSAS with a reference to 1Sam 16:14 in
which “the Spirit of the Lord departed from Saul.”
       By way of response, let it be noted for the record that many securitists have long acknowledged that in the
OT the Spirit could come and go upon a person.74 So Corner deliberately is misrepresenting the OSAS position.
Second, being sealed by the Spirit (2Cor 1:22; Eph 1:13; 4:30), being given the Spirit as a down payment (2Cor 1:22;
2Cor 5:5; Eph 1:14), and being given the Spirit as a gift (Acts 2:38; 10:45) are NT phenomena. These gifts are
irrevocable (Rom 11:29).
       Admittedly, Corner wants to limit the promise in Rom 11:29 to a nationalistic promise so that he can exclude
it from being an individualistic guarantee.75 But he then turns around and pits Chafer against Pink as OSAS
proponents who cannot agree as to whether the context is exclusively nationalistic.76 Chafer takes the nationalistic
route while Pink takes the individualistic alternative. However, Pink is a conditional securitist!77 It is a shame,
perhaps even a scam, for Corner to pretend that conditional securitists on the Calvinistic side of the fence are
“proponents of OSAS.”78 There is no excuse for someone who is familiar with FG writings to act as if the writings
of Pink and his ilk are written from the same perspective as those who genuinely believe in eternal security. Pink,
whom Corner cites, no less than Corner himself, believes that you must persevere or perish.79 Just because a
Calvinistic book bears the title Eternal Security does not mean that it genuinely affirms eternal security. Pink is not
a securitist!
       Aside from Corner’s mismatch, Corner paints himself into a corner with his inconsistent treatments of
Rom 11:22,29. If Rom 11:22 teaches that an individual can be cut off, as Corner repeatedly insists,80 then how can
Corner turn around and claim that 11:29 is not individualistic? If he can use 11:22 individualistically, then how can
he criticize OSAS for doing so in 11:29? OSAS claims that the principle of irrevocability in 11:29 applies
individualistically as well as nationally. Corner wants to limit the irrevocability of v. 29 pluralistically to Israel.
However, the context pertaining to Israel does not commence in v. 25—the point to which Corner takes his readers.
       As every well-informed reader knows, this whole section in Romans (chs. 9-11) is dealing with Israel. The

                                                       Conclusion
       The purpose of this seal in Eph 1:13 (in identifying us as belonging to God) is to guarantee our redemption
as His possession. In identifying us as His possession, the seal guarantees us redemption. Securitists naturally find
absolute security in our identity within this passage. The seal identifies us as His. Consequently, multiple attestations
of security are provided in Eph 1:13-14: the tense of the verbs, the meanings of seal and down payment, the sphere,
the affirmation of our being God’s possession, and the fact that we do not get any glory. Aspectual, lexical, spherical,
and doxological security abound in this context. Our soteriological security is completely assured. OSAS is the only
logical conclusion: once sealed always sealed. The affirmation of OSAS in terms of once sealed always sealed
leads directly to an affirmation of OSAS in terms of once saved always saved.

       Any doubt that says we can become unsealed has been found to have no right to exist. A time comes when
you simply have to grab your doubt exegetically by the throat and deny its right to exist.82 Doing so is a matter of
life and death.
       The essence of saving faith is being persuaded that you are unconditionally ____________ (secure, sealed,
saved, justified, forgiven, adopted, regenerated) as a result of having believed in Christ for eternal life. The Scripture
gives these and multiple other ways for you to fill in the blank and thereby come to saving faith as a result. Still, we
must fill in that blank in some manner that allows us to believe that we have been unconditionally guaranteed a life
with God in Heaven as a result of having believed in Jesus. Otherwise, we will not reach Heaven. Wilkin’s warning
makes a fitting conclusion:

             In other words, until a person believes that what he has received from the Lord Jesus is permanent
        and cannot be lost, whether he understands that as eternal life, salvation, or living forever with Him in
        His kingdom, he is not yet born again. A person cannot believe in Christ alone for his eternal destiny
        and also believe that he must do something to keep it.83

                                Chapter 2.
                  Conditional and Unconditional Promises
                                                  Corner’s Position
       In a section entitled God’s Promises are Conditional, Corner states, “God’s favorable promises or solemn
threats are conditional upon our continued obedience or disobedience to him.”84 Corner’s argument uses verses such
as 1Sam 2:30 and Eze 33:13 to demonstrate that even God’s promises which appear to be unconditional are, in fact,
conditioned upon our subsequent faithfulness: “Seemingly unconditional promises do have a condition attached.”85
Strings are attached, in Corner’s perspective, to all God’s promises.
                                      Illustration 9. Promises are Conditional
                                              Conditional Promises
 “Therefore the Lord God of Israel declares, ‘I did indeed say that your house and the house of your father should
 walk before Me forever’; but now the Lord declares, ‘Far be it from Me—for those who honor Me I will honor,
 and those who despise Me will be lightly esteemed.’” (1Sam 2:30)
 “When I say to the righteous he will surely live, and he so trusts in his righteousness that he commits iniquity,
 none of his righteous deeds will be remembered; but in that same iniquity of his which he has committed he will
 die.” (Eze 33:13)

       According to the conditionalistic argument, one really has to look at the fine print, so to speak, even when
God promises eternal life and salvation as gifts. After all, Ps 31:23 stipulates: “The Lord preserves the faithful.”
Preservation is thus conditioned on perseverance. For this reason, even though an eternal benefit initially was
promised in 1Sam 2:30, this benefit could be forfeited through subsequent lack of faithfulness. Faithfulness was the
string that was attached. Similarly, when God promised life to the righteous in Eze 33:13, God could rescind on
that promise in the case of subsequent unrighteousness on the part of man (cp. Eze 18:24-26). Consequently,
Corner’s thesis is that just because God has promised eternal life as a gift to the believer, this does not mean that
God cannot revoke that benefit if the believer ceases to be a believer. This gift was not given to the believer with
no strings attached. Unspoken conditions (such as remaining a believer) are more clearly articulated in other
clarifying texts. In this manner, the Arminian seeks to divest any promise from God as being a promise of
unconditional security.
       Even though I strongly affirm unconditional security, I will not dismiss Corner’s assertion too quickly.
Granted, some within my own camp may think that Corner’s argument does not deserve a detailed response and
will be satisfied to remark that Corner overstates his case when using the text from Ezekiel to assert: “Sin can
always be the righteous man’s downfall leading to his death (spiritual and/or physical)” (bold his; italics
mine).86 As is widely admitted within the securitist camp, sin kills—physically. Many of my fellow securitists may
be content to leave it at that and reply that Corner has simply moved beyond Ezekiel’s text when Corner uses it to
postulate that sin kills the righteous spiritually. Admittedly, I too perceive Corner to be in error in deriving the
inclusion of spiritual death from this text. Notwithstanding that assessment, however, in Corner’s defense, I would
point out that there is more to Corner’s argument than meets the eye.87
       Disproving Corner’s conjecture is like fighting an invisible foe: the monster hiding under the Arminian bed.
One cannot be content to prove that the reception of the gift of life is conditioned punctiliarly on faith alone if one
wishes to help Arminians get over their fears of monsters hiding under the bed. Invisible conditions still may be
irrationally perceived as lurking elsewhere in the text in which linear conditions are brought into play to kill
believers and take away the free gift of eternal life that God has given them if they do not comply with the additional
requirements—of which they may not have been aware initially but which God has clarified elsewhere. I will not

84
   Corner, 106.

   Ibid., 108.

   Ibid., 107.

   The righteous man dying in his sins (Eze 3:20) has been addressed already. See Grieve unto Death, 21.
Page 32                                                                                    Dual Sealing and Security
disagree with the premise of Corner’s position: Invisible conditions do in fact exist. The conditions may not be
visible to surface examination. Still, I interject a limitation: Strings may be attached to some of God’s promises.
       I will not seek to comfort the fearful Arminian by responding that there are no such things as invisible
monsters. Instead, for the sake of the Arminian who fears them, I will acknowledge their existence—and kill their
irrational fears. Imagine a father trying to comfort his little boy who keeps awakening in the middle of the night
with nightmares of monsters being under the bed. As a little boy, I used to have such dreams occasionally. So allow
me to be a little sympathetic to those who might be having such nightmares at a theological level.

        Father: “Son, I turned on the light and looked under the bed. There are no monsters under the bed.”
        Son: “But daddy, maybe they were hiding in a dark corner under the bed.”
        Father: “Ok, I will look with a flashlight.”
        Son: “But daddy, maybe the monster is invisible.”
        Father: “Perhaps you are right. Tell you what I will do. I will take my saw and cut off your leg posts
                so that your bed will fall down and rest directly on the floor and crush any invisible monsters
                that might have been hiding under it. I will kill your invisible monsters.”

       Hence, despite what FG securitists perceive to be an illegitimate use of the Ezekiel text, I will allow that
Corner has a valid point in that God has made promises, both positive and negative, where the fulfillment would
seem to be certain, yet upon further investigation the fulfillment of such promises is seen to be contingent. This text
and others like it do confirm that strings indeed are attached to some of God’s promises. I can understand, therefore,
to some degree, how Corner could use such texts to advance his theory that the seemingly unconditional promise
of eternal life is really conditioned on ongoing obedience. This hypothesis is confirmed, at least theoretically in
principle, by the texts from which the phenomena of unspoken, parallel linear contingency (£  £) is found subsequently
to exist. Just ask Jonah.88
                                                     Jonah
       Jonah was given a message by God: “Yet forty days and Nineveh will be overthrown” (Jonah 3:4). The
outcome would appear to be certain. So why did Jonah jump on a ship rather than jump at the chance to proclaim a
message of destruction to a people he wanted to see overthrown? As one reads this historical account, it becomes
apparent that Jonah’s knowledge of God’s character allowed him accurately to anticipate that the seemingly
unconditional promise God made to destroy Nineveh actually was contingent on the response of the wicked living
in Nineveh. Jonah fled because he feared that instead of Nineveh being overthrown, it might turn out that the
destruction God promised would be overthrown instead. God practically had to drag Jonah to Nineveh to get him
to preach this message of doom because Jonah feared that the doom was not unconditional. Jonah wanted to see
Nineveh destroyed.
       The Ninevites, upon receiving this proclamation from Jonah, suspected that their foreboding obliteration

         state (▬) of being sealed: (£▬ ).

This latter aspect, as it pertains to Heb 10:14, is a principle source of inquiry in Fallen from Grace but Not from
Perfection. Therein, the most satisfying aspectual representation found for this verse is “He has perfected [▬▬ ]
for all time those who are sanctified [].” In other words, as related to the present discussion, those who believe
punctiliarly () are sealed permanently (▬▬ ) so that the joint aspect would be represented by a dot (denoting
punctiliar contingency) over a dot and elongated bar (denoting resulting state). Punctiliar contingency is atop the
permanent result ( ●●▬▬).
Chapter 2. Conditional and Unconditional Promises                                                              Page 33
might be reversible and consequently responded with an attitude which questioned the actual fulfillment: “Who
knows, God may turn and relent, and withdraw His burning anger so that we shall not perish?” (Jonah 3:9). “Who
knows?” Jonah knew. In fact, he explains that this is the reason he jumped on the boat rather than jumping at the
chance to preach this message: “I knew that Thou art a gracious and compassionate God, slow to anger and abundant
in lovingkindness, and one who relents concerning calamity” (Jonah 4:2). So did God tell Jonah a whale of a lie
when God told Jonah that He was going to destroy Nineveh?
        Given the bluntness with which Jonah replies to God, I suspect that if Jonah had thought that he could accuse
God morally of lying, then Jonah would not have hesitated to do so. Instead, the only charge Jonah brings against
God is that God is gracious. Jonah complains because God changed His mind regarding the calamity God had
promised. Similarly, when God promises a positive outcome, might He relent concerning the positive outcome?
This is Corner’s point. In some cases, I concede this to be the case. Nevertheless, Corner goes beyond the biblical
text by implying that all (rather than some) of God’s promises are (rather than merely may be) contingent in their
fulfillment on our ongoing faithfulness. It is to this excess that I must object.
        Not all objections to Corner’s argument are valid, however. Many (even of those who affirm unconditional
security) would, with the children of Israel, respond to Corner, if not to God Himself, that “the way of the Lord is
not right” (Eze 18:25)—if it involves the Lord not keeping His promises when the Lord does not mention any
explicit subsequent conditions, when initially giving the promise. Thus I can understand, to a degree, Corner’s
frustration with those who affirm unconditional security since, in certain cases at least, God’s promises which
appear to be unconditional may (upon closer examination) prove to be conditional despite one’s initial impression
otherwise.
        To a certain extent, one might even feel Jonah’s resentment of God’s relentment. Is it right for God to overturn
His promises? Did God not in as much as promise Jonah that He would destroy Nineveh? So why did Jonah not
accuse God of not doing the right thing and pout: “Shall not the Righteous One do right?” Abraham asked as much,
“Shall not the Judge of all the earth deal justly?” (Gen 18:25) Here, one finds the first Jew, Abraham, backing God
down from making an unconditional promise to destroy Sodom (regardless of how many righteous people may have
lived there) into making a conditional promise that was contingent on finding fifty, then forty-five, then forty, then
thirty, then twenty, then only ten righteous people within the city. From the nature of the Deity with whom they
dealt, Abraham and Jonah perceived that they could not accuse God of lying even when He changed His mind.
Abraham was counting on the fact that he could change God’s mind.
                                                  Amoral Mutability
        How then are we to follow the example of Abraham and Jonah in reconciling the fact that God can change
His mind concerning promises He has made and yet not be considered a liar? How can we harmonize the fact that
God does indeed change His mind when elsewhere we are told, “God is not a man, that he should lie, nor a son of
man, that he should change his mind. Does he speak and then not act? Does he promise and not fulfill?”
(Num 23:19; NIV) Corner believes God’s promises are conditional. This text seems to say otherwise. Yet, I cannot
stop here and claim victory over Corner’s argument because there is more to this story than meets the eye. The
affirmation that God does not lie or change His mind is well known (1Sam 15:29). At the same time, multiple
affirmations show that God, does, in fact, change His mind (Ex 32:14; Jonah 3:10).
        Men not only change their mind amorally but also immorally. God never changes His mind immorally. Thus,
one might respond analogously that even among men it is recognized that there are matters about which a man may
change his mind without being considered a liar. The same is true of God. He does not change His mind regarding
matters that would make Him a liar (Heb 6:18). Still, analogies can only be pressed so far. The analogy of comparing
God to men breaks down since the text explicitly says: “God is not a man…that He should change his mind.” I take
this to mean that God is not a man that He could change His mind in such a manner that would make Him a liar.
He never changes His mind in a manner that would be inconsistent with the justice and grace of His own nature.
Since man can lie, man is incapable of making an immutable promise. Conversely, it must be acknowledged that
God is capable of making immutable promises. As a matter of fact, that is the only kind of promise He can make
(1Kgs 8:56; 2Cor 1:20). Eternal life, in fact, is a promise (2Tim 1:1). Paul clearly regards this promise of eternal
life as immutable since it was promised by “God who cannot lie” (Tit 1:2). It is at this juncture that I spot
weaknesses, major ones, in Corner’s argument.
        First, Corner cites examples of texts which show that God can change His mind. Inexcusably, however, he
fails to consider the texts on the other side of the discussion which indicate that God cannot change His mind.
Second, this failure in Corner’s treatment leads to a failure in his logic in that he misses the logical corollary of a
Page 34                                                                                      Dual Sealing and Security
balanced deliberation that considers the text on both sides of the equation. Logically, the fact that God can change
His mind about some promises (because they may be dependent on unspoken contingencies) does not necessarily
mean that God can change His mind regarding every promise. Not every promise is dependent upon such subsequent
contingencies. Just ask Abraham.
                                                  Unilateral Promise
        When God appeared as a flaming torch that passed between the pieces of the sacrifice which were cut in two,
the Lord made a unilateral covenant with Abraham that could not be contingent upon Abraham’s subsequent
faithfulness, and the Lord clearly signified that fact to Abraham by passing between the pieces without Abraham
(Gen 15:17-18). This was the object lesson that God provided Abraham by which Abraham could rest assured that
God could not violate the promise without violating His own integrity. God can and does make promises that are
not conditioned on the recipient’s subsequent faithfulness. No invisible strings could be attached. By failing to note
(or at least by failing to admit) this variety, Corner calls the integrity of his own argument into question.89 Just ask
Haley.
                                     Alleged Discrepancies of Conditionality
       The backbone of Corner’s defense for viewing all of God’s promises as implicitly conditional is found in his
citing classic texts which reveal God changing His mind. The text that Corner uses to introduce his section entitled
God’s Promises are Conditional is Jer 18:7-10. In Haley’s book, Alleged Discrepancies of the Bible, after supplying
a two-column grouping of texts which appear to be at odds with one another in asserting that God is changeable
versus unchangeable, Haley likewise uses Jer 18:7-10 to commence the premise of his solution:

            Here is brought clearly to view the underlying condition, which, if not expressed, is implied, in
      God’s promises and threats. Whenever God, in consequence of a change in character in certain persons,
      does not execute the threats or fulfill the promises he made to them, the explanation is obvious. In every
      such case, the change is in man, rather than in God” (italics his; bold mine).90

       Very well, Corner, Haley, and I are all in agreement that a passage such as this brings out the implicit
underlying contingency that may exist in God’s promises. Whenever we see God change His mind regarding a
positive or negative condition, we can be certain that there was an underlying condition (at least for the individual
realization). The problem is that Corner has sought to press this limited concurrence to mean that there is an
underlying contingency in all of God’s promises.
       Once again, I will not dismiss Corner’s argument just because of this mistake in his logic. In Corner’s defense,
I will acknowledge that by my best estimation it seems that Haley is of the same opinion as Corner in perceiving
universal conditionality underlying all of God’s promises. But unlike Corner, Haley supplies two columns
pertaining to the discussion of such texts: one side dealing with God’s immutability, the other with His mutability.
Haley explains that precisely because God is immutable (unchangeable) in His character, He must be mutable
(changeable) in His dealings with men and His emotions toward them. Splendid, I fully agree. Further, Haley makes
a most important observation that is not mentioned by Corner: “Some of his [God’s] declarations are absolute and
unconditional; the greater part, however, including promises and threatenings, turn upon conditions either
expressed or implied.”91
       Apparently, Haley makes a distinction between God’s declarations and promises and thinks that God’s
promises are always conditional, at least by implication. If I could but affirm from Haley’s discussion that he
believes that God’s immutable character causes some of His promises to be unconditional and some to be

89
   The integrity of Corner’s position rather than his personal integrity is being called into question with this statement
concerning unconditional promises. Within the present book, Calvinists who evidently share Corner’s opinion that
all God’s promises are conditional have even brought to the witness stand. Naturally, Corner would not mention
any unconditional promises if he does not believe they exist or does not believe that the statements of God’s
immutability can be used to affirm unconditional promises. Hopefully, it will be seen that, with such a concession,
I am attacking his position, not his person.

   John Haley, An Examination of the Alleged Discrepancies of the Bible (Grand Rapids: Baker Book House, 1977),
64-65. Haley also provides an interesting two-page discussion of how God could make the change He does in
2Sam 2:30 without violating His character.
