   Ibid.
Chapter 2. Conditional and Unconditional Promises                                                             Page 35
conditional, then I would be in full accord with Haley’s discussion of immutability. Indeed, internal consistency
within Haley’s discussion would seem to require that he agree that this is indeed the case. And we have seen
passages above which link God’s promises with His immutability.
        Unfortunately, I must hesitate from citing Haley as being in full agreement with my position in that some of
Haley’s subsequent comments lead me to suspect that he believes God is mutable in all His dealings with men.
Even his internal discussion within the section of immutability gives the impression that Haley believes that a
change in man requires a corresponding change in how God deals with man. Correspondingly, in Haley’s
estimation, if a believer actually ceased to be a believer, he would lose eternal life. (See extended note in SAS). So,
as it is, I must cite Haley as being in agreement with Corner’s thesis, even though to Haley’s credit he digs deeper
than Corner in that Haley acknowledges that the conditionality is rooted in God’s mutability and even deeper still
in recognizing that God’s mutability is founded upon God’s immutability. Surprisingly, Haley evidently fails to
deduce that since some of God’s declarations are unconditional, some of God’s promises (such as the promised gift
of life) are unconditional as well. From his own analysis, Haley should have deduced that some of God’s promises
are unconditional while most are conditional. His failure to do so causes me to regard him as a Calvinistic advocate
of conditional security.
        I, therefore, call Haley to the witness stand as a hostile witness in my defense of unconditional security. In
my cross-examination of Haley’s Calvinistic concurrence with Corner’s conditional security, I find that Haley has
gathered evidence regarding God’s immutability that may serve as a foundation from which to offer my objection
to the Corner-Haley opinion that 1Sam 2:30 proves that any time there is a subsequent change in the man who has
received the promise then there may no longer be an applicability of the promise.92 They seek to turn a limited truth
into a universal truth and in doing so have overstepped the boundaries of the text.
        It comes as no surprise when Calvinists join their fellow conditional securitists in the Arminian camp in
affirming conditional security from a text such as 1Sam 2:30: “Therefore the Lord God of Israel declares, ‘I did
indeed say that your house and the house of your father should walk before Me forever’; but now the Lord declares,
‘Far be it from Me—for those who honor Me I will honor, and those who despise Me will be lightly esteemed.’” In
commenting on this verse, Smith asks, “Can then a promise of God be withdrawn?” His answer is an unqualified,
“Yes, assuredly.” Honoring God is “one of these conditions essential on man’s part to secure the fulfillment of
God’s promises.”93 In short, if you want security, you will have to secure it by honoring God. One would deduce
from such unqualified statements that to secure the fulfillment of God’s promises regarding eternal life one must
honor God throughout one’s life. This conditional security is the joint premise of both Arminianism and Calvinism.
                                                     OSAS Position
        The OSAS thesis, in contrast, is that some of God’s immutable declarations are tied irreversibly to His
character so as to rule out the possibility of linear contingency. To be sure, God, who is faithful and just (Dt 15:4),
may nevertheless spurn Israel whom He has begotten as a son (Dt 32:18-19). Also, God may divorce Israel whom
He has married as a wife (Jer 3:8). Further, OSAS may concede that these texts typologically reveal how God may
deal with present-day believers. Granted, from the joint conditional perspective of both Arminianism and
Calminianism, a text like 2Tim 2:13, which is addressed to NT believers, is taken to mean that “if we are faithless,
then God remains faithful” to His promises to punish us with soteriological severity (cf. Hendriksen, NTC). In
complete contrast, OSAS believes that 2Tim 2:13 affirms unconditional soteriological security: Even if we are not
faithful in keeping His word, God is faithful to keep His word by keeping us.
        Even if OSAS were to agree with soteriological conditionalists and conclude that 2Tim 2:13 means that God
is faithful to judge us, OSAS would respond that this judgment is only misthological in scope. God will judge
unfaithful believers at the Judgment Seat of Christ (JSC). God’s despising us as His sons, divorcing us as His wife,
or being faithful to His promises to punish us when we deny Him are all, at the most, pictures of conditional security
applied exclusively at the misthological level. Not all sons will inherit the kingdom. Not all those betrothed to the
Lord will be found worthy to become His Bride.

92
   The logical deduction from Haley’s continued comments on p. 65 and from his section entitled final perseverance
(pp. 169ff) is that Haley is a Calvinist who affirms conditional security. After all, the typical Calvinistic stance
affirms both. In affirming OSAS and POTS, Calvinists offset eternal security with conditional security. The bottom
line is that Haley, like Corner, advocates conditional security.
93
   R. Payne Smith, 1 Samuel, Pulpit Commentary, eds. H. D. M. Spence and Joseph S. Exell (McLean, Virginia:
MacDonald Publishing Company, n.d.), 55-56.
Page 36                                                                                     Dual Sealing and Security
       At the individual and misthological level, those who bed with the devil will be given a certificate of divorce
so that they do not become the Bride of Christ. (A divorce was necessary to break a Jewish betrothal.) Paul is not
afraid of invisible monsters under the bed. These misthological monsters are real, and they are visible for all who
have eyes to see:
      2
       For I am jealous for you with a godly jealousy; for I betrothed you to one husband, that to Christ I might
      present you as a pure virgin. 3 But I am afraid, lest as the serpent deceived Eve by his craftiness, your minds
      should be led astray from the simplicity and purity of devotion to Christ. (2Cor 11:2-3)

       Paul is afraid, and rightly so, that he might not be able to present these Corinthian believers as pure virgins
to the Lord at the JSC. Likewise, in dealing with the believers at Galatia, he expresses similar fears: “I fear for you,
that perhaps I have labored over you in vain” (Gal 4:11). He does not question their regeneration,94 but he does
question whether they will qualify for the reward of the bridal inheritance. To inherit the kingdom as the Bride of
Christ (i.e., as a co-ruler with Christ), one must be a faithful believer in Christ. Being loved as the Bride of Christ
necessarily requires faithfulness to Christ.
       At the soteriological level, however, the Lord still loves us and we remain His own even if we are unfaithful.
One reason OSAS affirms that we remain His own is because of the Lord’s promise and prayer to unconditionally
keep us (Jn 10:28-29; 17:11). But such matters aside, for the purpose of the present discussion, the focus will be on
the classic problem posed by Haley between God’s immutability and mutability and the impact this has on Corner’s
premise of universal changeability regarding promises.
                                         Errors by Conditionalists
      To illustrate a columnar comparison of conditional versus unconditional promises, I will just consider a
contrast within 1Samuel and Ezekiel themselves, since they are the contextual home to Corner’s primary texts:
1Sam 2:30 and Eze 33:13.

      As already shown, Haley failed to acknowledge that the immutability of God’s character extends directly to
God’s promises. And Corner has been less than forthcoming about the fact that there are two sides to the contingency
expressed in God’s promises. In the biblical books to which Corner makes his strongest appeals to demonstrate that
God’s promises are conditional, additional passages indicate that God’s promises are also unconditional. Therefore,
Corner’s section would have been better entitled Some of God’s Promises are Conditional. The omission of his
admission (that only some of God’s promises are conditional) raises severe questions regarding the integrity of his
argument since Corner’s premise seems to be that all of God’s promises are conditional. Naturally, it is much easier
for Corner to argue that God’s promise of eternal life is conditional if there is no such thing as an unconditional
promise! Once, however, it is acknowledged that some of God’s promises are unconditional, the premise of Corner’s
argument is falsified, and his conclusion that the believer’s security must be conditional is nullified.95
94
  See Fallen from Grace but Not from Perfection.
95
  Just because some seemingly unconditional promises really are conditional does not necessarily imply that all
seemingly unconditional promises must be conditional. Some ≠ all. Not recognizing this basic fact resulted in
Chapter 2. Conditional and Unconditional Promises                                                              Page 37
       Because a balanced discussion of the relevant facts will take into account the fact that God can and does
make unconditional promises, the resulting question that must be asked is, “When God promises a believer eternal
life as a free gift, is God conditioning the possession of eternal life on the believer’s continued faith and/or
faithfulness?” In failing to acknowledge the possibility of unconditional promises, Corner has seriously impaired
his ability to deal adequately with the possibility that unconditional securitists may be categorically correct in
assigning soteriology to the realm of unconditionality for those who have become believers—regardless of whether
they remain believers.
                                             Errors by Unconditionalists
        In defending eternal security, Geisler devotes a paragraph to defending the premise that “salvation is an
unconditional promise” and a joint paragraph to affirming that “salvation is an irrevocable gift.” To his credit, unlike
the conditional securitists in the Calvinistic and Arminian camps, Geisler cites 2Tim 2:13 in defense: “God can
never take back the gift of salvation. He is bound by his own unconditional promise to be faithful, even if we are
faithless, for he cannot deny himself (2Tim. 2:13).”96 I also note appreciatively that in an expanded discussion
Geisler affirms that God’s promise of the gift of eternal life is an irrevocable, unconditional promise and clearly
grounds this affirmation in God’s immutability: “When he makes an unconditional promise he never fails to keep
it (cf. Gen 12:1-3; Heb 6:16-18)….The only thing that makes God morally bound to keep his word is his
unchangeable nature.”97 Truly, God’s immutability makes His gifts unconditionally irrevocable.
        Unfortunately, however, just as Corner overstates the case for unconditional security by assuming that all
God’s promises are conditional since some of His promises are conditional, Geisler mistakenly seems to overstate
the case for eternal security in assuming that just because God makes an immutable promise, the promise must be
unconditional.
        For example, Geisler wrongly cites Heb 6:17-18 as an example of an unconditional promise of eternal
security: “In the same way God, desiring even more to show to the heirs of the promise the unchangeableness of
His purpose, interposed with an oath, in order that by two unchangeable things, in which it is impossible for God
to lie, we may have strong encouragement, we who have fled for refuge in laying hold of the hope set before us.”
But this text is only an irrevocable promise to believers who meet the condition of ongoing faith and faithfulness.
        Rather than being a soteriological promise, as Geisler mistakenly assumes, this text is a misthological
promise. Lest it be thought that I am being too hard on my fellow securitists, I will point out that even some of my
fellow misthologists, such as Dillow, have fallen into the trap of taking the same short cut in defending eternal
security by soteriologically appealing to this text from Hebrews.98 However, this misthological promise does not
apply unconditionally to believers until they have completed the course of their lives and have been found worthy
of the reward. This text, like Heb 10:36, is saying that believers must endure in order to receive what was promised:
“For you have need of endurance, so that when you have done the will of God, you may receive what was promised.”
The question is, “What was promised—a gift or a reward?” A study of the word promise in the book of Hebrews
readily confirms that the promise of a reward is in view. This is not to say, as will be seen below, that a text like
Heb 6:17-18 has no value in affirming unconditional security, only one cannot take a shortcut in exegesis and make
a jump in logic to use this verse directly to support this position. Misthology has no need of missing links in its
argument.
        Similarly, Peter’s affirmation that a believer’s inheritance is “imperishable and undefiled and will not fade
away, reserved in heaven for you” (1Pet 1:4) is yet another text to which some of my well-meaning fellow

Corner implementing faulty logic and making a catastrophic, categorical failure to acknowledge that some of God’s
promises are unconditional. As a result, the underlying integrity of his entire argument is nullified at its foundation.
96
   Geisler, Security, 82.

   BECA, s.v. “Essentialism, Divine.” Although I take similar views as Geisler regarding immutability, I differ from

mind. Similarly, I believe that immutability does not result in impassibility in that God’s emotions can change in
response to man (cp. Murphree, Paradoxes, 106-107).

   In his chapter on eternal security, Dillow correctly grounds his argument in the irrevocability of the gift in

securitists, such as Barker, erroneously have appealed as teaching unconditional security: “It is God who has
reserved it for us. Did He make a mistake in reserving it for us?”99 Of course not. God has not made a mistake in
making the reservation. Yet Barker has made a mistake in thinking that just because a potential reward is eternal
that it must be unconditional in its final realization. The reward is eternal; the reservation is not.100 Both this text as
well as Heb 6:17-18 are talking about a misthological hope which only becomes an unconditional certainty when
one has met the linear contingency whose terminus is to be found at the end of one’s life (●▬      £● ). In other words,
                                                                                                     ▬
believers must endure to the end of their lives in order to come into the realized possession of this misthological
hope. Careful misthologists cannot accept the well-intentioned attempts of either fellow securitists or fellow
misthologists, such as Geisler, Barker, and (to a limited extent) Dillow, in erroneously using such texts to affirm
eternal security. I will not accept faulty evidence in support of my thesis, nor need I do so.
                                              Rewards are Conditional
        The problem, then, is that certain securitists and misthologists have failed to perceive that some of the texts
that they are citing in defense of unconditional security are actually dealing with the conditional nature of eternal
rewards. Corner has failed to note that the texts he poses in defense of underlying conditionality only confirm the
conditional nature of rewards. God’s intention was that the realization of the perpetual priesthood should be an
eternal reward for their conduct (Num 25:13; cp. Ex 29:9). God clarifies in 1Sam 2:30, however, that the realization
of this reward is contingent on ongoing faithfulness in that He would honor those who honor Him and despise those
who despise Him. Undoubtedly, the language of rewards rather than that of a gift is being implemented. Being
honored by God is conditioned on serving God and successfully enduring trials (Jn 12:26; 1Pet 1:7). Obviously, a
reward motif based on service is in view since rewards are conditioned on works. In conditioning the gift of eternal
life on the same thing as a reward, Corner is attempting to condition the gift on works and thereby turn the gift into
a reward.
                                                      Missing Links
       The fact that a reward may be contingent on linear contingency is not exactly ground-breaking news. It does
not take a rocket scientist to figure out that rewards are earned. Lay people can figure that out, even if some scholars
cannot. On the other hand, it would take a long chain of evolution in one’s theological thought process to
successfully turn (A) the imperishable gift of eternal life (which a believer already possesses) into (B) a reward
(which the believer must struggle to keep and endure to obtain). Too many missing links and categorical gaps exist
in Corner’s soteriological phylogeny to think that he has successfully turned a gift into a reward.
       We do not earn a gift. Just because a gift and a reward share the similar characteristic of being a promise
does not mean that they can be dug out of the text and arranged in a theological, evolutionary tree so as to link one
inseparably to the other with a line of linear contingency. A gift is a different kind of theological animal than is a
reward. They cannot be mated to produce the hybrid that Corner has imaged: a linear contingent gift. Securitists,
therefore, may liken Corner’s futile exegetical attempts to turn a gift into a reward (with a biased collection of
certain texts) to the failed efforts of evolutionists to make a monkey out of man by appealing to the fossil record.
99
   Harold Barker, Secure Forever (Neptune: Loizeaux Brothers, 1974), 52.
100
    My statement that the reservation in 1Pet 1:4 is not eternal might be countered by a securitist who insists on
making 1Pet 1:4 a soteriological text and who claims that just as the believer’s inheritance is reserved in Heaven,
so the unbeliever’s punishment in the darkness is reserved (2Pet 2:17), and this reservation is forever (Jude 1:13).
Thus, contrary to my statement, it might be claimed that the reservation is eternal after all. My response would be
that the realization of the reservation is contingent; therefore, the reservation itself is not eternal for those who have
not yet come into the realization. Unbelievers, in other words, are not unconditionally sent to Hell. Their going to
Hell is linearly contingent upon their remaining unbelievers. Likewise, the believers’ misthological inheritance is
contingent upon their remaining believers and remaining faithful. Once they reach Heaven, their reward is no longer
contingent. Conversely, once the lost reach hell, their punishment is no longer linearly contingent upon their
remaining unbelievers. The rich man may have become a believer when he saw Lazarus in glory, but it was too late.
The rich man’s punishment was no longer contingent; it was eternal. In similar fashion, I do not perceive Lazarus’
postmortem glory to still be contingent; it is eternal. (Just because one day every knee will bow and every tongue
confess does not mean that every knee and every tongue will be in Heaven.) Therefore, in saying that the reservation
is not eternal, I mean that it is not yet eternal for those who have not realized that reservation. Neither the reservation
for the righteous in 1Pet 1:4 nor for the unrighteous in Jude 1:13 is reserved unconditionally for those who have not
yet reached their postmortem destination.
Chapter 2. Conditional and Unconditional Promises                                                                Page 39
       Just as The Fossils Still Say NO!, to quote the subtitle of Gish’s classic rebuttal of evolution, a systematic
examination of the biblical texts still responds unyieldingly to Corner’s endeavor to classify all of God’s promises
as being conditioned on ongoing performance. By picturing God as giving man the promise of eternal life as a gift
and then taking it back because of man’s performance, Corner is making God out to be a liar. Placing the conditional
label upon such promises turns out to be libel.
                                                      Surely Live
        What about the fact that God promises the righteous that they will surely live and the unrighteous that they
will surely die and then clarifies that this promise is to be understood conditionally (Eze 18:13-14)? Do such texts
(which Corner digs up from only one side of the theological column to put on display as examples of seemingly
unconditional promises which are really conditional) provide the missing links Corner needs to prove that all of
God’s promises are in reality conditional? Not at all.
        These seemingly-unconditional-but-really-conditional promises are not unconditional promises of a gift that
were subsequently turned into a conditional promise of reward through a process of theological macroevolution
over a long period of prophetic time. Instead, these texts, like 1Sam 2:30, were promises of reward all along, which,
when traced through their development in Scripture and examined under the lens of progressive revelation, are seen
to be mere examples of misthological microevolution in which their underlying contingency becomes more
pronounced in some passages than others.
        Evolutionists are not the only ones who confuse microevolution with macroevolution. Conditional securitists
do so also. Corner has seen the microevolution in which the misthological nature of some linear conditional
promises is more clearly brought out subsequently, and he, consequently, has concluded that one may assume
macroevolution in which all promises are regarded as evolving from the same misthological nature. As to passages
such as Eze 33:13, the first thing to note is that God’s promise that the righteous will surely live is not to be
misconstrued to mean that they will unconditionally live, as God Himself clarifies in the passage itself.
        Second, the passage is not talking about either imputational righteousness or soteriological life. Rather,
practical righteousness and the reward of temporal righteousness are in view. The passage begins with repeated
warnings of the sword taking their lives if they are wicked. Yet those who warn the wicked of their impending
deaths will save their own lives (literally, save their souls, Eze 33:9). But this terminology (i.e., the saving of a
soul) is always used temporally or misthologically in the Bible, never soteriologically.
        Those who do not warn the wicked will, like the wicked, die. The people complain that they are rotting away
(obviously rotting on Earth, not in Hell) and are fearful that they will not live (Eze 33:10). Again, they are concerned
about being killed, about staying alive on planet Earth, in a dog-eat-dog world where it often seems that only the
fittest survive. Theological speculation about ontological extinction is the last thing on their minds. They are not
trying to stay alive on the branch of some imaginary evolutional soteric tree which is trying to turn their gift of life
into a reward. They simply are trying to survive, not only individually but corporately, as the house of Israel
(Eze 33:11). The survival of the house of Israel is also a concern in the background passage (Eze 18:31), where the
issue of life and death is clarified as the wicked person being put to death as a form of capital punishment rather
than a form of soteriological demise.
        Third, God has several reasons to be keenly interested in the survival of Israel as a nation. The Lord has too
many unfulfilled, unconditional promises invested in the nation of Israel to allow it to slip away quietly into the
night never to be heard from again within the pages of history. The OT is a record of Jewish history. At the time in
which Ezekiel was writing, the promised Messiah was yet to come from them and for them. And the restoration of
the Davidic kingdom still awaits a future time. The miraculous restoration of the nation of Israel in our time is an
event unparalleled in history. This modern miracle stands as a testimony to the fact that God knows how to keep a
promise, even when that promise is made to a people who are faithless. The unconditional nature of God’s promise
to the nation of Israel is further seen in that the nation of Israel was no longer intact in order to meet any linear
contingency before being restored as a nation. As to OT typology, unconditional security is pictured in how God
treats Israel. Amidst the misthological insecurity, pictures of unconditional security are linked to God’s
immutability (Mal 3:6).
                                                Typological Syllogisms
       Still, someone will respond in Corner’s defense that since the promise of life was made to the righteous who
stays righteous that the precedence is established, at least in principle, that the believer must remain a believer in
order to continue to have life as a believer. After all, Israel is an OT picture of a born-again believer. In other words,
Arminians will agree that God promises eternal life to a believer, but they will then stipulate that in order to keep
Page 40                                                                                      Dual Sealing and Security
eternal life one must remain a believer. Supposedly, the promises no longer apply if one ceases to be a believer.
The above passages from Ezekiel might be sought for typological support:

       1. The promise is that the righteous will surely live.
       2. If the righteous becomes unrighteous, he will surely die.
       3. Therefore, the promise of living is conditioned on remaining righteous.

So typologically, from an Arminian point of view, this might be thought to justify the following parallelism:

       1. The believer is promised eternal life.
       2. If the believer becomes an unbeliever, the promise of life no longer applies to him.
       3. Therefore, retaining regeneration is conditioned on remaining a believer.

       From this Arminian perspective, just as the righteous could lose their life in the OT if they did not remain
righteous, so NT believers can lose eternal life if they do not remain believers. The survival of the fittest becomes
the survival of the faithful in this theological migration (cp. Ps 31:23). Having already examined and refuted this
type of argument from multiple vantage points within the course of this present discussion and elsewhere, it will
suffice here to mention briefly a few objections to augment the present defense of unconditional security, which
regards it as inconsistent to think that one must remain a believer in order to retain the free gift of eternal life.
                                             Believer ≠ Ongoing Believer
       First, linear contingency necessarily implies, if not outright demands, that the benefit bestowed be regarded
as a reward rather than a gift. Thus, to base the possession of the gift of eternal life on linear contingency is to turn
this gift into a reward.101 To apply a misthological syllogism indiscriminately to a gift can be like trying to put your
right foot into your left shoe. It is not a natural selection. Second, the context in Ezekiel undoubtedly is talking
about the reward of life. The NT is clear that the gift of life is not conditioned on works and thus not a reward. The
typology is in disparity. Third, just because some believers no longer believe does not mean that they are no longer
considered believers. They may remain classified as believers substantively102 and trichotomously103 even though
they are no longer believers functionally.104 Fourth, there are promises in which the biblical writer has made it clear
that underlying (moral) contingencies do not exist.105 Fifth, there are conditional promises of misthological life in
the NT that would make much better typological parallels to these linear conditional texts than trying to cross-match
misthological life with soteriological life. This crossmatch turns out to be nothing but a mismatch. For example, if
one desires a typological parallel between conditional promises regarding life, let it be found in the natural
misthological correspondence between the OT and NT regarding the salvation of the soul, particularly since this is
what Ezekiel is talking about. Sixth, misthology fully acknowledges that perseverance results in salvation. But this
lends no support to Corner’s position because his argument is that perseverance results in believer’s soteriological
salvation. Misthology insists that the believer’s soteriological salvation is unconditional; it is the believer’s
misthological salvation that is conditional.
                                          Immutability and Conditionality
Fill in the Blank
        Having briefly reviewed some of my exegetical and theological objections to the implementation of such

    For example, believers are promised unconditionally that they will be delivered from tribulational wrath and

Chapter 2. Conditional and Unconditional Promises                                                                Page 41
passages picturing God’s mutability to defend unconditional security (and its attendant premise that all of God’s
promises are conditional), I will now further address the underlying philosophical assumptions by asking the
question, “Does the immutability of God’s character make His mutability in dealing with man ______________
(necessary, possible, or impossible)?” This blank is interjected in order to allow the reader a moment to pause and
mentally fill in the blank. The answer is not as easy as one might think initially and certainly not as easy as Corner’s
arguments would imply.
Necessary
        When Adam sinned, it changed how God dealt with him because Adam became something that he was not
before—a sinner. A change in God’s dealings with Adam was necessary. God expelled Adam from the garden.
Obviously, we should fill in the blank with the word necessary, even if doing so supports Corner’s contention that
when a believer becomes an unbeliever it is necessary to conclude that God withdraws eternal life from such
disbeliever. If this were the end of the story, then it might indeed be possible to speculate that God necessarily
withdraws eternal life from believers who cease to believe and that there is an underlying conditionality to all God’s
promises. As long as man has the ability to change, then man must have the ability to nullify God’s promises—if
God’s promises must correspondingly change in response to any and every response by man. If God does not have
the ability to make an immutable promise to a mutable man, then immutability becomes an impossibility for our
immutable God. The astute reader, however, will smell a rat somewhere in this limited perspective of the Corner-
Haley reconstruction.
        First of all, it does not make sense to think that everything man does requires that God change how He deals
with man. Your ordering mustard on your hamburger does not mean that God must treat you differently. An amoral
human action would not necessarily invoke a divine reaction. For that matter, one need not jump to the conclusion
that each immoral action by man results in a negative reaction by God. For one thing, when man sinned, God atoned.
This was a very positive reaction by God to a very negative action by man. Rather than merely responding as man’s
condemner, God responded as man’s redeemer. Secondly, in God’s positive reaction, God has taken counteraction
to make it possible for Him not to respond negatively to man as a sinner just because man sins. By a declaration of
justification, the Lord has found a permanent solution to sin in which He has, as expressed in the song Glorious
Day, “justified freely forever” those who ever believe (cf. Heb 10:10,14). Need we be reminded that this justification
is a gift (Rom 3:24)? This type of permanency rules out linear contingency since the benefit already has been
bestowed freely and eternally. God has “perfected for all time” those who have at any time been justified freely
forever.106
        In order to convey the permanency of regeneration, Peter stresses that it is sourced in the immutability
(imperishability) of God’s word (1Pet 1:23). In doing so, Peter ontologically links the immutability of God with the
new nature of man in such a way as to require that one acknowledge that man’s new nature is ontologically
immutable so that regeneration is irrevocable. It is not necessary that God withdraws eternal life from us each time
we sin. One bite killed Adam. One bite does not kill us because God has given us a tree of life, in the form of the
old rugged cross, that allows us to live forever. It is not necessary that God pronounces us sinners for each sin
because in the act of justification God has taken care of all our sins. Consequently, it is biblically and logically
unnecessary for God to change His dealings with us or His emotions toward us each time we sin. Far to the contrary,
God has been able to convey His attribute of immutability to humanity by linking His immutable character directly
to certain of His promises.
Impossible
       God cannot change His dealings with believers as believers because they cannot become unregenerate or
unjustified. Believers may cease to believe functionally, but they cannot cease legally and ontologically to be
considered believers in terms of the benefits of their justification and regeneration which were conveyed irrevocably
to them when they first believed. Stated more concisely, believers may no longer be believers functionally, but they
certainly are believers legally and ontologically. Just as one who has committed murder and who has been sentenced
to death does not have to continue to commit murder in order to be considered a murderer or to remain under the
sentence of death, so believers do not functionally have to continue to believe in order to be considered believers
or to remain in possession of eternal life. Just as it is impossible for God to lie, it is impossible for believers to die
(Jn 11:26). Filling in the blank with the word impossible is certainly possible.107 No philosophical reason prevents

106
      See Fallen from Grace but Not from Perfection for extended discussion of these two texts from Hebrews.
107
      See Fill in the Blank, 40.
Page 42                                                                                     Dual Sealing and Security
the blank from being filled in differently, depending on various biblical conditions. The question expressed by the
blank, in fact, poses a false trichotomy between the possibilities.
        Sometimes God may or may not choose to respond differently to man when man sins. It is not necessary that
the Lord disciplines His children for everything they do wrong. (For example, it is not necessary that a mother
discipline her little girl each time her daughter does something wrong. Love makes it possible, at times, to overlook
the offense of another.) On the other hand, sometimes God necessarily may have to respond differently to man when
man sins. There are some things that God in His righteousness cannot overlook. (Likewise, there are things that
even a loving mother simply cannot overlook.) Then again, sometimes God cannot respond differently to man when
man sins. God will still regard His child as His child. (A loving mother is still going to regard her sinful daughter
as her daughter.)
        The writer of Hebrews certainly found no difficulty in tying the immutability of God’s character together
with God’s promises in such a way as to convey a sense of security to humanity (Heb 6:17-19). Now to be sure, the
writer of Hebrews does so in this text with a discussion pertaining to linear contingency regarding rewards. But if
he can derive a sense of security (even when dealing with forfeitable rewards) from immutable misthological
promises, then how can we derive less than soteriological security (regarding a nonforfeitable gift of eternal life)
from God’s soteriological promises? Do not both sets of promises flow from the same source—from God’s
immutability? Of course, they do. For insecuritists (such as Corner) to think that God can only make linear
conditional promises and for securitists (such as Geisler) to imply that God can only make punctiliar conditional
promises are shortsighted both biblically and philosophically. Surely, it is within the realm of God’s creative ability
to make both punctiliar and linear conditional promises.
        From the perspective of the Lord’s mutability in dealing with men, one is not surprised to hear, “The Lord
said to Solomon, ‘Because you have done this, and you have not kept My covenant and My statutes, which I have
commanded you, I will surely tear the kingdom from you, and will give it to your servant’” (1 Kings 11:11). Okay,
God is going to take the kingdom away from Solomon. God is changing His dealings with Solomon. No problem.
Realization of rulership is a reward. Solomon will certainly miss out on the eternal rewards he would have had
otherwise (cf. 1Chron 28:9). But the surprise comes in the next verse: “Nevertheless I will not do it in your days for
the sake of your father David, but I will tear it out of the hand of your son” (1Kings 11:12). What happened to God’s
temporal threats in which He promises to punish the righteous if they become wicked? Did they evaporate into a
hypothetical vapor? No. Solomon actually has sinned and deserves the death sentence. Below this surface
conditionality, however, there is an underlying unconditionality (not conditionality), which comes bubbling to the
top.
        For the sake of another, God does not give Solomon the punishment he deserves. God refuses to change His
temporal dealings with Solomon because of His love for David. This provides an OT picture of unconditional
security. For Jesus’ sake, God does not deal with believers as with lost sinners but as with sinful saints. He will
punish us for our sins, but there is a limit to how far He will take that punishment. At the very least, this text is an
OT confirmation that God does not necessarily, in every situation, have to change His dealings with man in the
conditional manner prescribed even when man changes in his dealings with God. Grounds for finding an underlying
bedrock of unconditional love in His treatment of a wayward person are possible because of His love for another
faithful person who loves that wayward person. Just as there is to be a depth to our love that allows us to love the
children of God unconditionally simply because we love their Father, there is an unfathomable depth to God’s love
that allows Him to love us just because His Son does.
        Parents may find that, in going through the drudgery of picking up some of their child’s toys or clothes, they
feel an emotional attachment to that toy or piece of clothing because of the love they have for their child. When my
children were young, I would sometimes pick up their toys and feel an emotional attachment to the toy because of
my emotional attachment to my children.
        Suppose the unthinkable were to happen, and parents who had been picking up their child’s toys suddenly
find out that their child was hit by a car and died on the way home from school. If their child were to die, the parents
might find a measure of solace in lying in the child’s bed and looking at the child’s closet full of clothes and toys.
Paternally, one would expect them to have tears streaming down their faces after such a tragic loss and feel a touch
of their child’s presence conveyed to them through their child’s belongings. Indeed, it may take a considerable
amount of time before the parents would be able to bring themselves emotionally to the point of letting go of those
objects.
        If parents can have such a sense of unconditional attachment to old, ragged, childish toys and to tattered
clothing because of their love for their children, then who is Haley or Corner to say that just because we change,
Chapter 2. Conditional and Unconditional Promises                                                             Page 43
God’s feelings toward us must change. There is nothing that such toys or pieces of clothing could do that would
cause the parents of the departed child to love the toys or clothing any more or any less. A toy that honks or beeps,
under those circumstances, would not be loved because it could honk or beep. A torn pair of jeans would not be
loved less because of its defect.
       Although in DLF I certainly affirm misthological love in that I recognize that there are degrees and
expressions of intimacy with God that are conditioned on how we, as children, respond to His love, I also believe
that there are depths to His love for us as His children which are unconditional and undisturbed by the change in
surface conditions. At those depths, God could not love us more, and He will not love us less.
       My disappointment with Haley’s articulation is not with his discussion of immutability per se but with the
limitations his own understanding of immutability has on his apprehension of eternal security. Thiessen, in contrast,
takes much the same position as Haley concerning immutability: “It is necessary for an unchangeable God to change
in his dealings with changing men in order to remain unchanging in his character and purposes.” Yet to Thiessen’s
credit, he takes one small, yet crucial, step further than Haley in clearly affirming that God “cannot change with
regard to…his promises (1 Kings 8:56; 2 Cor. 1:20).”108 Thiessen even does a commendable job of subsequently
linking God’s immutability directly to eternal security. These immutable promises need not be limited to linear
contingencies but may be applied to punctiliar contingencies as well. Geisler assumed as much in using these classic
texts for immutability to defend eternal security.109
       Despite the fact that there are statements in Thiessen’s chapter on perseverance with which I would disagree,
his quote of Boettner’s Reformed Doctrine is surprisingly right on target: “The nature of the change which occurs
in regeneration is a sufficient guarantee that the life imparted shall be permanent.”110 Sometimes even Calvinists
break rank and correctly acknowledge the obvious implications of a text, even though doing so puts them at odds
with their Calvinistic peers. Boettner may be commended on this occasion for proving to be an exception to the rule
that Calvinists tend to condition retention of regeneration on perseverance. And to Thiessen’s credit he, like Geisler,
quotes Rom 11:29 in support of eternal security. Stated syllogistically in terms of this verse, my thesis is simple:

      1. The promised gift from God of eternal life is already possessed by the believer.
      2. “The gifts…of God are irrevocable” (Rom 11:29).
      3. The promised gift of eternal life is a gift which the person who has believed already possesses irrevocably
         and is, therefore, no longer conditional.

       Surprisingly, support for my defense of unconditionality is not limited to positive specimens. There are also
examples of God reaching a point where He says, in effect, “Enough is enough.” Moses flatly stated as much: “The
Lord was angry with me on your account, and would not listen to me; and the Lord said to me, 'Enough! Speak to
Me no more of this matter’” (Dt 3:26). When God swore in His wrath that the children of Israel would not enter His
rest (Heb 3:11; 4:3), the Lord was not speaking hypothetically. The Lord was making a declaration that actually
was immutable and unconditional. There was nothing the children of Israel could do to reverse it. They repented.
But it was too late to enter that land and take possession of it or experience a restful inheritance in it (Num 14:39-
40; Dt 1:41-46). Unlike the occasion in which the people of Nineveh repented and thus changed God’s mind, it was
impossible for these blood-bought Jews to enter the rest even though they repented. Even so, it may be impossible
for God’s children today to obtain that promised reward even if they repent (Heb 6:6). God’s immutable nature
enables Him to make immutable promises and threats which cannot be overthrown by subsequent obedience or
108
    Henry Clarence Thiessen, Lectures in Systematic Theology, revised by Vernon D. Doerksen (Grand Rapids:
Eerdmans Publishing Company, 1979)¸ 83. Also see Murphree, 104.
109
    Geisler’s argument that God makes unconditional promises of eternal security are, of course, meant to be taken
as affirming that once a person meets the punctiliar requirement of becoming a believer, no contingencies remain.
The affirmation of unconditional promises is not a denial that the promises were ever conditional, but only that
these promises do not remain conditional for the person who has met the qualification. These promises are
unconditional for the person who has now fulfilled the condition. Unconditional security for the person who has
become a believer is an argument that the promises are now unconditional for the person who has met the punctiliar
condition of trusting in Christ alone for eternal life. In the case of eternal security, the promises are now
unconditional for the person who has met the punctiliar condition of trusting in Christ alone for eternal life, which
logically results in unconditional security.
110
    Ibid., 296.
Page 44                                                                                   Dual Sealing and Security
disobedience.
        God irrevocably has promised to remain unchanging in certain aspects toward changing man even if man
makes certain changes. By the sheer nature of the case, in such cases man cannot make subsequent changes that
will undo the results of his earlier changes if God already has responded irrevocably to man’s earlier changes.
        If a person takes the mark of the beast, for instance, God irrevocably has committed Himself to send that
person to Hell.111 Counter wise, if a person is sealed with the Spirit, God irrevocably has committed Himself to save
that person from Hell. Regardless of what change or changes a person may make after having taken that mark or
seal, that person’s fate is sealed unconditionally by the punctiliar change that he or she made which resulted in that
mark or seal. Put differently, securitists can glean arguments in favor of eternal security even from God’s
unconditional threats because these threats serve to remind us that our immutable God can make unconditional
promises. The fact that the Lord can be steadfast in carrying out certain threats, regardless of our subsequent change
in performance, gives us reason to believe that He can be just as steadfast in carrying out certain positive promises,
regardless of our subsequent change in performance. In such cases, we cannot change the outcome regardless of
what subsequent changes we make. The outcome already has been determined unconditionally from the moment
God responded irrevocably.
Possible
       Briefly, I will survey my primary points and fill in one more blank. God’s immutable character makes it
possible for Him to make both conditional and unconditional promises. (This is yet one more way we can fill in the
above blank.112) God can communicate His immutability to us with propositional statements and convey that
immutability to us with immutable promises. Not only is this a philosophical possibility, but it is also a biblical
reality that the Lord has, in fact, done so.

      1. God’s immutable character makes immutable promises possible.
      2. Immutable promises are not only possible, but they are also actual.
      3. One purpose of such immutable promises is to give absolute assurance.
      4. Immutable promises rule out a remaining contingency for those who have fulfilled the condition. God
         cannot change the condition subsequently.
             a. Although some immutable promises are based on linear contingency, there is no logical reason to
                 believe that all immutable promises must be based on linear contingency.
             b. God’s gifts are conditioned on punctiliar contingency.
                       i. The gift of justification requires punctiliar contingency.
                      ii. The gift of regeneration requires punctiliar contingency.
                     iii. Since these gifts are bestowed punctiliarly and permanently, they are no longer contingent
                          once they are bestowed. They are unconditional to the one who has received them.
             c. God’s rewards may be conditioned on linear contingency, but even these rewards may cease to be
                 conditioned on ongoing contingency once the condition has been met. Some promised rewards may
                 become unconditional upon the realization of that reward.
             d. One purpose of immutable unconditional promises (whether they are regarding gifts or rewards) is
                 to give absolute assurance of their irrevocable fulfillment.
      5. Just because promised rewards may have an underlying contingency of works does not prove that a
         promised gift may be conditioned on works.
      6. God is not necessarily mutable in all His dealings with man.
             a. Not every change in man necessarily causes a change in how God deals with man.
                       i. Not every human action requires a corresponding divine reaction.
                      ii. God already may have taken countermeasures to make certain divine responses
                          unnecessary, even impossible, when man sins.
                     iii. Just because a believer ceases to believe does not mean that such a person must necessarily
                          no longer be regarded as a believer.

      See “Grace Camp” in the appendix dealing with the unpardonable sin in The Outer Darkness.

            b. God has responded positively to us even when we have responded negatively to Him. It is,
               therefore, irrational to presume that a negative response from us must necessarily nullify any
               positive response from Him.
                                                    Conclusion
      Lest there be a charge that I have a few missing links of my own in this response, I will take pains to illustrate
the connectivity in thought in such a manner that the logical cohesiveness should be readily apparent.
                           Illustration 11. Golden Chain of Soteriological Immutability




        God’s immutable character enables Him to make immutable promises that immutably bestow gifts, such as
justification and regeneration, in such a manner that these promises cannot be conditioned on ongoing contingency.
The end product is unconditional security.
                                       Illustration 12. Superficial Excavation




       Corner’s superficial analysis only dug deep enough to prove that some seemingly unconditional promises are
really conditional. In having only gone that far, Corner fails to acknowledge that what he finds are simply rewards
in the strata he examines. His error is understandable, to a degree, in that there are those within the Calvinistic camp
Page 46                                                                                  Dual Sealing and Security
who, likewise, believe that the presence of underlying conditionality demonstrates a bedrock of mutability beneath
all of God’s promises. Still, the error of one camp does not justify the error of another camp.
                                          Illustration 13. Missing Links




       By regarding a gift simply as an evolved reward just because he has found an underlying, ongoing
contingency for some rewards, Corner confuses the artifacts that he excavates. Regrettably, there are Calvinists
who, likewise, believe that they legitimately can hypothesize that a gift is a reward. They then regard their theory
as an established theological fact, regardless of the missing links in their exegesis.
                                       Illustration 14. Deeper Excavation




       Corner was certainly not the first to dig down below the topsoil and find underlying conditionality. Haley
does so as well. But Haley does not confuse this conditionality with bedrock. Like many others, Haley digs much
deeper and finds a stratum of mutability underlying the conditionality. Not stopping there, Haley probes even deeper
until he hits true bedrock and finds that below the mutability there is a principle of immutability.
Chapter 2. Conditional and Unconditional Promises                                                              Page 47
        Nevertheless, Haley confuses the facts as well but for a different reason. Realizing that a gift cannot actually
be conditioned on ongoing performance, Haley attempts to break the artifacts apart into their composite parts of
hypotheticality and conditionality and then tries to arrange the pieces into two separate piles labeled accordingly.
In this way, he hopes to avoid making the gifts he finds in the text truly conditional on ongoing obedience. His
motives may be well-intentioned, but in doing so, he destroys the state of the artifacts as they frequently are found
in the text, resulting in disjointed articles that were intended to be joined semantically. As I examined the exegetical
findings that Haley has uncovered, I noted that others have dug just as deeply into the text as he has, but they have
come away with vastly different conclusions than what he derived. These securitists (such as Thiessen, Geisler,
Barker, and Dillow) found that God’s promise of eternal life is grounded firmly and unconditionally in His
immutability. In making this observation, not only did they dig as deeply as Haley, they dug more widely in their
theological observations.

                                         Illustration 15. Wider Excavations




        In examining the work of securitists who have gone before me, I have been able to substantiate their primary
findings by correcting some of the inconsistencies in their labeling, while at the same time cataloging the strata in
which they have made their discoveries. Their defense for unconditional security has been found to rest solidly on
the bedrock of God’s immutable word (Is 55:11). God’s gifts are tied irrevocably and directly to His immutable
character. Accordingly, His promises of soteriological gifts inevitably lead to unconditional security for the believer
who has received those gifts so that punctiliar, immutable contingency must be affirmed.
        To elaborate my findings in terms of the excavations done by others whom I have depicted in the above
illustration, it will be noted that those who have dug down through the layers of apparent unconditionality to find
underlying conditionality have further found that this conditionality rests atop God’s mutability. Yet this mutability,
in the final analysis, is grounded upon the bedrock of God’s immutability. Corner prematurely concludes that he
has dislodged unconditionality from immutability by pointing out that the promises he examines in his section on
the conditionality of God’s promises indicate that one would have to dig down through conditionality and mutability
Page 48                                                                                     Dual Sealing and Security
before one could reach immutability. Given the parameters of his limited analysis, one could see how such a mistake
could easily be made. As a result, he has failed to note that rewards were the only thing he found down this shaft.
        Securitists have done further excavations and moved up the hermeneutical slope to dig other shafts as well.
In doing so, they have discovered that God’s promised gifts, such as eternal life, are directly embedded in God’s
immutability. No intermediate levels of conditionality or mutability are left as one commences up the ridge. The
mountain of unconditional, soteriological security remains unscathed by Corner’s limited excavations in the valley
below. As geologists must carefully record the strata of the geological column in which they find their artifacts, so
misthologists must carefully note the context in which they discover facts pertaining to conditionality.
        While performing these hermeneutical digs, I have unearthed some mistakes made by soteriologists while
digging on the mountain of immutability and discovered that the doctrine of rewards may be found embedded in
the soil of this mountain as well. The difference between these rewards and those found in lower altitudes is that
there is no longer any remaining condition for these mountain rewards. These rewards have been removed from the
shifting sands of linear conditionality and moldable clay of mutability, have gravitated to the granite of the mountain
by the power of God’s solemn oaths, and have become permanently embedded therein.
        Unlike the rewards found in the valley, these mountain rewards are not contingent on any remaining
conditions and therefore do not transverse the various sublevels of contingency found in more diverse subterranean
levels of the valley. Like gifts which have been realized (at least partially) by their acceptance and are therefore no
longer conditional, so these rewards are no longer contingent, even though they may await future realization.
        For example, the realization of the gift of life (although partially realized in the present by the believer who
has experienced regeneration) still awaits even further realization in that this believer is assured of living with God
in Heaven and then on Earth at the realization of the millennial kingdom.113 Yet those believers whom the Lord has
sworn will not enter His rest will find that there is nothing they can do to undo the future realization of that
misthological exclusion. The outer darkness unflinchingly awaits such believers when they more fully partake of
His misthological displeasure.
        Most importantly, it must be noted that an immediate drop from four to two layers occurs as soon as one
starts up the slope where securitists have found proof of unconditional security. This two-layered mountain ground
is also the home of unconditional threats as well. Both promises and threats found in the topsoil of seemingly
unconditionality on these mountain slopes will be found to be lying directly upon the bedrock of God’s immutability
so that there is no longer any conditionality to detract from unconditional soteriological security or to buffer one
from misthological consequences which have now become certain of fulfillment. There is no disparity between
being seemingly unconditional and truly unconditional once one reaches these slopes. The reason unconditional
security seems unconditional is because it really is unconditional. The seeming unconditionality on the mountain
rests directly upon God’s immutability and therefore actually is unconditional.
        This study has looked under the bed and found no room for lurking invisible monsters waiting to devour the
soteriological life of believers should they doze off. A believer can rest soteriologically in eternal security knowing
that this analysis has looked under the bed and found nothing but bedrock. There is no room for invisible, unfulfilled
soteriological conditions. Any such speculations have been crushed to death by the weight of the evidence. Corner’s
argument has died of a coronary as it attempted to climb the steep slopes of unconditional security and has been
completely disposed and decomposed in the soils of that altitude where securitists have made their soteriological
bed. That altitude dispels an attitude of soteriological fear.
        Almost as equally emphatically, I must point out that conditional promises are found in the four-layered soil
of the valley. No gifts exist in those soils. While misthological themes might penetrate all four strata and some such
themes may have their conditionality hidden from a superficial view, no soteriological benefits are found in the
valley because those who have partaken of soteriological benefits have received a gift which is immutable in its
fulfillment and must, therefore, be found entrenched in the unshakeable mountain slopes. Although unconditional
gifts and rewards may both be found on the slopes, they are located on those mountain slopes because their condition
has been met. These mountain artifacts may be distinguished from one another by discerning the fulfilled condition
which resulted in their being placed there. Rewards are based on works; gifts are not. A gift is not to be confused
with a reward nor a reward with a gift just because both share a similar characteristic of being grounded in God’s
113
    This SAS statement presumes that the outer darkness will be located on Earth during the millennial kingdom,
which was the position taken in TOD. More recent research suggests that the outer darkness might be in the outer
realm of the heavenly sphere. See MMP4. Regardless, all believers will have redeemed bodies and be somewhere
in the heavenly kingdom.
Chapter 2. Conditional and Unconditional Promises                                                                Page 49
immutability. By retaining our ability to discern a gift from a reward, we can avoid the mistakes of conditional
securitists when mining in the valley below and the errors of the unconditional securitists when mining in the
mountain above.
        To conclude, this inquiry dug much deeper into the theological column than Corner. Like others before me,
I found that underlying conditionality rests atop God’s mutability in His dealings with men. I dug even deeper and
found, as others have also, that God’s mutability in dealing with man rests atop the bedrock of His immutable
character. Moreover, it has further been discovered that securitists have found an indissoluble direct link between
eternal security and immutability. I pictorially have represented this discovery in the mountain slopes.
        At best, Corner’s limited excavations merely show that misthological promises have undergone
microevolution in clarification. The more thorough misthological investigations herein, on the other hand, have
uncovered passages which show that not even all of God’s misthological promises or threats can be overturned by
a subsequent change in a people or in a person. The principle of unconditionality applies both collectively and
personally, both positively and negatively to artifacts found in the mountain strata, and these strata are devoid of
any remaining conditionality for those who already have met the soteriological or misthological contingencies,
whether those contingencies were initially punctiliar or linear. Unconditional security rests directly upon the
bedrock of God’s immutability.
        Corner has no excuse for taking the head of a soteriological animal found on the mountain slopes and
attaching it to the body of a misthological animal found in the valley and then proclaiming that he has found the
missing link that proves unconditional security through a process of theological macroevolution. The only thing
such a claim proves is that his argument died on the slopes. He has only concocted the existence of a creature that
God never created.
        Perhaps his thesis found the slope too slippery for it to retain its footing, and so (after finding the head of an
argument for eternal security on the steep slopes and after severing that unconditional head from the buried body
of literature defending its unconditional status) his thesis lost its footing, fell dead, and dropped the severed head,
which then rolled down the slope into the hole that Corner had dug into conditional security in the valley. And
Corner, subsequently finding the severed head of unconditional security in the hole which he had dug for conditional
security, imagined that an argument from the pages of unconditional security would fit just as well within the
confines of his conditional premise, not realizing that his argument had detached the head of an unconditional
promise and attached it to the body of a misthologically linear commitment. One must be highly imaginative, after
all, in order to imagine how Corner could imagine that a gift is a reward and portray advocates of conditional
security as advocates of unconditional security, mixing them together as if they were one.
        Evolutionists are notorious for mislabeling and cross matching fossil remains. Martin elaborates concerning
one infamous example:

           By the way, did you know that there never was an actual dinosaur called Brontosaurus?
      Brontosaurus fooled the scientific community for many, many years. It turned out to be the head of
      one creature and the body of another. The evolutionary community was too embarrassed to admit this
      mistake for more than fifty years.114

       Hopefully, the Arminian community will not take as long to admit Corner’s mistake. Misthologists contend,
in contrast to conditional securitists, that a gift is not a more highly evolved reward. A gift is not, never has been,
and never will be a reward. The immutability and integrity of the giver of the gift require that the two not be
confused. There is no such creature as an eternal gift that can be taken back by its giver once it has been given.




114
   Jobe Martin, The Evolution of a Creationist: A Laymen’s Guide To The Conflict Between The Bible and
Evolutionary Theory (Rockwall, TX: Biblical Discipleship Publishers, 2004), 171-172.
Page 50   Dual Sealing and Security

                                        Dualistic Bilaterality
                                                    Arrabon is a Gift
        The argument SAS (which has been retained in the body of the present book), was that Paul intended his
readers to perceive a dualistic, bilateral-unilateral soteriological security in the manner in which he has employed
arrabon in Eph 1:14. Upon reading this verse in its context, Paul intended for his readers in Ephesus to grasp the
fact that God had made them unconditionally secure in Christ. God had promised them eternal life in Christ and
had secured them in Christ.
        God had made them a bilateral invitation: Believe in Christ and be consequently: adopted as sons (Eph 1:5);
sealed in Christ (Eph 1:13); entitled to a heavenly inheritance (Eph 1:11,14); assured of full redemption as His
possession (Eph 1:14), His inheritance (Eph 1:18); saved by grace apart from works (Eph 2:5,8-9); and members of
Christ’s body (Eph 3:5). All these wondrous truths had become true of them because they had believed in Christ.
By believing in Christ, they had fulfilled their part of this bilateral agreement. Fulfilling the remainder of this
agreement, to bring about the full realization of these soteriological promises, rested now solely and unilaterally
upon God. As a surety, these believers had been given the Holy Spirit as a down payment on their unconditional
security.
        Paul would fully expect that this surety would give his readers a firm sense of unconditional security as they
read his epistle, even when they reached Eph 4:30 and were implored not to grieve the Holy Spirit by whom they
had been sealed for the day of redemption. They are not to be shaken in their confidence that the gift of being saved
apart from works and their having the Holy Spirit as an earnest down payment of that benefit (namely, salvation
apart from works) was unilaterally secure. God would not default on the follow-through He had promised. Certainly,
these gifts, given apart from works, could not be lost because of works! The earnest of the Holy Spirit was theirs to
keep regarding that aspect of their salvation.
        In SAS, I stopped at this point. I did not proceed past Eph 4:30. Specifically, I did not proceed to Eph 5:5:
“For this you know with certainty, that no immoral or impure person or covetous man, who is an idolater, has an
inheritance in the kingdom of Christ and God.” Why did I not include this text in SAS? Because in my mind there
was no need to do so. Eph 5:5 is talking about the remaining bilateral contingency of believers having a
misthological inheritance in the kingship of Christ. In other words, it warns them that immoral or impure believers
will not have any inheritance in the kingship. They will be in the kingdom (so Eph 1:13-14) but have no portion in
the kingship. This regal portion had nothing to do with the soteriological inheritance of believers unilaterally
assured by God. Since SAS was focused on soteriology rather than misthology, I left the treatment of Eph 5:5 to
my book that delves more into misthological themes: The Outer Darkness.
        The inheritance was used dualistically, to be sure, but in my opinion at that time the soteriological aspect of
the inheritance was in the first part of the epistle, the misthological aspect in the latter part of the epistle. This is the
stock mentality among those in my camp, and I was certainly not exempt: Eph 1:14 is exclusively soteriological,
and Eph 5:5 is exclusively misthological. Or as Dillow expresses it: “While the inheritance in Ephesians 1:14 refers
to our birth-inheritance which comes to us by faith alone, in Ephesians 5:5 Paul addresses our reward-inheritance
which can be lost or enhanced.”115 Never the twain shall meet, so why bother dealing with the latter in a book
dedicated to the former? So I did not bother with this view in SAS.
        While I was assisting in proofreading a subsequent revision of Dillow’s Final Destiny (DFD), he requested
that I look at his original treatment of “The Earnest of Our Inheritance.”116 In this original, pre-published version
he had taken a misthological view of Eph 1:14. He had removed it in his published version of Destiny because of
peer review. He had returned to his original traditional view, that is, the soteriological view. In the sciences, peer
review is important, and theology is a science. In fact, it has been called the queen of the sciences. We must submit
our work to review by our peers. Therefore, Dillow’s listening to his peers demonstrates scientific prudence. As to
my initial review of his new view, I was not impressed when I first read his misthological proposal for Eph 1:14.
Too many unanswered objections remained.

115
    Joseph C. Dillow, Final Destiny: The Future Reign of the Servant Kings, 20th edition (Monument, CO: Paniym
Group, 2014), 122.
116
    Personal Correspondence, 12/5/2012.
Page 52                                                                                     Dual Sealing and Security
       The heart of his misthological take on Eph 1:14 was that if arrabon denotes an earnest, then it must refer to
a bilateral contingency that is conditioned on our works—due to the lexical usages of arrabon as a bilateral earnest
in ancient usages and the contextual contingency of the misthological inheritance in Eph 5:5.117 My principal
objections against his model were.

      1. Making the Holy Spirit God’s down payment in a misthological bilateral contingency would mean that
         we lose the down payment, and thus the Holy Spirit, if we are unfaithful. How would his new model
         explain unconditionally secure believers losing the Holy Spirit?
      2. His proposed model seems to violate the law of non-contradiction by taking arrabon misthologically in
         this text, while it is used soteriologically in both of its other Pauline occurrences (2Cor 1:22; Eph 4:30),
         and in all three occasions, it refers to the same entity—the Holy Spirit.118
      3. The sealing is soteric, not mistholic. So why think that the earnest is mistholic rather than soteric?
      4. The Holy Spirit is a gift. The misthological interpretation would seem to make the Holy Spirit part of the
         reward (payment) that we are given misthologically as an inheritance.
      5. Posing bilateral contingency is no obstacle to a soteriological interpretation of Eph 1:14. Indeed, my
         preference in SAS was for bilateral contingency, and yet I interpreted the verse soteriologically.

       Notwithstanding my original negative assessment to Dillow’s new proposal for Eph 1:14, I did find his
incorporation of Eph 5:5 to be intriguing. Could it be, I suggested to Dillow, that Paul intended for his readers to
perceive, retroactively of course, that the misthological dimension of the inheritance found in this latter reference
was to inform their initial understanding of the nature of the inheritance in 1:14 so that it is understood
dualistically?119 Is duality a reasonable possibility? After all, Paul clearly did not intend for them to think that the
Holy Spirit was an earnest of an equal inheritance for all believers in all capacities. His warning in Eph 5:5, not to
mention his multiple exhortations regarding the misthological nature of the inheritance, would make this abundantly
clear. Might Paul purposefully have used ambiguity to allow for soteric-mistholic duality? Dillow’s suggestion
merited further consideration. Soteric-mistholic duality is certainly a common NT phenomenon.
117
    Lexically, some interpreters would contend that an earnest was a business term that bilaterally committed the
seller and buyer to complete the deal under stipulations and penalties. In this commercial transaction, God is the
buyer who obligates Himself to reserve a misthological inheritance for believers upon the condition that believers
are faithful. God buys their faithfulness, so to speak, with His promise of an inheritance. Unfaithful believers fail
to obtain this inheritance because they fail to follow through on their part of the sale. What Dillow failed to factor
into his proposed new model, though, was that the lexical examples he provided indicate that the seller would have
to pay back the original earnest to the buyer if the seller did not complete the terms of contract. Like myself, Dillow
used the engagement ring analogy. A man who gives a ring as a pledge is morally obligated to marry the woman to
whom he gives it if she is faithful. What Dillow failed to acknowledge was that the woman is morally obligated to
return the ring if she is unwilling or unable to follow through with the groom’s expectations. The groom has the
right to expect his ring back if she does not follow through. In our email correspondences, Dillow did not think this
was a problem because he considered the Holy Spirit a gift which could not be returned. In my assessment, however,
he was mixing apples and oranges with this assumption. In the examples for earnest which he provided, the earnest
was not a gift. So I challenged him to rework his model or defend his assumption. Since he did not pursue it at that
time, I am doing so here. My proposal is that we have apples and oranges, so to speak, but we need to keep them
separate. We need to consider the possibility, for instance, that Paul is picturing the Holy Spirit as both a
soteriological pledge and misthological earnest. In that case, the Spirit would be an unreturnable gift in the former
capacity but a forfeitable reward in the latter capacity.
118
    In his Neo-Dillowian model, Dillow reasonably conceded that the Holy Spirit appears to be given as a unilateral
soteriological pledge (arrabon) in 2Cor 1:22 and 2Cor 5:5, unconditionally assuring believers that they will receive
spiritual bodies. (Therefore, the soteriological arguments made in SAS remain intact.) However, he also sensibly
countered that this does not mean that arrabon could not be taken as a bilateral earnest in Eph 1:14 since that
context is dealing with the inheritance.

      The soteric-mistholic duality of eternal life is well-known in FG circles. The following list was expanded
from some of my earlier writings to include more passages dealing with the soteric-mistholic duality of eternal life.
These passages denoted with a bold font span both lists:

• Spiritual life is a gift: Mt 7:14; Jn 3:15-16,36; 4:14 (cp. 4:10); 5:24; 6:40,47,54,68; 10:10a,28; Rom 5:18; 6:23;
  1Tim 1:16; 1Jn 5:11,13.
• Spiritual life is a reward: Mt 7:14; 19:16,29; 25:46; Mk 10:17,30; Lk 10:25; 18:18,30; Jn 6:54,68; 10:10b, 12:25;
  Rom 2:7; 5:21; 6:22; Gal 6:7-8; 1Thess 5:23; 1Tim 6:12; Tit 1:2; 3:7; Jam 1:12; 1Pet 3:7; Rev 2:7,10-11; 7:17;
  22:14.

        As already noted herein, the duality of eternal life is widely recognized. This duality already has been graphed
herein as it relates to Jesus.120 As it relates to believers, as pictured in the graph above, our entrance into Heaven
was unconditionally guaranteed the moment we came into possession of eternal life as a result of believing in Jesus
for such life. Our enjoyment of that life, however, is subject to wide variation. Such observations are very
elementary for misthologists. Our FG misthological peers would have no trouble agreeing with and expounding
upon the basic implications of the above graph of eternal life. Some passages of Scripture talk about eternal life as
a gift, while other passages talk about the reward dimension of eternal life. We may not agree with each other on
the classification of every passage, but the general accuracy of the above graph would be well accepted within our
ranks.
