      See Illustration 5. Did Jesus Lose Eternal Life?, 17.
Page 54                                                                                   Dual Sealing and Security
                            Illustration 17. The Duality of the Inheritance in Romans




       Along the same lines, the above graph of the inheritance would be readily acknowledged by our fellow
misthologists as being an accurate representation of the biblical picture of the inheritance presented by Paul: “ The
Spirit Himself bears witness with our spirit that we are children of God, and if children, then heirs also: on the one
hand, heirs of God, but on the other hand, co-heirs with Christ if indeed we co-suffer with Him in order that we
also may be co-glorified with Him” (Rom 8:16-17; TM). To the degree we suffer with Christ, we will enjoy being
co-gloried with Christ as co-rulers with Christ. Yet the reward dimension of this inheritance can be forfeited
altogether by unfaithful believers. Notwithstanding, the gift dimension of our inheritance as children of God is in
no way conditioned on our suffering with Christ and is not diminished in the least by our unfaithfulness. A gift is
not a reward. This is basic Misthology 101.
                                 Illustration 18. Model One: The Classical Model
Appendix 1: Dualistic Bilaterality                                                                             Page 55

       Similarly, the gift dimension of the inheritance and the items associated with it in Eph 1:13-14 must be kept
distinct from the reward dimension of inheritance in Eph 5:5. Never the twain shall meet, being separated by a
soteriological threshold. They are related yet separated. Thus, when Dillow asked that his peers consider a paradigm
shift so that the earnest and inheritance in Eph 1:14 are understood misthologically, most naturally and strongly
they resisted. Would this not also entail understanding the sealing and Spirit as being rewards? As I shared with
Dillow, my peer review of his proposal would be completely negative, just like that of those to whom he originally
submitted this proposal.
       At the theoretical level, I realize that one might pose that we could lose the Holy Spirit because Paul said that
we were sealed with the Spirit until the day of redemption, even though I would be highly resistant to such a
proposal.121 Nevertheless, notwithstanding my objections, one might theorize that just as David feared to lose the
Holy Spirit in Ps 51:11, so believers might fear to lose the Holy Spirit at that time if they lived a life that was
grievous to the Spirit (Eph 4:30). Even so, as securitists, we would insist that the regeneration imparted by the Spirit
is not at stake. Still, even if the concession were made that unfaithful believers might lose the Holy Spirit in a non-
regenerative sense, the sealing would not be a reward. So how could the earnest be forfeitable? In addition to these
considerations and my other principle objections noted above, my “Warning to Misthologists” would certainly seem
applicable to such an interpretation.122 This warning would urge us not to turn this soteriological text into a
misthological text! For, otherwise, we unnecessarily, and probably quite wrongly, risk weakening Paul’s original
intent, which is to affirm unconditional security. Additionally, even if you could somehow make it past all these
objections, Occam’s Razor surely would cut such an interpretation to shreds. So, if at this point you simply want to
shred the suggestion that arrabon is a misthological concept and toss any suggestion otherwise into the garbage,
then I will certainly understand. I did too...initially.
                                                  Arrabon is a Reward
        On the other hand, a modification to Dillow’s original hypothesis might even strengthen the case for
unconditional security. Plausibly, Paul intended for us to see both possibilities. I would fully expect for Paul to
expect his readers to adopt the perspective pictured above in Illustration 18. Model One: The Classical Model upon
their initial reading of his letter. He would expect them to rejoice in their unconditional security and in having the
Spirit as their unconditional surety as they read Eph 1:13-14, and he would also expect them seriously to
contemplate his exhortation not to grieve the Spirit (Eph 4:30) as they read his warning in Eph 5:5. However, it is
reasonable to conjecture that, given time to reflect on what he had written, he would expect them also to suspect
that there might be some way Eph 5:5 would have an impact on Eph 1:13-14.
        Conditionalists have come to conclude that the 5:5 can completely nullify Eph 1:13-14. Obviously, for reasons
already explained herein, I believe that they have completely misunderstood Paul’s intention with their supposition.
For similar reasons, I initially rejected Dillow’s proposal that Eph 5:5 could completely nullify the soteriological
nature of arrabon of Eph 1:13-14. But could there be a richness to Dillow’s proposal that I had not perceived until
he asked me to consider his argument? Might the conditionalists have been partially right? I will submit the
following modification to Dillow’s misthological proposal for peer review, thereby allowing a misthological
conditionality. Paul may intend a richness to his text that even Occam’s Razor will not be able to cut.
        Paul expected his readers to perceive arrabon to be an unconditional gift upon their initial reading of his
letter. Indeed, in the context of ch. 1, arrabon appears to be nothing more, in the minds of many, than an affirmation
of unconditional security. However, Paul further expected his readers to reflect upon what impact his warning in
Eph 5:5 might have upon their initial understanding of Eph 1:13-14. Naturally, he would not expect them to undo
what he had done, however, in that original context. He purposefully had imparted the unmistakable impression
that arrabon was an unconditional gift that had been granted permanently because they had completed their part of
the transaction by coming to saving faith. The part of the transaction was a done deal on their part. Come what may,
the down payment, the Holy Spirit, was theirs to keep as a security of their eternal security. Still, Paul would expect
them to see a depth to his teaching that they had not perceived upon their initial reading.

      See Sealed or Saved, 18.

      See The Outer Darkness, 960-961.
Page 56                                                                                       Dual Sealing and Security
                                  Illustration 19. Model 2: The Neoclassical Model




       Upon their further reflection on what he had written, my original hypothesis was that Paul intended for his
readers to perceive a duality to how he implemented arrabon and some (not all) of the related terms. Note that seal
is only used in the horizontal orientation in the graph, that is, in a strictly soteriological capacity. The soteriological
dimension was a done deal on the part of the believers. There was nothing further that they had to do in order to
come into the realization of the soteriological dimension of their inheritance. At the same time, they were eventually
expected to perceive that an additional richness to their experience, as described in Eph 1:13-14, was at stake if they
grieved the Holy Spirit by whom they were sealed (Eph 4:30).
       Briefly, they could lose their fellowship with the Spirit. Being sealed with the Spirit in one’s spirit (i.e., the
inner man, Eph 3:16) did not automatically mean that they would have fellowship with Him in their hearts. Just as
having Christ dwell in their hearts was not automatic (Eph 3:17), so having the Spirit dwell in their hearts was not
unconditional. Spiritual union with the Spirit was static; heartfelt communion was dynamic. Although believers
could not lose the gift of the union, they could lose the reward of the communion. If they failed to walk in the Spirit,
they would lose fellowship with the Spirit, not only now, but more importantly in the future, when it was time to
realize the inheritance. The misthological fellowship one could have had with the Spirit could be taken away at the
Judgment Seat of Christ.
       The sweetest thing we have in life is the fellowship of the Spirit. To miss out on the misthological dimension
of that fellowship for all eternity would be the greatest misthological loss imaginable. Only a fool would trivialize
losing the Spirit in this capacity. To be sure, we cannot lose our soteriological union with the Spirit, as denoted by
the horizontal dimension of the graph. But the vertical dimension is subject to wide variation. Not everyone will
have an equal inheritance. While we are all equally God’s inheritance/heritage in the sense of being God’s children
(cp. Ps 127:3), we will not equally share in His inheritance in the sense of having opportunities for fellowship with
Him.
       In Illustration 19. Model 2: The Neoclassical Model, I did not place the seal in the vertical dimension because
at the time I was originally entertaining this new model I did not believe Paul would. He may expect us to see a
duality to our having the Spirit, earnest, inheritance, and possession. But I viewed being sealed in Christ as being
purely and exclusively free. We cannot lose the (ministry of the) Spirit in this capacity. Nor is this the only occasion
in which Scripture would encourage us to see a purely free aspect being juxtaposed with an aspect that functions
dually.

                               Illustration 20. The See/Enter Duality of Eternal life




       In my New Birth by the Sovereign Wind (NBSW), I explain the probable duality intended for the word see in
Jn 3:3 in the above chart. Yet, I warned that the word enter should not be understood dualistically in Jn 3:5. As I
stressed in my “Warning to Misthologists” in The Outer Darkness, do not interpret enter neoclassically, that is,
dually. In my proposal for Eph 1:13-14 (at this point in the present discussion), I am heeding my own advice in
NBSW. As entering the kingdom in Jn 3:5 is to be understood exclusively in the soteriological sense, so sealing in
Eph 1:13 is strictly soteriological.123

Page 58                                                                                      Dual Sealing and Security
       These texts make for especially interesting conceptual parallels because the dual components within these
verses are understood primarily in a soteriological sense and only secondarily in an additional sense. Furthermore,
these dual elements are juxtaposed with elements that are singularly soteriological. Not all such conceptual parallels
fall into this category, though, although this does not distract from their applicability. For example, even though
Rev 2:11 is found at the opposable end of the chart from Eph 1:13-14, it is an exclusively misthological text,
implements a soteric-mistholic duality through the instrumentality of the Principle of Correlativity, and involves a
grammatical litotes, it nevertheless is an interesting parallel to Eph 1:13-14, because in both cases the original author
would not have expected his readers to pick up on the duality until they had reflected upon the implications of what
was written later in his book. For the purpose of classification, which is an important part of the scientific inquiry,
these passages have been classified as falling into three categories in the chart:

      •   Class 1: Singular and Dual Use of Elements
      •   Class 2: Singular Use of Singular Elements
      •   Class 3: Dual Use of Singular Elements

       Whereas, the duality in Rev 2:11 is litotetically and correlatively derived, the duality expressed in 1Pet 2:9 is
of a different sort and is particularly important since its duality is derived from the OT background Peter employs.
If Paul has this same OT background in mind, then the supposition that a secondary misthological dimension is
intended by Paul in Eph 1:14 is virtually certain. Regardless, the fact that peripoiesis (possession) always has a
misthological dimension the other times it is used in the NT and the certainty that it has a dualistic soteric-mistholic
dimension in 1Pet 2:9 (see MMP3) give strong grounds for concluding that a soteric-mistholic dimension is intended
in Eph 1:13-14 as well.
       As securitists, broadly speaking, we have two competing models, therefore, by which to interpret Eph 1:13-
14. Classically, we can interpret it as dealing exclusively with soteriology and thus as simply advocating
unconditional security (see Illustration 18). Neoclassically, we can interpret it as stressing unconditional security
in the immediate context while, at the same time, laying a foundation for allowing some of its elements to be
perceived as having a misthological range, upon reflection from the broader context (see Illustration 19).125 Given
my research in other texts in which I have seen similar dualistic phenomena (e.g., MMP2, MMMP3) and the fact
that the above chart is only a sampling of that data, I am willing to give this neoclassical model a fair hearing.
Accordingly, one might argue that the case for unconditional security is now doubled regarding this passage. The
argument being advanced in favor of unconditional security is twice as strong now that securitists have two
consistent models by which to interpret this passage. But wait...there’s more!
       As seen in the above table of elements (Illustration 21), the neo-Dillowian model is just another potential
example of class-2 duality. Given that Paul uses this type of duality in the other class-2 passages, one may propose
that he does so here as well. Dillow’s class-2 perspective is really a third model advocating unconditional security
in Eph 1:13-14. His new model does not weaken the case for unconditional security; rather, it strengthens it. Here
is why.

125
    Dillow’s (neo-ultraistic) interpretation of Eph 1:13-14 is a work in progress. Further consideration of his
alternative model will be deferred to his treatment in which the potentially misthological elements in Eph 1:13-14
are considered exclusively misthological. Normally, a neo-ultraistic perspective would be a misthological synthesis
between a soteriological and misthological perspective. In this case, Dillow’s proposal was a synthesis between two
competing soteriological perspectives in which insecuritists, unwittingly, were presenting their soteriological
interpretation as if it were a misthological interpretation. Their cross contamination of the data results in their
making a reward out of the gift—a theological brontosaurus. The neoclassical position being entertained within is
a synthesis of classical position and the neo-Dillowian proposal. This neoclassical position is not susceptible to the
brontosaurus phenomena, however, in that the neoclassical model has a soteriological threshold by which to prevent
cross contamination of the data, preventing the gift aspect from becoming confused with the reward aspect. The gift
aspect of the arrabon remains a gift; it does not turn into a reward. Securitists are aware of the accuracy of this
approach as it pertains to eternal life and even to the inheritance in other passages. Therefore, I suggest that the
same consideration be given to the study of the inheritance in this passage. A class-1 understanding is feasible.
Furthermore, the present study will take tentative steps toward developing a working class-2 model that will address
the weaknesses currently perceived in that model by peer reviews.
Appendix 1: Dualistic Bilaterality                                                                               Page 59
                              Illustration 22. Model Three: The Neo-Dillowian Model




       If Dillow counters my criticism of his model by insisting that being sealed in Christ is an affirmation of
unconditional security, then he is free to explore the possibility that Paul intends for the remaining imagery in the
verse to be understood in an exclusively misthological sense without jeopardizing the doctrine of unconditional
security. As we know from Paul’s own pen and from our own experience, the fellowship of the Spirit is a prize of
the utmost value (Phil 2:1). Yet this fellowship is subject to wide fluctuation. Indeed, we can lose our fellowship
with the Spirit. Being a partaker in that communion is conditional. Could not the heartfelt fellowship that we
experience with the Spirit now be but a small foretaste of the fellowship with the Spirit available to us then, if we
will just walk in fellowship with the Spirit now? Could this not be part of Paul’s point in Eph 1:13-14? We need
not confuse communion with union. Losing communion with the Spirit in the present is not pictured by Paul as
losing one’s union with the Spirit (1Cor 6:18-20), so why pose that he would regard losing one’s misthological
fellowship in the future with the Spirit as being a soteriological issue?126
       Further, the neo-Dillowian model, as seen in Illustration 22, is able to appeal just as readily as the other two
models to the soteriological threshold in order to avoid conflict with the law of non-contradiction. All three models
(the classical, neoclassical, and neo-Dillowian) have a threshold, and, in certain respects, all three are dualistic.127

126
    As a trichotomist, I might pose that our ontological union with the Spirit (in our spirits) is not forfeitable but our
soulical communion with the Spirit (in our hearts) is forfeitable. Nevertheless, a potential objection could be raised
that the Spirit is given as an arrabon in our hearts (2Cor 1:22). Yet counters are readily at hand. For example, having
the Spirit as an arrabon in our hearts might be equated with being joined with the Spirit in our spirits and
distinguished from having fellowship with the Spirit in our hearts. Or, perhaps, following the neo-Dillowian model,
being sealed is soteriological in 2Cor 1:22 but having the Spirit as our arrabon is misthological. Or perhaps Dillow
would counter this objection in some other manner. Regardless, if with only halfhearted effort I can suggest counters
to my own objections to Dillow’s new model, then surely his argument is more plausible than originally perceived.
There appear to be a variety of ways that he can avoid the law of non-contradiction regarding the cross references.

Moreover, Occam’s Razor is blunted by the realization that this third model has no more moving parts, so to speak,

the prestigious position of being the most forceful argument for unconditional security in this passage. At the risk

       These models are not in competition with one another. They are playing on the same team. What is more, if
Dillow can strengthen his case for the third model, it only serves to strengthen the case for the second model rather
than weaken it. My proposal in model 2 is a synthesis of the classical and neo-Dillowian models. If models 1 and 3
are strong, then they give model 2 two strong legs upon which to stand. The bottom line is that Dillow’s newer
model makes the case for unconditional security three times stronger than it was in an exclusively classical model.
So, as securitists, we should wish him well in developing this model.
       The only remaining objection that comes to my mind is that some conditionalists will stand up and shout that
we must limit our interpretation to one perspective because the standard hermeneutical textbooks tell us that we
must limit ourselves to the singular authorial intent. I respond by pointing out that Paul had not read our
contemporary hermeneutical textbooks and was probably not aware that he had to limit himself in such a manner.
If the same Holy Spirit can invite us to look at the matter from multiple points of view is the same Holy Spirit who
inspired Paul to express such a point of view, then who are we to say that Paul was so shortsighted so as not to be
able to see how his analogy could be true from multiple points of view? Plausibly, Paul expected his readers to be
able to look back and see how his statement was true from multiple vantage points. I am comfortable with that point
of view, indeed, comfortable enough now to wade into the deeper end of the pool in the next appendix.

and 5:5) to establish the duality. Nevertheless, I have only been partially consistent with that methodology in that I
did include weeping and gnashing of teeth in the chart, yet this duality must be established by a composite of
passages. No single passage uses the expression in both a soteric and mistholic manner. Some passages are soteric;
some are mistholic. They are united, however, by the Principle of Correlativity. See TOD for further discussion of
this principle and for other passages that could be added to the table of elements. For example, the possibility of
believers being condemned along with the world (1Cor 11:32) might easily have been added as a class-3 duality.
My Table of Soteric-Mistholic Elements herein is only intended to be suggestive, by no means exhaustive.
                                          Appendix 2:
                                      Soteric-Mistholic Seal
                                                    Introduction
      Moving into deeper waters, we now need to examine the possibility of misthological sealing more thoroughly.
I added the previous appendix to this book back in 2012 as a result of my interaction with Dillow but never published
it because other writing projects were more pressing. But early in 2019, I received a request from Hollandsworth
for a master Scripture index of my books.128 I could not supply an updated one for the present book because I had
never published the revised edition. The time had come for me finally to get around to releasing this revision.
However, in the interim, two things had occurred that required this additional appendix.
                                    Background for Misthological Perception
       First, my own writings had progressed. For example, I had released MMP2, which now allowed a
misthological interpretation of entering the kingdom in Jn 3:5 as a secondary vantage point if certain stipulations
were observed to maintain soteriological entrance as the primary vantage point. However, allowing a soteric-
mistholic dualism for enter in Jn 3:5 might have an impact on my argument for seal in Eph 1:13-14. Thus, I
interjected a note in Appendix 1 that we would have to examine that possibility in this second appendix.
       Second, Hollandsworth had also sent me a link a few months earlier to sermons he was preaching on
Ephesians.129 He then followed up with a link for his sermon on Born from Above, covering new birth in Jn 3:3,5.130
He was taking a more misthological view of Ephesians than I had in TOD, though he was acknowledging its
soteriological components. Similarly, he was adopting a misthological view of enter in Jn 3:5, while noting its
soteriological context. He was open for my feedback. Because I had not released my revision of SAS, he was
unaware that I now allowed misthological implications regarding Eph 1:13-14 as a result of my interaction with
Dillow years previously. In our personal correspondences, Dillow and I already discussed a misthological
interpretation of Eph 1:14 as being probable.
                                  Hollandsworth’s Misthological Advancement
      Hollandsworth goes beyond a misthological interpretation of Eph 1:14 to adopt a misthological interpretation
of several additional passages. For example, he takes Eph 1:1 as addressing the saints who are faithful, focusing the
address of the epistle to faithful believers. Eph 1:5 is taken as a misthological predestination to misthological
adoption as mature sons. The predestination for the inheritance in Eph 1:11 is also misthological and contingent
upon our faithfulness.131 The present redemption of our bodies in Eph 1:7 is soteric, while the future redemption in
Eph 1:14 is misthological and tied with the misthological inheritance in v. 14. Hopefully, he will put his discussion
in book form soon.
      Given the discussion between me and Dillow provided in the previous appendix, many of the misthological

positions taken by Hollandsworth in his sermons on Ephesians are in harmony with my previously acknowledged
propositions. For instance, the possibility of a misthological adoption was entertained for Rom 8:23 in TOD.
Hollandsworth advanced the misthological perception by adopting an exclusively misthological view of adoption
in all relevant passages (i.e., Rom 8:15,23; 9:4; Gal 4:5; Eph 1:5). His proposal of an exclusive misthological
perception regarding mature sons (huios) is certainly plausible. The concept of contingent misthological
predestination was already confirmed in TOD. A misthological inheritance was acknowledged in several passages
in TOD. Yet, by more consistently interweaving these misthological perceptions into the context of ch. 1,
Hollandsworth has provided a stronger misthological context for the misthological interpretation of Eph 1:14.

blend.
                                    Illustration 24. Three Proposals for Duality
       1. Soteric-mistholic (Cauley in TOD).
       2. Soteric-mistholic (Dillow and Cauley in DSAS Appendix 1)

                                                  Soteriological Seal
       In his sermons, Hollandsworth still takes Eph 1:13 as soteric: “In Him, you also, after listening to the message
of truth, the gospel of your salvation—having also believed, you were sealed in Him with the Holy Spirit of
promise.” Therefore, Hollandsworth understands the verse as confirming that believers are permanently and
unconditionally indwelt by the Holy Spirit. This soteric sealing by the Spirit assures them of unconditional security.
However, he takes Eph 1:14 as mistholic, evidently thinking that it adds an additional perspective so that the Spirit
also “is given as a pledge of our [contingent misthological] inheritance.” We must fulfill our call to experiential
holiness to actualize our misthological hope. Such a view certainly falls within the parameters of the models
entertained as plausible in Appendix 1. Indeed, I would like to see Hollandsworth advance his model in a publication
of his material on Ephesians. We both perceive soteric-mistholic duality. We simply have competing models by
which to express it. Scientific inquiry welcomes healthy competing models as an iron-sharpening-iron experience.
At this juncture, he has posed a soteric seal and mistholic earnest, which is a possibility Dillow and I had previously
considered.132 I had also considered the possibility of a soteric seal and soteric-mistholic earnest.133 In the present
appendix, though, I would like to pose yet one more dualistic possibility: a soteric-mistholic seal in conjunction
with a soteric-mistholic earnest.134
                                              Dual Seal in Rom 4:11
       Before considering the possibility of dualistic sealing in Eph 1:13, I will first prove it in two other Pauline
passages. In 2012, Dillow and I, independently of one another, both advanced the thesis in DFD and TOD,
respectively, that Rom 4:12 provides a pivot point in Romans 4 from soteric to mistholic themes. Dillow said, “In
verse 12 Paul moved beyond initial justification to walking in faith throughout one’s life.”135 Similarly, I provided
a discussion in TOD with an illustration making Rom 4:12 this pivot point. After listening to Hollandsworth’s
sermon, however, I was curious as to whether the Bible ever affirmed the possibility of a misthological seal. To my
surprise, both Dillow and I had missed it, though it was right under our noses. Given the joint discussions by me
and Dillow, it must be concluded that Paul is talking about a misthological seal in Rom 4:11. Dillow’s treatment
and mine may be consulted for the details for Rom 4:12ff. I will supplement our earlier discussions in 2012 with
this augmentation.

    Like computer software, every model has bugs in it that must be worked out. My models are no exception.
Indeed, working out the bugs is often where the most interesting discoveries are found. One bug that Hollandsworth
would need to work out with his model is to explain why the Spirit is a gift-seal in Eph 1:13 but a reward-earnest
in Eph 1:14 and a gift-seal-reward-earnest in 2Cor 1:22. However, a gift cannot be a reward. So if Hollandsworth
is successful in maintaining a thoroughgoing distinction between the two in Eph 1:13-14, he will need to explain
what happens to that distinction in 2Cor 1:22, where the seal seems to be equated with the earnest. One way to do
so would be to take arrabon as an exclusively soteriological pledge in 2Cor 1:22, thus paired with an exclusively
soteriological seal in that context. But other options are available.

       Dillow’s discussion of the passage in Romans is excellent. Yet both he and I missed an obvious detail. Nor
were we the only ones grappling with the soteric-mistholic implications of the text who failed to pick the correct
soteric-mistholic pivot point. Eaton placed the soteric-to-mistholic pivot point in Rom 4:13. He believed that both
references to uncircumcised faith in Rom 4:11-12 are soteric but that Abraham’s persistence in this initial faith is
mistholic and the subject matter of Rom 4:13-21.136 Govett perceived Rom 4:11 to be soteric and Rom 4:13 to be
mistholic. He sought to make Rom 4:12 the pivot point by breaking it up grammatically into describing two groups:
one soteric, the other mistholic. Govett, Eaton, Dillow, and I were are all in agreement as to the basic thrust of the
passage, but perceiving the exact nature of Paul’s transition from the soteric to mistholic vantage point was proving
to be somewhat problematic. I interacted with Eaton and Govett in TOD, and my perception at that time can be
summarized with the following color coding.

      My misperception at that time was that Rom 4:11 was simply describing Abraham’s soteric faith while he

faith long before the confirmation of his faith in the narrative location of Gen 15:6, but his persistency in faith from
that previous point of justifying faith began to qualify him for receiving an inheritance even from that initial point
of justification. We may deduce that the misthological faith Abram exercised at age 70 in leaving Ur was the first
biblically inferred misthological step of faith that Abram exercised. Likewise, Abraham’s first step of
misthologically signed faith was in Rom 4:11, but this was not Abraham’s first step of faith toward qualifying for
the inheritance. Both misthological steps of faith were taken long before Genesis 22. Had Eaton deduced that the
pivot point was in Rom 4:12 rather than Rom 4:13, he would have been in a much better position to infer that
Abraham’s misthologically qualifying faith, like our NT faith, starts at the point of punctiliar justifying faith. From
that punctiliar moment on, our persistence in faith has the potential to qualify us misthologically for the inheritance.

was in his uncircumcised state before he left Ur. That perception is foundationally correct, but it is insufficient.
Nevertheless, I correctly perceived in TOD that in Rom 4:12, Abraham’s faith in God started accruing mistholic
value after Abraham obeyed God by leaving Ur and started following God in faith. This verse is simply describing
mistholic faith. For Rom 4:13, I took a composite approach. In v. 13, Paul repeated the soteric expression, the
righteousness of the faith from v. 11 but placed it in a misthological context. Thus, the soteriological component
was foundational for the misthological advancement. This dualistic approach was also correct and basically what
Eaton had suggested. My advancement over Eaton’s argument, at that time, was that I recognized that the transition
took place earlier than what he proposed. Eaton placed it in v. 13. I placed it in v. 12. My shortcoming was that,
back then, I did not realize that the same argument was applicable to v. 11. Paul took a dualistic approach there
also.
       Like myself, Dillow placed the pivot point at v. 12. He quoted Rom 4:11-12 and then proceeded to list the
three steps of faith that Abraham took in v. 12, which are noted by the author of Hebrews. Yet, like myself, Dillow
failed to factor in the step provided by Paul in Rom 4:11, which I have interjected below in red.
                               Illustration 27. Abraham’s Four Misthological Steps
      1. “By faith Abraham, when he was called [in Ur at the age of 70], obeyed by going out to a place which
         he was to receive for an inheritance; and he went out, not knowing where he was going” (Heb 11:8).
      2. By faith Abraham, in the land of Canaan at the age of 99, received “the sign of circumcision, a seal
         of the righteousness of the faith which he had while uncircumcised” (Rom 4:11).
      3. “By faith he lived as an alien in the land of promise, as in a foreign land, dwelling in tents with Isaac and
         Jacob, fellow heirs of the same promise; for he was looking for the city which has foundations, whose
         architect and builder is God” (Heb 11:9-10).
      4. “By faith Abraham, when he was tested, offered up Isaac; and he who had received the promises was
         offering up his only begotten son; it was he to whom it was said, ‘In Isaac your descendants shall be
         called’” (Heb 11:17-18).

                                   Illustration 28. Timing of Abraham’s Circumcision




      The timing and context of the second step is critical. In Illustration 27, it is denoted in red and highlighted in
yellow. In Illustration 28, it is the second one circled in red. God introduces and requires circumcision in Gen 17.
That chapter begins with a demand that Abram would walk before God blamelessly: “Now when Abram was
ninety-nine years old, the Lord appeared to Abram and said to him, “I am God Almighty; walk before Me, and be
blameless. And I will establish My covenant between Me and you, and I will multiply you exceedingly” (Gen 17:1-
2). The establishment of this covenant is based upon the expectation of a blameless walk. In accord with this
expectation, God proceeds to change Abram’s name to Abraham and to promise Abraham that he and his decedents
will possess all the lands of his sojourning, that is, “all the land of Canaan, as an everlasting possession” (Gen 17:8).
Other blessings are also listed, such as being numerous and being kings (Gen 17:6). But God proceeds to demand
circumcision for the realization of the promise: “God said further to Abraham, ‘Now as for you, you shall keep My
covenant, you and your descendants after you throughout their generations. This is My covenant, which you shall
keep, between Me and you and your descendants after you: every male among you shall be circumcised” (Gen 17:9-
10). The misthological overtones are apparent. The Promised Land is a reward conditioned on a blameless walk in
Appendix 2: Soteric-Mistholic Seal                                                                              Page 65
                       137
keeping the covenant. No wonder Paul and other NT writers want believers to be misthologically blameless
(Eph 1:4; 5:27; Phil 1:10; 2:15; Col 1:22; 2Pet 3:14; Jude 1:24). In receiving circumcision in order to inherit the
land, Abraham is a misthological prototype of the faithful NT believer. Inheriting the Promised Land is a prototype
of the misthological inheritance of the Reward City (e.g., Heb 13:14). Misthological blamelessness (righteousness)
and misthological perfection (maturity) are required to inherit/realize such rewards.
       Moreover, this OT circumcision is a sign (prototype) of this misthological covenant with its attendant
requirement that we walk blamelessly as God’s sanctified (set apart) people: “And you shall be circumcised in the
flesh of your foreskin; and it shall be the sign of the covenant between Me and you’” (Gen 17:11). The passage
proceeds to timestamp when Abraham was circumcised: “Now Abraham was ninety-nine years old when he was
circumcised in the flesh of his foreskin” (Gen 17:24). Obviously, this circumcision is misthological—a
misthological sign that points to the misthological covenant. The OT background is crystal clear as to the
misthological nature of this covenant, inheritance, and blamelessness (cp. Gen 15:1 in TOD). The NT picks up on
the misthological nature of these blessings and requirements and applies them to NT believers. Paul even calls this
misthological sign a seal in Rom 4:11. Certainly, then, Paul must mean a misthological seal! These factors call for
a reclassification of the righteousness of the faith which Abraham had in v. 11. This faith which Abraham had in
his uncircumcised state would include both (1) his soteric faith which he had before he left Ur and (2) his mistholic
faith which He had after he left Ur, inclusive of his faith at the time of his circumcision.

       My statement in TOD was accurate insofar as it goes: “Following ‘in the steps of the faith of our father
Abraham’ in Rom 4:12 is the footbridge by which Paul thematically crosses from soteriological imputation to
misthological imputation for those believers who follow in Abraham’s steps.” Dillow and I both perceived that
Rom 4:11 is talking about soteriological faith and the soteriological imputation of righteousness and that Rom 4:12
makes a transition to misthological faith and misthological imputation of righteousness. Hence, we naturally treated
Rom 4:12 as the pivot point from soteriology to misthology. What we failed to note, though, was that Rom 4:11 is
a combination of soteriology and misthology. At the very least, the sign and seal are mistholic, while the faith and
righteousness are soteric. However, the mistholic faith that Abraham had while in his uncircumcised state at the
time he was circumcised in v. 11 was inclusive of the soteric faith he had in God before he left Ur. His uncircumcised
faith was a combination of mistholic faith built upon a soteric foundation. Therefore, the mistholic nature of the

as his reward (per Hebrews)? My hypothesis is that Abram was a soteriological believer long before he received
his misthological call at age 70. Misthologically, even at the age of 70, he was seeking to qualify for terrestrial and
celestial rewards. His terrestrial descendants would be as numerous as the sand of the seashore, and his celestial
descendants would be as numerous as the stars of heaven (Gen 22:17; Heb 11:12). Typologically, his misthological
faith can be used soteriologically to illustrate Paul’s point in Rom 4:3. The lack of explicit biblical details regarding
the misthological nature of his faith at the juncture of his call allows a soteriological application typologically just
as the lack of genealogical data in the biblical text allows Melchizedek to be used typologically to represent Christ,
even though, historically, Melchizedek was probably Shem. Additionally, Moses may have implied a soteric-
mistholic duality regarding Abraham’s faith in Gen 15:6 and Paul may have picked up on it in Rom 4:3,22. See my

sign and seal, plus the OT background, in addition to the timing of the circumcision, and the grammatical structure
Paul uses provide a precise understanding of what Paul means. He uses an inclusio so that the first reference to the
righteousness of the faith in v. 11a is equivalent to that in v. 13. The phrase is soteric-mistholic in both occurrences,
demanding that faith and righteousness be seen from both vantage points, as color coded above.

        By trying to break v. 12 up into dealing with two groups, Govett tried to make the pivot point halfway through
v. 12. The other soteric-mistholic interpreters in the chart did not break v. 12 up into two groups. Rather, they
recognized the break between the soteric and mistholic groups as occurring before v. 12 (so Eaton) or after v. 12
(so Dillow in DFD and me in TOD). I perceived the soteric-mistholic composite of v. 13 in TOD, with a mistholic
emphasis, but took v. 11 as simply being soteric. Upon further reflection herein, however, a soteric-mistholic blend
with mistholic emphasis is superior even for v. 11, depicting Abraham as the father of two groups of people: soteric
believers (v. 11b), and mistholic followers (v. 12). Therefore, the green in v. 11a is a composite of the blue in v. 11b
and the yellow in v. 12.
        Abram believed in God for eternal life before leaving Ur, before taking the first step of misthological faith.
Abram was seventy-five years old when he left Haran (Gen 12:4). The sign/seal of circumcision “of the faith which
he had while uncircumcised” at least covered his timespan from seventy-five to ninety-nine years old, thus the
twenty-four years of his sojourning in the land of promise until the point he was circumcised. (More likely, it covers
the time he left Ur at age 70 until his circumcision at age 99, thus 29 years.) Paul zooms in on the faith Abraham
had at the critical point of receiving circumcision and expounds upon it (Rom 4:14-25), thereby highlighting the
fact that Abraham’s misthological seal was a sign of his strong misthological faith. Abraham was sealed because of
his faith. This faith and sealing were mistholic.
        Abraham’s faith was mistholic at the point he was circumcised and normally would simply be colored yellow
in v. 13, which is how Dillow apparently takes it (the righteousness of the faith). Yet, by repeating the phrase as
an inclusio from (v. 11), where it would originally be blue (the righteousness of the faith) before having the yellow
applied from v. 12, Paul places the mistholic faith of v. 13 on top of the soteric faith of v. 11, like a building on a
foundation, thus resulting in a green coding combination (the righteousness of the faith). The yellow coding in
v. 13 is built upon the blue coding in v. 11, thus rendering a green composite in v. 13. Conversely, the blue coding
in v. 11 anticipates the yellow coding in v. 13, thus rendering a green composite in v. 11.
        The sign of circumcision he received at this point in time in v. 11 was a seal (1) of the righteousness of the
soteric faith he had before leaving Ur (thus qualifying him to be the soteric father of those who believe without
being circumcised so that righteousness might be soteriologically imputed to them) and (2) of the righteousness of
the subsequent mistholic faith he had in leaving Ur to go out and qualify for a misthological inheritance on Earth
and a heavenly inheritance in the Reward City (thus qualifying him to be the mistholic father of those who follow
in his steps of obedient faith). His seal of righteousness starts off being blue as you read the first statement of this
fatherhood and becomes yellow as you read the second statement regarding his fatherhood. These two statements
of his fatherhood are sandwiched in an inclusio formed by the phrase the righteousness of faith. The first inclusio
anticipates the blue and yellow and is composed by it. The second inclusio recaps it.138
138
    Abraham was seeking both land on Earth and a Heavenly City. Abraham’s physical descendants, the Jews,
forfeited their heavenly inheritance in the Reward City. This was taken away and given to his spiritual descendants,
the church. His obedient spiritual descendants will compose the Bride of Christ and inhabit the Heavenly City.

                           Illustration 31. Abraham’s First Sealed Misthological Step




       God made it clear that this covenant was conditioned on a blameless walk from that point in time for its
realization (Gen 17:1ff). Nevertheless, God also makes it clear that He would bring it about; it was only a question
of time (see TOD). In the process of time, Abraham’s misthologically sealed faith was eventually perfected when
he offered up Isaac (Jam 2:21-23). Although Abraham’s faith in Gen 17 was not his first step of misthological faith,
it was his first step of misthologically signed faith. Earlier in Genesis, the rainbow had been given to Noah as the
sign of the covenant between God and the Earth (Gen 9:12-17). Now, circumcision was being given as the sign of
the covenant between God and Abraham and his physical and misthological decedents (Gen 17:11). The first sign,
given to Noah, would protect the land from destruction. The second sign, given to Abraham, would qualify
Abraham’s decedents to inherit the land of promise. The first sign apparently foreshadows soteriological sealing,
while the second sign foreshadows misthological sealing. In any event, my illustration from TOD had to be amended
to point out that the first step of faith that Paul had in mind in Rom 4:12 was the step he had just mentioned in
Rom 4:11, which Paul expounds upon in the rest of that chapter. I have pictured this footstep in red in the above

However, the Jews will still qualify for their earthly inheritance in the Promised Land. Jointly, Abraham’s
descendants will be like both the sand of the seashore in inheriting the Earth and the stars of Heaven in inheriting
the heavenly realm (Gen 22:17).

illustration to denote the blood of Abraham’s circumcision. This covenant was inaugurated by his blood. Many
years later, Zipporah would call Moses a bridegroom of blood because of the circumcision she had to render on
their son to save Moses’ life (Ex 4:24-26). God is quite serious about this covenant and the blood it requires from
those submitting to it. Realization of the promised inheritance provided by this covenant is not free. It is mistholic.
But it is only truly applicable to those already in a covenant relationship with God, representing a soteric foundation.
Paul calls this sign a seal.

      11
        He received the sign of circumcision, a seal of THE RIGHTEOUSNESS OF THE FAITH

                                                     Color code:
                                                blue + yellow = green
                                            soteric, mistholic, soteric-mistholic

       Originally, I was going to color code the opening phrase of v. 11 as yellow, and thus purely mistholic: He
received the sign of circumcision, a seal. After all, this circumcision is certainly mistholic. However, several
considerations prevented me from doing so: (1) Grammatically, since this is a seal of what follows, it should be
color coded with the same color as what follows, not as a seal of the righteousness of the faith, but as a seal of
the righteousness of the faith. (2) The seal is contextually associated with both Abraham’s soteric and mistholic
fatherhood. Thus, this seal of his fatherhood should be marked as the composite color green. (3) The inclusio
suggests that the opening and closing should both be balanced with the same color and emphasis. They are like the
matching bottom and top of a hamburger bun, sandwiching the soteric-mistholic fatherhood in-between. (4) Pauline
usage of the seal elsewhere is best taken as having a composite green color, not an exclusively yellow color.
(5) Objections to the duality can be countered by noting that the duality does not confuse the gift foundation with
the subsequent rewarding walk. Rather, the duality perceives the misthological emphasis as resting upon an entirely
separate but essential soteriological foundation. This foundation-versus-building imagery is consistent with Pauline
thought: “According to the grace of God which was given to me, as a wise master builder I laid a foundation, and
another is building upon it” (1Cor 3:10; cp. Eph 2:19-22).
                                             Dual Seal in 2Tim 2:19
       When I first wrote SAS, I said, “We know of no cases where being sealed by God is linearly contingent upon
the action of the one who is sealed. Accordingly, I would have limited the seal in 2Tim 2:19 to soteriology and
highlighted it as follows.
                                Illustration 33. Simplistic Polarity for 2Tim 2:19-21
      19
         Nevertheless, the firm foundation of God stands, having this [A] seal, “The Lord knows those who
      are His,” and, [B] “Let everyone who names the name of the Lord abstain from wickedness.”
      20

                                                    Color code:
                                                    Blue, yellow
                                                  soteric, mistholic
Appendix 2: Soteric-Mistholic Seal                                                                             Page 69

I perceived no overlap, no green, for 2Tim 2:19-21 when I wrote SAS. Upon subsequent reflection, however, since
Paul intends a soteric-mistholic seal in Rom 4:11, one is left to ponder if he intends a misthological aspect to the
seal in 2Tim 2:19. The conjunction (and) and condition (if) certainly could be taken as leading to this conclusion:
                             Illustration 34. Simplistic Dual Option for 2Tim 2:19-21
      19
         Nevertheless, the firm foundation of God stands, having this [A] seal, [B] “The Lord knows those
      who are His,” and, [C] “Let everyone who names the name of the Lord abstain from wickedness.”
      20
         Now in a large house there are not only gold and silver vessels, but also vessels of wood and of
      earthenware, and some to honor and some to dishonor. 21 Therefore, if a man cleanses himself from
      these things, he will be a vessel for honor, sanctified, useful to the Master, prepared for every good
      work. (2Tim 2:19-21)

         One way to interpret the passage is to say that this (A) seal is a joint composite of (B) God’s ownership,
protection, and authentication and (C) God’s approval (A = B + C). Those whom the Lord knows as members
of His household include both (1) those of His children who do not abstain from wickedness and are therefore found
to be dishonorable vessels of wood and earthenware at the Bema (such as Hymenaeus, Philetus), and (2) those of
His children who abstain from wickedness and are therefore found to be honorable vessels of gold and silver at the
Bema (such as Timothy). The composite seal denotes both God’s seal of ownership (and thus unconditional
soteriological security) and the possibility of God’s seal of approval (and thus conditional misthological security).
NAC is correct to interpret 2Tim 2:20 as saying Paul “spoke of unworthy Christians, who were to be avoided. Paul
was suggesting that the church contained both faithful and unfaithful believers; some served for desirable ends, and
others accomplished shameful ends.” But NAC is inconsistent regarding 2Tim 2:19 when it erroneously asserts:
“Paul was effectively saying that Hymenaeus and Philetus did not belong to the people of God. God knew who his
true people were. Those who were truly God’s people would turn away from the false teaching in Ephesus.” NAC’s
comments on v. 20 refute its comments on v. 19. Consistency would indicate that Hymenaeus and Philetus were
unfaithful believers. If the (B) statement (the Lord knows those who are His) is soteric, as NAC believes, then the
proceeding context indicates their soteriological security. The straying of Hymenaeus and Philetus from the faith in
2Tim 2:17-18 would indicate that they are still in the house (God’s household) in 2Tim 2:20, but they will be vessels
of misthological dishonor.
         The objection will be raised, however, that the (B) statement (the Lord knows those who are His) is a quote
of Num 16:5 from the Korah rebellion, where Moses “spoke to Korah and all his company, saying, ‘Tomorrow
morning the Lord will show who is His’” (e.g., NICNT, NIGTC, WBC). Conditionalists, such as Marshall, wrongly
make this rebellion a soteriological matter and therefore picture the NT implementation of this statement as teaching
conditional soteriological security, and then they compound the error by erroneously concluding that the passage
has nothing to do with “salvation by works,” but is strictly by “grace alone,” despite the fact that they rightly
associate it with exerting the effort necessary to run the race and fight the good fight to win the crown (2Tim 4:7).139
Contrary to such muddled thinking, salvation by grace alone is not conditioned on you cleansing yourself from
dishonorable practices. Consequently, NIGTC, for example, is wrong to assert, “The statement quoted here affirms
God’s ability to differentiate between true and false believers and becomes here the reason for believing that God’s
foundation stands firm in the present situation.”
         To the contrary, securitists can certainly counter that Paul is alluding to the Korah rebellion and
acknowledge, if that is the case, that the B-statement pertains to God’s misthological seal of approval: “When Moses
heard this, he fell on his face; and he spoke to Korah and all his company, saying, ‘Tomorrow morning the Lord
will show [knows, LXX] who is His, and who is holy, and will bring him near to Himself; even the one whom He
will choose, He will bring near to Himself’” (Num 16:4-5). In both the OT and NT passage, the problem pertains
to in-house fighting among those who practice the C-statement, in that both groups name the name of the Lord. A
misthological rather than soteriological division is in view. At the base level, both groups qualify as belonging to
the Lord. But the passage does not stop at that level but is concerned about winning God’s misthological approval.
For this reason, Dillow interprets 2Tim 2:19 as referring to believers whom God knows “in the sense of finding

These people who went down alive into Sheol were saved by the blood of the Passover lamb. They had passed
through the waters of the Red Sea. Typologically, they represent believers. Historically, they probably were
believers in the promised Messiah. In any event, they should be treated as believers typologically. Jude concurs:
“Now I desire to remind you, though you know all things once for all, that the Lord, after saving a people out of the
land of Egypt, subsequently destroyed those who did not believe” (Jude 1:5). These believers were saved, then
subsequently destroyed because they did not believe that God had placed His misthological seal of approval on
Moses to elevate him above them. The soteriological correlation Jude draws in Jude 1:11 should not be equated
with a soteriological interpretation. Rather, Jude expects us to see a soteric-mistholic. As explained in TOD,

                As to the correlativity hypothesized between the judgment pronounced on demons and unfaithful
         believers, several passages indicate that some type of connection exists between the judgment of
         (1) Satan, demons, and unbelievers as compared to that of (2) unfaithful believers. In Jude 1:11-13,
         being destroyed in the rebellion of Korah is linked (not equated) with spending eternity in the black
         darkness forever. But the destruction that took place at Korah would be presumed to represent
         misthological destruction. Thus, misthological destruction is purposely linked with (but not equated
         with) soteriological destruction (Korah ≅ Hell; Korah ≠ Hell). Not only do these two verses supply this
         link in back-to-back conjunction, but Jude uses the destruction (apollumi) that took place after the
         people were saved from Egypt to depict the misthological outcome for believers who subsequently
         disbelieved after being saved out of Egypt (Jude 1:5), and he links this destruction with the
         soteriological outcome for unbelievers who never believed (Jude 1:11).
  •      The Lord, after saving a people out of the land of Egypt, subsequently destroyed [apollumi] those who
         did not believe. (Jude 1:5)
         The Lord saved them and then destroyed some of those whom He saved. One such occasion, in which
         He destroyed those whom He saved out of Egypt, was at the rebellion of Korah:

                                 Illustration 35. Salvation Linked with Destruction




             Dissimilarities must not be lost amidst similarities, however, in developing a principle of
      correlativity. In the study of typology, dissimilarities can be just as important as similarities. Jude is
      not saying that those saved typologically out of Egypt will be condemned soteriologically to the same
      black darkness as the unregenerate. Nevertheless, surely Jude intends for us to see a correlation between
      the misthological destruction that occurred at Korah and the soteriological destruction of the black
      darkness with which he links it. In addition, the plausible usage of the Enochian soteric outer darkness
      as a backdrop for the Matthean mistholic outer darkness has been noted already. Moreover, the fact that
      those cast into the outer darkness and the fires of eternal punishment both experience a weeping and
      gnashing of teeth is surely not incidental or coincidental, but neither is it identical (Mt 8:12; 13:42,50;
      22:13; 24:51; 25:30; Lk 13:28). Given this plausible Enochian linkage, the highly probable Judean
      linkage, and the provable verbal linkage made by Jesus, the position adopted herein is that some
      intentional mistholic-soteric correlation exists between the outer darkness and the black darkness.
      Although not identical, the darkness is applicable in some manner to both unbelievers and believers.

      Given this analysis from TOD, a better color-coding option is available for 2Tim 2:19-21 than the simplistic
options posed earlier in Illustration 33 and Illustration 34. Given its OT background, the phrase, the Lord knows
those who are His, is not a soteriological statement but a misthological statement and should be color coded as
follows.

    Soteric fire ≅ mistholic fire (Jude 1:6,23). Correlations can either be soteric-mistholic or mistholic-soteric. Peter

Nevertheless, although misthological security is conditional, it is a certainty for those who meet the condition. If
you qualify to be a golden vessel, then you can rest assured that the misthological aspect of God’s foundational
nature to reward those who seek Him will certainly apply to you. God has set His seal on this misthological certainty.
Contingency does not mean uncertainty for those who are meeting misthological requirements. They can know that
they will be vessels of honor. They do not bite their nails as they anticipate the Bema. Like Paul in Rom 8:33-39
(which is another soteric-mistholic passage),143 they can have unflinching misthological confidence as they face
their trials through the strength of the Spirit.
                                               Dual Seal in Eph 1:13-14
       Having now established that the sealing in both Rom 4:11 and 2Tim 2:19 is certainly soteric-mistholic, we
should seriously consider the possibility that the sealing in Eph 1:13-14 is dualistic as well. After all, since we are
sealed with the Spirit who is our earnest, linking the seal with the earnest is intuitive. If one component is soteric,
then the other would seem to be also. Conversely, if one component is mistholic, the other would seem to be as
well. Alternatively, if one is dualistic, then the other also would apparently be dualist. A diametrical relationship
would not be the most likely candidate. And a symmetrical relationship would be more likely than an asymmetrical
one.
                          Illustration 37. Soteric and Mistholic Options for Eph 1:13-14

            Interpreters             Soteric Mistholic Relationship of S to E
 1. Classical Caulian/Dillowian      S&E                   Symmetrical
 2. Neo-Dillowian/Hollandsworth S              E            Diametrical
 3. Neo-Caulian1 (neoclassical)      S&E E                Asymmetrical
 4. Neo-Caulian2                     S&E S&E               Symmetrical
 5. Chitwood                                   S&E         Symmetrical
                              S = seal; E = earnest
       The above chart summarizes the various options being explored in the present appendix. The first three were
explored in the previous appendix. The first interpretation is symmetrical: soteric seal—Spirit—soteric earnest. The
fifth interpretation is also symmetric: mistholic seal—Spirit—mistholic earnest. We have not yet explored that
possibility but will do so now. Primarily, by appealing to typology, in his book, Search for the Bride, Chitwood
argues that both the seal and earnest are exclusively mistholic.144 The sending of Abraham’s servant, Eliezer, to
secure a bride for Isaac in Gen 24:1-67 typologically represents the role of the Holy Spirit in securing a
misthological Bride for Isaac. I fully concur with Chitwood in that assessment and would highly recommend his
book as a profitable read. However, I would contend that Chitwood has overstated his case when he endeavors to
make bridal misthology the “singular purpose in view” (p. 17). Even his own typology mitigates against such
singularity. For example, the Spirit must procure the bride from her own family. Therefore, the creation of such a
family would be a soteric act by the Spirit. Furthermore, the maidens who accompany Rebecca from her own family,
riding on the camels with her back to Isaac, would denote soteric family members who do not qualify for mistholic
brideship. Riding on the camels marked them as her family members, nevertheless.145 “Then Rebekah arose with
her maids, and they mounted the camels and followed the man. So the servant took Rebekah and departed”
(Gen 24:61). Chitwood well remarks:

intended both vantage points (cp. Rom 8:17). See Dual Chain in Rom 8:29-30, 80.
