    At the moment of saving faith, believers are dualistically marked, soteriologically as family members and
misthologically as potential heirs of the kingdom. Realization of this misthological potentiality requires that they
fulfill the terms of bridal conditionality. Just as we were misthologically chosen in Eph 1:4 because of our punctiliar
soteriological faith, we were misthologically marked/sealed/identified in Eph 1:13 as potential heirs at the moment
of punctiliar soteriological faith.
Appendix 2: Soteric-Mistholic Seal                                                                                Page 73

                “Ten” is the number of ordinal completion in Scripture, and individuals riding upon “ten
         camels” at this time would show ordinal completion in relation to those going forth to meet Isaac.
         That is, they all went forth — not only Rebekah, but all within the scope of that shown by the number
         “ten.” But only one would be presented to Isaac as his bride, whom Abraham’s servant had procured,
         prepared, and removed.
                And it will be exactly the same when Christ’s bride is removed at the end of the dispensation.
         The bride will not depart alone. Rather, the bride will be accompanied by others, which Scripture
         clearly reveals will be all other Christians. (Emphasis his, 130.)
                          Illustration 38. Soteriological Sealing for a Misthological Purpose




        Chitwood astutely notes the misthological implications but fails to reckon the underlying soteriological
connection. Typologically, we could say that the camels represent soteric sealing. As a result of riding on the ten
camels, all the maidens reach the destination. Likewise, as a result of our soteriological sealing, all believers will
be raptured. Chitwood correctly observes that this rapture will be for all believers. Accordingly, he should have
deduced that camels represent a soteriological device, which I would identify with the Spirit’s sealing. Yet the
ultimate reason for soteriologically sealing all believers is to create a misthological Bride for Christ. Abraham’s
servant, Eliezer, was sent to fetch a bride for Isaac and brought back other maidens with her as an auxiliary means
to accomplish that bridal objective. The other maidens, obviously, were not the reason Eliezer took the camels and
went to Mesopotamia (Gen 24:10). Eliezer’s purpose for the trip and for the camels was to obtain the bride. The
other maidens were a collateral interest to the primary goal. We can likewise deduce that the mistholic Bride of
Christ is the reason for the soteric sealing. Misthologically securing the Bride is the primary objective.
Soteriologically securing all believers is a secondary, collateral necessity.146
     Even Chitwood bends the typology to accommodate the details. For example, he cites Gen 24:53, quotes the
first part of it, and yet rightly indicates that the Spirit offers the jewels indiscriminately to all believers: “The Spirit
takes this Word in His possession and opens the Word to an individual's understanding. He takes this Word and
spreads before Christians all the ‘jewels of silver, and jewels of gold, and raiment’” (p. 55). But that part of the
verse actually states, “And the servant brought out articles of silver and articles of gold, and garments, and gave
them to Rebekah.” These items were not offered to the family at large, though other precious items were given to
her brother and mother in the second half of the verse: “He also gave precious things to her brother and to her
mother.”
     Chitwood has taken the misthological earnest offered to Rebekah and treated it as though it is offered to all
believers. I do not fault him for doing so. However, his doing so shows the reasonableness of being flexible in one’s
typological implementation, allowing the antitype to help one interpret the type. Antitypes unlock the types.
Therefore, his argument for misthological exclusivity is typological suspect. Typologically, the Holy Spirit is very
active in performing some of the same actions on the soteric family as He is on the mistholic bride. Chitwood has
failed to notice that the creation of this body by the Spirit would necessarily be typologically implied by Abraham’s
servant being sent to her family, giving gifts to her family, and bringing part of her family back with her. Typology
suggests duality. Chitwood is correct not to limit the offer of the earnest to the Bride. However, the typology/type
does not make the offer of the earnest to all believers explicit. The initial offer of the earnest as being applicable to
all believers is a necessary inference drawn from the antitype. The typology/type is focused on the misthological
realization of the earnest as being applicable only to the Bride.

146
      See discussion of collateral love in DLF.
Page 74                                                                                     Dual Sealing and Security
     Hence, only Rebekah’s camel is denoted with the word earnest being displayed in full visibility in Illustration
38. The earnest is not fully visible in relation to the other camels since only through the implications of the antitype
does it become apparent that the earnest is initially applicable to all believers potentially. The soteriological nature
of the camels, on the other hand, is rather easily discerned. The soteriological nature of the earnest is more difficult
to discern, given its emphasis on misthology. Thus, the Neo-Dillowian proposal, also adopted by Hollandsworth, is
understandable. Yet Chitwood’s perception is more accurate in discerning that both the seal and earnest should be
treated similarly in scope. However, they need not be treated equally in emphasis. The seal is soteric-mistholic; the
earnest is soteric-mistholic. The difference in emphasis is symmetrical, though. Duality, rather than exclusivity, is
symmetrically implied when the type is interpreted in light of the antitype.
       Logically, Chitwood’s argument for exclusivity is suspect as well. He argues that soteriological salvation
“remains completely unaffected by God’s various works with man throughout the different dispensations” (emphasis
his, 20). In the beginning, soteriological life was imparted by God’s breath (Gen 2:7). Sealing had nothing to do
with it. Thus, Chitwood argues that sealing could not be added as an additional soteriological measure later. Perhaps,
but the proof is in the pudding, as they say, and we will find reasons to question his assumption as we proceed. His
argument that the Spirit’s convicting the world of sin, righteousness, and judgment in Jn 16:7-11 is exclusively
mistholic is not particularly persuasive. Thankfully, though, he takes the world in Jn 3:16 to “encompass all those
in the world. God gave His Son for all” (p. 35). Given that soteriological foundation, why not take world
dualistically in Jn 16:7-11 also? After all, Chitwood acknowledges the dualistic (soteric-mistholic) need of washing
in Jn 13:8-10 (pp. 39, 192). Chitwood reminds me of a Calvinist arguing for limited atonement when he says that
the “whole world” in 1Jn 2:1-2 is just referring to believers (p. 40), a proposition which I would reject coming from
a Calvinist. Likewise, Chitwood’s argument that righteousness in that Johannine passage is exclusively experiential
is specious. Dualistically allowing soteriological imputed righteousness in addition to experiential righteousness
would be just as valid an option. And trying to limit judgment in that context to just the Bema for believers is
strained as well.
       Chitwood’s basic rationale is that there is nothing new under the sun soteriologically, so the sealing cannot
be soteriological since it is new. Yet his argument falters because he acknowledges that believers are made a new
man in Christ (p. 66; Eph 2:12-15). We are a new creation (p. 67; 2Cor 5:17). We are a new soteriological entity,
the body of Christ (p. 68), formed from both Jews and Gentiles by our immersion (baptism) into the Spirit (p. 67).
We have a new positional standing in Christ (p. 68).

             The key expression is “in Christ.” It is “in Christ” that old things have passed away. If the
      individual was a Jew before being immersed in the Spirit, placing him “in Christ,” then he is no
      longer a Jew. He has become a new creation “in Christ.” If the individual was a Gentile before being
      immersed in the Spirit, placing him “in Christ,” then he is no longer a Gentile. Rather, he (the
      believing Gentile), as the one who was previously a Jew, has become a new creation “in Christ.”
      And “in Christ” there is “neither Jew nor Greek,” but one new man. (Italics his, 67.)

Chitwood fails to acknowledge that these factors prove that the sealing by the Spirit would be a new soteriological
work as well. Instead, he oddly concludes the opposite:

             Ephesians 1:13,14 deals mainly with a work of the Spirit which occurs at the time of man's
      salvation — an immersion in the Spirit (cf. Matt. 3:11; Acts 1:5), referred to as a sealing with the
      Spirit in Eph. 1:13. And this work of the Spirit, though occurring at the time of man’s salvation, has
      nothing to do with man’s salvation. It is a work of the Spirit subsequent to salvation. (Italics his, 72.)

He admits that the immersion in Spirit is referred to as a sealing, which would necessarily be soteriological and thus
color coded as blue, and he yet asserts that it has nothing to do with soteriology! To the contrary, since the immersion
in the Spirit is the new soteriological means by which we are made part of the body of Christ at the moment of
regeneration and since this immersion is “referred to as a sealing with the Spirit,” the sealing with the Spirit must
be a new soteriological event that occurs at the moment of soteriological salvation. Chitwood’s own line of
reasoning requires this deduction. He acknowledges that the immersion is new but tries to circumvent its
soteriological import by making it separate from soteriological salvation: “But the immersion in the Spirit is
something peculiar to the present dispensation, which is separate from salvation by grace” (emphasis his, 72). He
Appendix 2: Soteric-Mistholic Seal                                                                            Page 75
acknowledges that it is something new (i.e., “peculiar to the present dispensation”) and then tries to separate it from
soteriology because it is new. Immersion in the Spirit is new, yes, but it is not separate from the soteriological
dimension. His own analysis requires that immersion be considered a soteriological event since it places us in Christ
and makes us a new creation in Christ.
       His argument against a soteriological dimension to the sealing is thus seriously flawed. The hypothesis that I
am willing to entertain, however, is that sealing is for a misthological purpose, namely, that we might realize a
misthological inheritance and redemption. That aspect of Chitwood’s argument is plausible. Yet, for various
reasons, his own analysis requires that his misthological exclusivity must be rejected. For example, he goes on to
state: “And it is a simple matter to see that this work of the Spirit, peculiar to the present dispensation, can have
nothing to do with salvation by grace. If it did, something would have changed at the beginning of the dispensation
relative to salvation by grace” (emphasis his, 73). To the contrary, the typology indicates that, when it came time
for God to create Eve (from Adam’s body to form his bridal body) and when it came time for Rebekah to form
Isaac’s bridal body from her family, the body from which the bridal body was composed would first have to be
created by the Spirit. A soteriological foundation was necessary upon which to build a misthological superstructure.
A soteriological aspect to the sealing is thus implied typologically. In any event, regardless of the typological
implications, the soteriological foundation to the sealing is demanded exegetically, as Chitwood unwittingly
concedes, yet again, when commenting subsequently on the seal in Eph 1:13:

             Or, in chapter two [Eph 2:13], this sequence of the Spirit’s work is expressed another way:
      Those “made nigh by the blood of Christ” find themselves, through being sealed with the Spirit from
      chapter one, positionally “in Christ” (v. 13). And “in Christ,” where there is “neither Jew nor Greek”
      (Gal 3:26, 28), “the middle wall of partition” (previously existing between Jew and Greek) has been
      broken down. “In Christ” there now exists one new man, in which a “middle wall of partition” cannot
      exist (Eph. 2:14,15). (Italics his, 84).

Soteriologically, this sealing has a positional function in making us a soteriological new man from which the Bride
will be misthologically formed. The soteriological nature of this seal is glaringly apparent even in Chitwood’s own
statements:

             Christians have been sealed with the promised Spirit in connection with becoming a part of
      the one new man “in Christ.” Thus, the seal would involve being brought into the position which
      God requires (becoming Abraham’s seed, etc....). And the seal would show God's stamp of
      approval relative to identification and ownership. The seal would confirm, attest, authenticate, and
      certify that everything was in order for the Spirit to conduct His search for the bride among those
      forming the one new man. (Italics his, 85.)

              This immersion in the Spirit places the saved individual “in Christ,” forming an entirely
      new creation (II Cor. 5:17). This occurs at the time that the Spirit breathes life into the one having no
      life, though subsequent to and separate from this initial work of the Spirit. (Italics his, 119.)

              And the Spirit finds these individuals throughout succeeding generations, covering the entire
      two millenniums. Individuals in each succeeding generation, through the immersion in the Spirit,
      continue to be added to the one new man, allowing the Spirit to continue His search for the bride.
      (Italics his, 120-121.)

Such statements demand a soteriological understanding for the sealing. The sealing is used in soteriological
connection with us becoming a new man/creation in Christ and shows God’s soteriological approval
regarding His soteriological ownership. The soteriological-foundational understanding of the sealing cannot
be denied. However, we may concede to Chitwood that this soteric sealing has a misthological purpose.
Chitwood’s argument would have been much better served if he had noted the duality of the sealing. Even
though his arguments against the soteriological nature of the seal are hereby rejected as being inconsistent
with his own analysis, his affirmation of the misthological intention of the seal is herein entertained:
Page 76                                                                                        Dual Sealing and Security
               This sealing work of the Spirit, being a pledge (Gk., appabon), has to do with the thought of a
       first installment or down payment. Christians, through this means, now possess a legal claim to the
       inheritance; and a first installment portends full payment at a future date. This guarantees that the
       inheritance will one day be received.
               A realization of the inheritance though is connected with a redemption; and this redemption,
       along with the inheritance, is future (Eph. 4:30). The past work of the Spirit forms a pledge that the
       inheritance will be realized, but only in connection with a future redemption of the inheritance.
               This future redemptive work is clearly connected with the inheritance. And, as previously seen
       at the beginning of this chapter, the redemption of the inheritance is fully dealt with in Revelation
       chapters five through nineteen, drawing from a number of the Old Testament types. Inheriting with
       Christ involves a territory over which Christ and His co-heirs will reign (Ps. 2:7-9). And this
       inheritance has to do with the earth and all that is therein, presently under Satan’s rule and control,
       requiring a redemptive process.
               Though the Spirit will complete the work in and among Christians which He has set out to
       perform (Phil. 1:6), Christians, through faithfulness or unfaithfulness, can either realize or forfeit the
       awaiting inheritance with God’s Son. (Emphasis his, 86.)

       Chitwood’s seal-equals-pledge-equals-misthology equality merely needs to be amended to a seal-equals-
pledge-inclusive-misthology duality. This part of his argument is attractive. Still, his importing a misthological
nature into the seal leaves a glaring question unaddressed. If believers are unfaithful, do they lose the Spirit? After
all, according to Chitwood, God gave us the partial misthological payment with the intention of our meeting the
requirements in order to receive the full misthological payment. If we fail to meet those requirements, thus failing
to obtain the full payment, does this mean that we forfeit the partial payment? Conditionalists will scream, “Yes!”
Securitists, such as myself, respond just as strongly, “No!” Accordingly, I have presented a very strong case for
soteriological security in relation to the soteriological nature of the seal in the first part of this book. However, if
the seal and pledge have a misthological dimension, then the question of our losing the Spirit as our seal and down
payment must be reexamined from a misthological framework. Unfortunately, Chitwood fails to address this
question, thereby throwing a monkey wrench into his whole argument. Yet various portions of his writings can be
pieced together to salvage the misthological aspect of his argument.

              The parable of the ten virgins in Matt. 25:1-13 deals centrally with this same overall issue, with
       “oil” used symbolically in the parable in exactly the same manner as it is used in the Old Testament.
       All of the virgins possessed oil, but only the five wise virgins possessed an extra supply of oil. And
       when they were called to an accounting — at “midnight,” — only the five wise virgins were allowed
       to enter into the marriage festivities with the Bridegroom (vv. 6ff).
              That would be to say, in the antitype, all Christians possess the Holy Spirit. He indwells every
       Christian. But not every Christian has the extra supply of Oil. Not every Christian is filled with the
       Holy Spirit. And when Christians are called to an accounting — at “midnight” — only those filled
       with the Spirit will be allowed to enter into the marriage festivities with the Bridegroom. (Emphasis
       his, 192-193.)

       Securitists will allow that unfaithful believers can lose the Holy Spirit, as pictured in the parable, in the sense
of not having the spiritual reserves necessary to be misthologically qualified to participate in the marriage festivities.
All church age believers are indwelt by the Spirit (in their spirits), but they are not all filled with the Spirit (in their
souls). Losing the filling of the Spirit can be pictured as losing the Spirit in relation to the soul. In Mt 25:1-13, the
oil/Spirit can run out, representing the lack of any extra supply of oil, namely, lacking the filling of the Spirit in the
soul. Likewise, believers can be temporally thrown away from Christ and misthologically cast away from Christ
like dried branches (Jn 15:6). Believers can be experientially severed from Christ (Gal 5:4). They can be
misthologically cast into the outer darkness away from Christ (Mt 8:12; 22:13; 25:30). Misthologists are well
acquainted with the duality in which it is possible to affirm that nothing can separate us from Christ soteriologically,
yet unfaithfulness can separate us from Christ temporally and misthologically. Therefore, posing that believers can
lose the Spirit in one sense but not the other is reasonable.
       Curious as to whether Chitwood would concur with that proposal, I searched the Scripture indexes of over
thirty of his books for his discussion of Rom 8:9: “However, you are not in the flesh but in the Spirit, if indeed the
Appendix 2: Soteric-Mistholic Seal                                                                              Page 77
Spirit of God dwells in you. But if anyone does not have the Spirit of Christ, he does not belong to Him.” This is
the classic text demonstrating that having the Spirit is a soteriological issue, at least in the present dispensation.
Unfortunately, I came up empty. Apparently, Chitwood has failed to address this text, at least directly, in his
discussion. Yet this text, as typically understood in dispensational circles, would be highly detrimental to his thesis
that nothing new has transpired soteriologically in the present dispensation. Having the Spirit dwell in all believers
soteriological is typically considered a new development, frequently associated with their being sealed with the
Spirit. Thus, I included this text in my discussion of the sealing in the present book. Plus, classic texts showing that
OT believers could lose the Spirit have also been considered: (1) “Now the Spirit of the Lord departed from Saul,
and an evil spirit from the Lord terrorized him” (1Sam 16:14). (2) Do not cast me away from Your presence, and
do not take Your Holy Spirit from me” (Ps 51:11).
        Chitwood, however, failed to discuss Rom 8:9 in his denial of the soteriological implications of the sealing.
The only way to counter having the Spirit dwelling in believers as something new would be to pose that believers
within all dispensations have had the Spirit dwelling in their spirit. Some believe that to be the case. Securitists
would argue that what was at stake was losing the anointing of the Spirit rather than losing the Spirit Himself. So I
looked through Chitwood’s Scripture indexes to find what he believes about OT believers losing the Holy Spirit.
He takes the standard dispensational framework:

             The common experience peculiar to all who were empowered by the Holy Spirit in the Old
      Testament was the Spirit coming upon rather than indwelling them. The Holy Spirit came upon and
      empowered individuals for service, but there was the ever-present danger that through disobedience
      the Holy Spirit would be removed. This occurred in Saul’s life because of his disobedience
      (I Sam.15:1-26; 16:13,14), and this is what David feared following not only his sin with Bathsheba
      but his subsequently having her husband slain when he prayed, “...take not thy Holy Spirit from me”
      (Ps. 51:11).147 (Italics his.)

Although OT believers could not lose the spiritual life imparted by the Spirit, they could lose the Holy Spirit. I kept
reading since Chitwood was providing this as background information for his discussion of the foolish virgins as
being church-age believers whose lamps were running out of oil/Spirit. He provides the standard dispensational
response:

              Conditions pertaining to the reception of the Holy Spirit during the present dispensation
      are not the same as those pertaining to the reception of the Holy Spirit during the past
      dispensation. During Old Testament days the Holy Spirit came upon individuals; but today,
      rather than coming upon individuals, the Spirit indwells and fills them.
              The indwelling of the Holy Spirit occurs at the time an individual believes on the Lord Jesus
      Christ, at the point of salvation (Eph. 1:13,14; I Cor. 3:16). Individuals being filled with the Spirit
      though is a different matter entirely. Christians, those indwelt by the Spirit, are commanded to "be
      filled with the Spirit" (Eph. 5:18). This is a separate work of the Spirit following His initial work at the
      point of salvation.
              The experience of Christians relative to the Holy Spirit during the present dispensation is in
      perfect keeping with the parable of the ten virgins. All ten had lamps with oil, but only five possessed
      an extra supply of oil. That is to say, all Christians are in possession of the Holy Spirit, but only some
      are filled with the Holy Spirit. Only some have the extra portion of oil, as in the parable.148 (Italics
      his.)


147
    Arlen L. Chitwood, Prophecy on Mount Olivet, revised edition (Norma, OK: The Lamp Broadcast, Inc., 2011),
192.
148
    Ibid., 193. This would have been a good place for Chitwood to also cite Rom 8:9 as supporting his position that
the Spirit indwells all believers today, that is, in the present NT dispensation, not in previous dispensations. Jn 14:17
and Jn 16:17 should also be added as collaborating texts. Accordingly, this is a new soteric benefit. Thus, Chitwood
is wrong to say that nothing new was introduced soteriologically in the NT. Consequently, he is mistaken in his
assertion that the seal could not be soteric just because it would be a new soteriological benefit.
Page 78                                                                                        Dual Sealing and Security

His statement here destroys his argument against soteriological sealing discussed above. Here, he acknowledges
that the indwelling of the Spirit is a new phenomenon. Moreover, he admits that it is associated with Eph 1:13-14!
NT believers do not lose the indwelling of the Spirit; they only lose the filling of the Spirit. Thus, this indwelling is
soteriological and is associated with being sealed with the Spirit and given the Spirit as a pledge in Eph 1:13-14.
Obviously, Chitwood is being inconsistent when he denies the soteriological dimension of the seal and pledge.
However, Chitwood’s typological contribution to the discussion is certainly not without value. He is correct to stress
the misthological nature of the typological background. The seal has a soteriological function, but it is given,
ultimately, for a misthological purpose, which may or may not be realized for all believers.
        Since I could not find where Chitwood had discussed Rom 8:9, I read his commentary on Jude 1:19 since he
would consider it a related text. He believes that both texts are talking about believers having or not having the
Spirit. The thesis of his commentary is that the apostates being described in Jude 1:19 were mature believers who
departed from the faith. Let’s compare the two texts:

           •   But you are not in the flesh but in the Spirit, if indeed the Spirit of God dwells in you. Now if anyone
               does not have the Spirit of Christ, he is not His. (Rom 8:9; NKJ)
           •   These are sensual persons, who cause divisions, not having the Spirit. (Jude 1:19; NKJ)

As a securitist who takes both texts as describing NT believers, Chitwood must provide an explanation that would
reconcile an obvious discrepancy.

               The thought in this passage is not at all that the Spirit of God does not indwell these
       individuals, for He indwells every believer; and these are “apostates” — believers who have stood
       away from “the faith.” The thought centers around the fact that here are individuals who, within their
       apostasy, now “mock” the things of God. They follow the ways of the man of flesh and the Spirit of
       God has nothing to do with the entire matter. The leaven has done its work, everything is alien to the
       Spirit's control, and these apostates are completely estranged from the Spirit’s work in their lives.
               (An unsaved person, unlike a saved person, is spiritually dead. He is no more in |a position to
       act in the spiritual realm, understand spiritual truth, than a physically dead person is in a position to
       move about in the physical realm.
               A similar situation exists between Christians who have an understanding of the Word of the
       Kingdom and the apostates existing among these Christians [along with numerous other Christians in
       the Laodicean Church of today who would not be considered true apostates but still have no
       understanding of, and often have an aversion to, the message concerning the coming kingdom of
       Christ].
               One group is spiritually attuned to the Word of the Kingdom, and they can not only understand
       the message but they can experience spiritual growth through a continued study of the message. The
       other group though is completely estranged from the Spirit’s work in this respect. And, relative to the
       Word of the Kingdom, they find themselves in a similar position, spiritually, to that of the unsaved
       person. Their spiritual senses either remain or have become so dulled and deadened that they cannot
       understand any more about the message than an unsaved person can understand about it [cf.
       Heb. 5:13,14]. (Italics and inserts his.)

       In Chitwood’s assessment, the Spirit indwells these apostate believers, but they do not have the Spirit in
another sense since they are estranged from the Spirit. Chitwood, therefore, poses a duality. There is a sense in
which all believers have the Spirit and another sense in which all believers do not have the Spirit. Although I did
not concur with his attempt to limit Jude as dealing exclusively with regenerate apostates in TOD, I did allow
correlative applicability to regenerate apostates. One might even consider intentional duality on the part of Jude. In
any case, regardless of the particulars, securitists can agree that all believers have the Spirit in a soteriological sense,
but not all believers have the Spirit in an experiential sense. This is but a hair’s breadth away from acknowledging
that unfaithful believers can lose the Holy Spirit in a misthological sense. Frankly, it only makes sense that unfaithful
believers will lose out on misthological intimacy with the Spirit and the empowerment of the Spirit that they could
have had otherwise. Intimacy with the Father and the Son is conditional (see DLF). Surely, we must conclude that
Appendix 2: Soteric-Mistholic Seal                                                                            Page 79
intimacy with the Spirit is also conditional (cp. Eph 4:30). We certainly know that His empowerment is conditional.
Therefore, typology would suggest that just as David feared to lose the regal anointing of the Spirit, NT believers
can lose the sealing of the Spirit as it relates to their having God’s stamp of approval as being qualified to inherit
the kingdom as kingdom rulers. If the sealing has anything to do with being qualified to be in the running as a
potential heir of the kingdom, and surely it does, then those who fail the race must lose that regal aspect of the
sealing. They could lose the Spirit as misthological seal/earnest but not as a soteriological seal/pledge. It could be
that the former is the exclusive intent of the arrabon here (Eph 1:14) and the latter is the exclusive intent elsewhere
(2Cor 1:22; 5:5) so that both sets of texts would be necessary to demonstrate the duality of arrabon. Yet, given that
it has been proven that Paul intends a soteric-mistholic duality for the seal in other singular texts (Rom 4:11;
2Tim 2:19), posing a pledge-earnest duality for the arrabon here is reasonable as well, especially considering the
soteriological and misthological richness of its text and context.
                                         Dual Inheritance and Redemption
       Considering the current hypothesis of duality in Eph 1:13-14, the richness of the text concerning the
inheritance and redemption in v. 14 needs to be re-examined. Paul was ambiguous. Some translations move left
and right into the realm of interpretation in trying to remove his ambiguity.
                             Illustration 39. Three Types of Translations of Eph 1:14

      1. Our Possession                         2. Ambiguous                        3. God’s Possession
 Who is the guarantee of our            Who is the guarantee of our             Who is the guarantee of our
 inheritance until the redemption       inheritance until the redemption        inheritance, until the redemption
 of our possession, to the praise       of the possession, to the praise of     of God's possession, to the praise
 of His glory.                          His glory.                              of His glory.

Translations tend to fall into these three types of categories regarding whose possession is in view. I have translated
them similarly so that the key differences might be focused upon. Most translations falling into the first category
cheat by translating the noun as a verb.149 I am not doing so. The beauty of Paul’s ambiguity is best seen in Greek.
English just does not suffice; nevertheless, a wooden English translation is also provided
                                      Illustration 40. Greek Text for Eph 1:14




149
    For example, the ESV translates the noun redemption as the verb acquire: “Who is the guarantee of our
inheritance until we acquire possession of it, to the praise of his glory.” We are the ones who must acquire/purchase
the inheritance. This would make little sense if God were perceived as making a down payment. The purchaser
would be the one expected to make the down payment. Thus, the ESV wisely translates arrabon as guarantee. So
does the RSV: guarantee...acquire. Conversely, if we adopt the translation of pledge instead of down payment, the
imagery of God giving us pledge for our purchasing the inheritance may seem rather strange. However, Eliezer
gave the jewels to Rebekah as a pledge so that she would sojourn to acquire the inheritance (Gen 24:53). And these
jewels can be pictured as an engagement ring. So a misthological understanding of the pledge has precedence.
Page 80                                                                                       Dual Sealing and Security

        The possessive article (woodenly translated as of the) is easily rendered into English in the first phrase
because Paul specifies whose inheritance and whose praise he is talking about—ours versus His (i.e., God’s). Paul
is talking about our inheritance in the first phrase. But Paul sandwiches the middle phrase in-between and does not
specify if he is talking about our possession or His possession. Is Paul talking about the redemption of our
possession, namely, our inheritance—thus the redemption of our inheritance, as the first translation category would
take it?150 Or is Paul talking about the redemption of us as God’s possession (ultimately as His inheritance; cp. Eph
1:18), as the third translation category would take. This study in duality suggests that Paul is masterfully using
ambiguity to affirm both possibilities, as the second group of translations would take it. Paul affirms both God’s
soteriological possession of us and our misthological inheritance of the kingdom. This possession is ours-His.
Rather than adopt the interpretive translations on the left or right, I would opt for a combined interpretive translation
as being superior: “Who is the guarantee of our inheritance until the redemption of our joint possession, to the
praise of His glory” (TM). This interpretive translation captures Paul’s thought better than the other two alternatives
and is even superior to the ambiguous literal English translations in conveying the implicit mutuality implied by the
Greek duality.
        Govett’s interpretation of Eph 1:14 is on target in that he explains it in terms of the duality of the inheritance:
“There are two parts in the heritage of the believer. (1) The gift of the inheritance, which is certain, and which is
eternal....(2) ‘The reward of the inheritance,’ or the prize of the millennial kingdom....It is citizenship in the heavenly
city” (emphasis his).151 Adopting this joint-inheritance perspective in Eph 1:14 allows us to color code it accordingly
as green and to perceive Paul as saying that the Holy Spirit is “the guarantee of our inheritance until the
redemption of our joint possession” (TM). Understandably, therefore, Paul warns: “And do not grieve the Holy
Spirit of God, by whom you were sealed for the day of redemption” (Eph 4:30). Then, a few verses later, Paul
clarifies that what is at stake in grieving the Holy Spirit is the inheritance in the kingdom. “For this you know with
certainty, that no immoral or impure person or covetous man, who is an idolater, has an inheritance in the
kingdom of Christ and God” (Eph 5:5). The reward aspect of the inheritance is at stake (cp. Col 3:23). Receiving
the full inheritance is conditioned on being filled with the Spirit, which is impossible if one is grieving the Spirit.
Those believers who grieve the Holy Spirit lose the intimacy with Him and the empowerment from Him that they
could have had otherwise—both in this life and the next. Nevertheless, they do not lose the life imparted by the
Spirit to their spirit. In that respect, they will always have the Spirit within them.
                                              Dual Chain in Rom 8:29-30
        As to the dual nature of the inheritance, as is well-known in misthological circles, Paul combines both aspects
in one verse: “If children, then heirs also: on the one hand, heirs of God, but on the other hand, co-heirs with
Christ, if indeed we co-suffer with Him in order that we may also be co-glorified with Him” (Rom 8:17; TM).
If you put blue heirship and yellow heirship together, you get green heirship, which is what Paul has done. The
context of this famous verse has soteriological and misthological themes interwoven and culminates in a dualistic
crescendo. Due to a textual variant, Rom 8:1 is frequently misperceived as being simply soteriological, but the
context supports the MT reading, which includes the second half of the verse, thereby underscoring its dual nature:
“There is therefore now no condemnation to those who are in Christ Jesus, who do not walk according to the
flesh, but according to the Spirit” (NKJV). All believers are in Christ soteriologically. But only those believers
who walk according to the Spirit are without condemnation, which sets the stage for a misthological conclusion.
Still, soteriological elements are foundational. A verse already noted as important in our discussion regarding the
sealing of the Spirit is Rom 8:9: “However, you are not in the flesh but in the Spirit, if indeed the Spirit of God

150
    Insecuritists employee the first option to equate our inheritance with our redemption and make it conditioned on
our performance. However, if one wishes to take this first option, consult Chitwood’s discussion in Search. He
argues from typology that the redemption of the inheritance is a misthological issue. His misthological exclusivity
is certainly better than the soteriological-misthological singularity offered by insecuritists. Also, his model provides
helpful typological reasoning for the misthological aspect of the inheritance. Nevertheless, my preference is for a
securitist-misthologist duality, so I will not pursue Chitwood’s discussion any further at this juncture. The Pauline
material suffices to make my point.

    Robert Govett, Govett on Ephesians, third printing (Hayesville, NC: Schoettle Publishing Co., 2010), 34-35. My
other writings may be consulted for discussion of the Reward City, that is, misthopology (e.g., TOD, LTPW,
MMP4). For defense of the eternal nature of rewards, see RAE.
Appendix 2: Soteric-Mistholic Seal                                                                              Page 81
dwells in you. But if anyone does not have the Spirit of Christ, he does not belong to Him.” Though Paul does
not explicitly say that we are sealed in Christ by the Spirit as he does in Eph 1:13, he is certainly expressing the
same concept. Rom 8:9 is a conceptual parallel to Eph 1:13. Unbelievers are in the sphere of the flesh and devoid
of the Spirit. Believers are soteriologically in the sphere of the Spirit and indwelt by the Spirit. In Rom 8:10, all
believers are soteriologically in the sphere of Christ. In Rom 8:11, the Spirit soteriologically dwells in all believers.
Conversely, in Rom 8:13, only those believers who live by the Spirit really live. In Rom 8:14, only those believers
who are led by the Spirit are misthologically sons of God. Yet in Rom 8:16, all believers are soteriologically children
of God. The soteriological-misthological duality is clearly summarized in Rom 8:17, as noted. Whether Rom 8:18-
25 is soteriological or misthological is open to debate. Soteriological terms seem to encompass a misthological
range. In any case, Rom 8:28 certainly seems to focus on faithful believers: “And we know that God causes all
things to work together for good to those who love God, to those who are called according to His purpose.”
Opinions vary as to whether loving God is merely a general description or a necessary condition. If the former
postulation is correct, then God causes all thing to work together for all believers, even those who do not love Him.
If the latter position is correct, this promise only applies to believers who love God.
                                  Illustration 41. Golden Chain for Rom 8:29-30




       This raises a question about those called in Rom 8:29-30. What do these options about those who are called
in Rom 8:28 imply for the those who are called in the golden chain that follows in Rom 8:29-30? FG interpreters
are divided on the issue. Four basic options are available.
                         Illustration 42. Option 1: Soteriological Chain for Rom 8:29-30

                         Illustration 43. Option 2: Misthological Chain for Rom 8:29-30

those called are those who love God, rather than all believers in general, then a misthological calling is more

Society, 2013), 238-242.
Page 82                                                                                     Dual Sealing and Security
                           Illustration 44. Option 3: Composite Chain for Rom 8:29-30




       In TOD, the soteriological and misthological vantage points were both affirmed as being valid. If the calling
is misthological, then a misthological predestination might be entailed as well. Thus, a composite option was
entertained in TOD, taking those two links as misthological and the rest of the chain as soteriological. In this way,
the chain could be regarded as an amplification of both parts of Rom 8:17, not just the misthological latter half of
the verse. Misthological links could be found within the soteriological chain. What must be carefully maintained in
this case is that the breaking of the misthological links does not break the soteriological implications.
                            Illustration 45. Three-Link Soteric Chain for Rom 8:29-30




       The problem with this proposal from TOD is that it is not immediately apparent how the soteriological aspect
of the chain would remain intact if the misthological links were broken. Therefore, the duality might be better
expressed with another model.
                              Illustration 46. Option 3: Dual Chain for Rom 8:29-30




       In more recent years, I have developed the theory of duality more so and would be inclined simply to take all
the links in the golden chain as dualistic. The whole chain has soteriological applicability, and the whole chain has
misthological applicability. Paul intends for us to see how his chain of thought has dual applicability. The point that
must be carefully maintained in this dualistic model is that we are not dealing with one chain but two chains, or we
are dealing with two different vantage points of the same chain. Either articulation allows us to avoid confusing or
fusing the gift aspect with the reward aspect.
                          Illustration 47. Transparent Vantage Points for Golden Chain




       Picture overlaying a golden chain with a blue filter or a yellow filter. If you overlap both filters, you have a
joint green vantage point. Some FG interpreters are seeing the passage through the blue filter, some through the
yellow filter, some through both. In the latter case, Paul is perceived as saying in Rom 8:39 that nothing will separate
any believer from the soteric love of God in Christ and, furthermore, nothing will separate any faithful believer
from the mistholic love of God in Christ.
Appendix 2: Soteric-Mistholic Seal                                                                               Page 83
                                        Illustration 48. Duality of God’s Love
             Conditionally Loved in Christ Unconditionally and Conditionally Loved in Christ




       In TOD, the above spherical illustrations were used to summarize passages teaching two different aspects of
God’s love. Believers are sealed in God’s love. The sealing on the left is conditional. The right side of the illustration
encompasses that sphere of sealing within a larger sphere of sealing. The larger sphere is soteric and thus
unconditional. In the smaller circle, believers have a joint responsibility to keep themselves in God’s love
misthologically. God has the exclusive responsibility to keep believers in His love soteriologically. Even if they
step out of God’s love misthologically, they remain in God’s love soteriologically. See TOD for further discussion
of that dual sealing regarding God’s love.
                                       Illustration 49. Duality of Spirit’s Seal
   Unconditionally Sealed by Spirit                   Unconditionally and Conditionally Sealed by Spirit
Page 84                                                                                      Dual Sealing and Security
       Similarly, in Eph 1:14, we likewise see a dual sealing regarding the inheritance. However, the relationship is
pictured inversely in this illustration as opposed to the previous illustration. The unconditional soteriological sealing
of our trichotomous spirit is pictured in blue on the left by a golden seal of the Holy Spirit. This soteriological
sealing cannot be undone, and we cannot lose the Holy Spirit from our spirit. Pertaining to the Holy Spirit being
given to us to assure us of reaching Heaven, we cannot lose or return this gift. For reasons discussed in the first two
chapters of the present book, the ministry of the Holy Spirit given to us as a gift to assure us of soteriological
entrance into the kingdom cannot be undone or returned. However, as noted in the two appendixes, this
unconditional sphere can be placed inside the yellow conditional sphere on the right. The sealing associated with
this outer sphere is represented by a golden engagement ring. This misthological sealing is conditional. The sealing
of the Holy Spirit in this capacity can be lost. Pertaining to the Holy Spirit being given to us to enlighten and
empower us so that we can qualify as heirs of the kingdom, we can lose the Spirit, that is, forfeit His intimacy,
enlightening, empowering, and filling. The greenish tint of the smaller inner sphere on the right is due to its
placement in the larger yellow sphere. The greenish tint is only perceived as greenish in relation to the larger sphere.
In actuality, if you were to cut the outer sphere open, the inner sphere would be found to be just as blue as ever.
       Trichotomy and bridal misthology lead to the perception that the outer yellow sealing is related to the soul
and is necessary to qualify to be the Bride of Christ. This perception is reinforced by Paul’s prayer in Eph 3:16-17:
“That He would grant you, according to the riches of His glory, to be strengthened with power through His Spirit
in the inner man, so that Christ may dwell in your hearts through faith.” This inner man is our regenerate spirit.
The Holy Spirit empowers us through our regenerate spirits so that Christ may dwell in our hearts/souls
experientially as we live by faith. In this way, we are filled with the Holy Spirit (Eph 5:18) in our hearts/souls and
thereby qualify to be the Bride of Christ, the perfection of misthological beauty (Eph 5:25-27).
                                       Illustration 50. Duality of Spirit’s Seal




       This illustration is repeated above from the vantage point of a threshold. The soteriological seal of the Spirit
is a free gift. That aspect of the seal cannot be lost. Over and above this level of sealing, however, is the
misthological sealing of the Spirit regarding our potential allocation in a share of the inheritance as kingdom rulers,
pictured as an engagement ring associated with the misthological Bride of Christ. Realization of this allocation is
contingent upon our faithfulness.
Appendix 2: Soteric-Mistholic Seal                                                                             Page 85
                                                  Seal of Perfection
       Once upon a time, Lucifer was the seal of perfection: “You had the seal of perfection, full of wisdom and
perfect in beauty” (Eze 28:12). That seal of perfection has now been passed on like a torch to Abraham and those
like him. Lucifer’s seal of spiritual perfection, notwithstanding, like Abraham’s seal of physical circumcision, is
only a prototype. The Bride will be God’s ultimate seal of perfection, shining more brightly than Lucifer ever could.
God used Lucifer’s fall to create the Bride (see DLF). God also used Abraham’s obedience to create the Bride. Both
were necessary to create the Bride. She is the personification of beauty since Her beauty has been forged through
the trials She faithfully has endured. She needed both the tempter and Her resistance to the temptation to become
the raving beauty that She is. Accordingly, She will not only replace Lucifer but sit on a throne higher than his
prototype throne. The fulfillment of the type must exceed the prototype. Lucifer’s seal is a prototype of the
misthological sealing of the Bride, which is a logical deduction outflowing from Abraham’s misthological seal.
God not only “sets a seal upon the stars” (Job 9:7), the Lord will set His seal of perfection on the Bride. She, in
turn, will be a seal over His heart (SSol 8:6).

                                                Conclusion
      One need not see duality on every occasion in which Paul discusses sealing. The sealing in 2Cor 1:22 should
probably be taken as exclusively soteriological, for example. Yet Paul’s propensity is to convey duality with a
soteriological foundation. He does not necessarily expect all his readers to perceive the duality, though. Some will;
some won’t. Chromostereopsis provides an example where modern readers might be able to relate to Paul’s
expectations. Consider a sign maker who uses a color scheme that will be perceived differently by different readers.
Certain colors can cause an illusion of 3D depth perception for some people.
                                       Illustration 51. Chromostereopsis Test




       At a recent birthday party at my house, my younger sister, Janina, asked if any of us had a problem with
floating text on signs. When she reads certain signs, some text appears to project out from the board while other
text seems to recede into the board. The overall effect is that of depth perception. I informed her that I have the
same perception. Other members of our family were skeptical. So I went and created a test case on my PC and had
them come and look at it. Some of the family experienced depth perception; some did not. This phenomenon and
discrepancy are attributable to chromostereopsis. When looking at a sign with red and blue text on a black
background, about 70% of viewers will perceive the blue text as projecting in front; about 30% will perceive the
red text as projecting in front (I fall into this category); and about 10% will perceive no projection (my brother falls
into this category). You can do a Google search for other examples and for more in-depth explanations.
Traditionally, glasses made for watching 3D movies had a blue lens and a red lens, for instance.
       A similar phenomenon occurs when discussing soteriological-misthological duality. Some people see such
texts as strictly soteriological; some people see them as strictly misthological; other readers see such texts as dual.
As for those who see the duality, some of them fail to understand the cause of the duality and confuse the two. On
Page 86                                                                                    Dual Sealing and Security
the other hand, some see the duality and understand the cause of the duality but disagree as to which aspect is
primary and which aspect is secondary. My perception of duality has increased over the years, and my perception
as to which aspect is primary varies according to the passage at hand. Sometimes I have trouble deciding which
aspect is primary and simply affirm both aspects without being able to determine primacy. Indeed, in such cases,
the biblical writers may have intended to affirm both aspects without making one primary over the other. The literary
artistry of the biblical writers might use variations to allow primacy on certain occasions while employing equality
on other occasions and singularity on yet other occasions.
       Paul’s discussion of sealing is an example of such literary variation. For those securitists who can only
perceive a soteriological dimension in Eph 1:14, I wish them well and have retained that perception from SAS in
chapters 1 and 2 of the present book. For those misthologists who perceive a duality, I offer appendixes 1 and 2 as
additions to that original discussion in SAS.
       Some texts, however, such as Rom 4:11 and 2Tim 2:19, require duality. These texts were not included in the
original discussion of sealing in SAS. For one thing, their duality had not yet been perceived at that point. Yet,
hindsight is twenty-twenty, as they say, and, in retrospect, their duality is apparent. Some texts, though, such as
2Cor 1:22, are probably best perceived as examples of singularity. The original soteriological argument of SAS
stands in this case and undergirds the soteriological perception of dualistic texts. Even so, a dualistic text such as
2Tim 2:19 still makes a strong case for the defense made in SAS for unconditional soteriological security. Rather
than weakening the case for unconditional soteriological security, accepting the reality of duality strengthens it. The
Arminian claim that the seal may be forfeited can be allowed as being half right. The misthological aspect of the
seal is forfeitable, but the soteriological aspect cannot be forfeited. Securitists can prove their case from either
singularity or from duality or from both. Forfeits are limited to a confusion derived from ill-advised fusion. They
fail to perceive that their position is derived from a composite of unconditional soteriology and conditional
misthology. They see green and fail to perceive that two lenses have been used to create this composite color.
      Excursus on Eaton’s Duality Regarding Eph 1:13-14
                                                    Introduction
       Eaton’s commentary on Ephesians is hard to acquire and makes a rather unique contribution to the discussion.
Consequently, I have provided chapter 4 of his commentary below. Like Chitwood and Hollandsworth, Eaton
believes the inheritance is a reward. Yet, unlike Chitwood, Eaton believes the seal and down payment of the Spirit
both refer to a gift aspect. Chitwood takes both the seal and down payment to be mistholic. Hollandsworth takes
the seal as soteric and the down payment as mistholic. In any event, like myself and Hollandsworth, Eaton is posing
a soteric-mistholic duality. We simply have different perceptions as to the most plausible way to express Paul’s
thinking along those lines. Eaton also differs from the other misthologists in posing an (exclusively) experiential
(rather than legal or ontological) interpretation for the seal and down payment. Yet, in some ways, his proposal
mirrors my hypothesis in explaining how believers might lose the Holy Spirit in a limited capacity.
                                               Eaton’s Commentary

                                                  (Ephesians 1:11-14)
              At the end of verse 10 Paul moved from the present to the future. God has a plan for the future,
      a plan ‘to bring together all things in Christ’. Now he speaks of our part in that plan.
              God starts this work of reuniting the universe - says Paul - by bringing into being a united human
      race under Christ. Paul refers first to Jewish Christians (‘we who first hoped in Christ’) and then goes
      on to mention the Ephesians who were gentile Christians (‘you also’). In this passage ‘we’ means the
      earliest generation of Jewish Christians; ‘you’ means ‘you gentile Christians at Ephesus’.
              First, he has something to say about Jewish Christians. ‘In Him also we were allocated an
      inheritance, having been predestined according to the purpose of Him who works all things according
      to the counsel of His will (1:11), in order that we might be to the praise of His glory, we who first
      hoped in Christ’ (1:12).
              1. Christians are allocated an inheritance. The translation here is disputed. (i) Sometimes it
      is taken to mean ‘we were chosen’ (see the New International Version - NIV). (ii) Sometimes it is
      taken to mean that we were ‘made an inheritance’ for God. The idea that God has believers as His
      inheritance is sometimes found in the Bible. (iii) Sometimes it is taken to mean that we have already
      obtained our inheritance; ‘we have obtained an inheritance’ says the old Authorised [sic.] Version. The
      first interpretation makes Paul repetitive; it twice has the idea of being chosen. The second
      interpretation is good biblical thinking but has no parallel in Ephesians. The third interpretation gives
      the impression that inheritance is automatically received with our initial salvation; this is out of
      harmony with Paul’s teaching elsewhere.
              The most likely translation is: ‘we were given an inheritance’ or ‘we were made heirs’ (see NIV
      footnote) - that is, people who will get an inheritance. I translate: ‘we were allocated an inheritance’.
      It is common biblical teaching that believers will ‘inherit’ the promises. They must persist in faith so
      as to get to their inheritance. Paul often mentions it after he has mentioned justification or sonship (as
      in Romans 8:16-17) and it seems he does the same here. He mentions the matter explicitly in l:18.
              2. The pathway of our life is also planned. We are allocated an inheritance, ‘being predestined
      according to the purpose of Him who works all things according to the counsel of His will...’. This
      means that not only is our actual future inheritance planned for us; also God has planned the course
      that we shall take in life to get to our inheritance. Our good works are foreordained (as Paul will say
      in 2:10). God is working everything together in our life so as to bring us to our destiny.
              3. Our fulfilling our destiny will bring glory to God. When we achieve God’s will for our life
      it will be ‘to the praise of His glory’. We shall get honour [sic.] for achieving something of God’s
      purpose, and He will get honour [sic.] too.
              Next, Paul tells us that gentiles will be involved in this also. In verses 11-12 Paul is speaking
      of ‘we who first hoped in Christ’, the first Christians who were all Jews. But now he says that gentile
      believers are destined for inheritance also (see what he will say in 3:6). He says, ‘In Christ also, having
      heard the word of truth, the gospel of your salvation, and having believed in Him, you were sealed
      with the Holy Spirit of promise (1:13), who is the down-payment of our inheritance until the redemption
Page 88                                                                                     Dual Sealing and Security
      of the possession, to the praise of His glory’ (1:14). The same salvation and inheritance that has come
      to Christian Jews has also come to Christian gentiles. The proof that Christian Jews and Christian
      gentiles are equal at this point is that gentiles have received the Holy Spirit just as Christian Jews have.
             After a person has heard the gospel and has trusted Jesus Christ, he can expect to receive the
      ‘seal’ of the Holy Spirit. We remember the story of Acts 10 and 11. When the gentiles first came into
      the church it was perfectly clear that God had accepted gentile believers because He gave them the
      outpouring of the Holy Spirit. Acts 10:44-47 and 11:15-18 are parallel passages to Ephesians 1:13.
             This gift of the Spirit is obviously a conscious experience. Paul is referring to more than a secret
      and hidden new birth. (i) It was an obviously experiential matter in Acts 10 and 11. (ii) Paul goes on
      to say that the seal of the Spirit is a downpayment [sic.] of our inheritance; and it is not possible to
      have a down-payment of heavenly glory without knowing about it. (iii) Similarly in Galatians 3:1-5
      Paul refers to a gift of the Spirit which follows simple faith. Paul was looking back to something that
      was well-known in their experience. (iv) How can a ‘seal’ be secret and hidden? A ‘seal’ is that which
      makes things certain and obvious. The outpouring of the Spirit is obvious to others (as in Acts 10:44,
      45) and it is surely obvious to the persons themselves (see Romans 8:15-17 which has much in common
      with this passage).
             The sealing of the Spirit is a foretaste of heavenly reward. You were sealed with the Holy Spirit
      of promise, says Paul, ‘who is the down-payment of our inheritance until the redemption of the
      possession, to the praise of His glory’ (1:14).
             The sealing of the Spirit is a down-payment of heavenly reward. It is a part of heavenly joy,
      empowerment and assurance that will be fully given to us in heaven. Paul says it is a down-payment
      until we get our full reward on the day when our final redemption comes to us, that is, on the day of
      resurrection. The sealing of the Spirit should motivate us to go after our final inheritance, because it is
      the foretaste of our final inheritance. The ‘sealing’ is an experience now of what we shall have in great
      abundance in heavenly glory. The person ‘saved through fire’ loses inheritance. The person ‘rewarded’
      receives a high level of glory. The ‘down-payment’ of the Spirit is a sample of the reward he is looking
      for. (Bold and italic his; highlight mine.)
                                                     Experiential Seal
       Eaton’s equation of the seal and down payment of the Holy Spirit with a conscious, experiential, outpouring
is questionable. Paul affirms, “The gifts and the calling of God are irrevocable” (Rom 11:29). Therefore, given
Eaton’s premise, this conscious, experiential, outpouring would be irrevocable. Yet neither Scripture or our
experience confirm this to be the case. Moreover, Eaton’s equation of the Spirit’s experiential outpouring with a
down payment given to make the attainment of a misthological inheritance possible would suggest that the Spirit’s
enablement is lost if the believer fails to attain the inheritance. Perhaps supplemental arguments could be provided,
however, for Eaton’s proposition that would make it more plausible. For example, TOD advocated that it was
possible for believers to lose their spiritual gifts and that the parable of the ten virgins suggests that believers can
lose their oil (representing the Holy Spirit) in the compacity that they currently are experiencing the Holy Spirit.
       As to spiritual gifts, TOD acknowledged that believers can hide their light and their talents. Spiritual gifts,
which would normally be experientially visible, can be hidden. The spiritual gift itself can be taken away (in the
sense of forfeited when it has run its allotted time), and as a result the opportunity to use that gift can be removed.
The talent, therefore, could represent the spiritual gift and its entailing responsibilities and opportunities to serve
the Lord which are forfeited. As TOD explains in an endnote:

             If it is objected that since spiritual gifts are forfeitable so the gift of eternal life must be
      forfeitable also, let it be remembered that spiritual gifts are not necessarily eternal (1Cor 3:8-10). The
      spiritual gift is not taken away before it has run its allotted course. The reward is in having the gift
      extend beyond its freely allotted time. For example, it is one thing for a firm to give an employee a car
      to use freely for an entire year. For the firm to transfer possession of that car over to the employee at
      the end of the year as a reward for exceptional service is quite another. The former might be considered
      a perk (perhaps even a gift); the latter definitely would be considered a reward, not a right for simply
      being an employee.
             A spiritual gift, as such, is a God-given energization for service during the allotted time in which
      that gift is intended to be used freely in performing that service. Apparently, even those who had the
Excursus on Selective Timeline for Abraham                                                                      Page 89
      gift of tongues, for example, lost the exercise of that gift within the course of their lifetime because
      the gift was not intended to run the course of their lifetime. The gift was expected to be in operation
      during their lifetime, not throughout their lifetime. What was given freely was the ability to exercise
      this gift for a limited period of time, not for an unlimited period of time.
              Spiritual gifts cease to be gifts that may be used freely once they have run their intended course.
      Even the gift of teaching will cease once a Christian teacher dies or is raptured because the capacity to
      use this gift freely was limited to his or her time on earth during the present advent. Whether that
      capacity to teach is granted again in heaven will be contingent upon one’s service on earth. If one has
      been a faithful teacher on earth, then one will be rewarded with increased ability and opportunity to
      teach in heaven. The talent that freely was given to be exercised on earth will be increased
      misthologically for exercise in heaven—if the believer has been faithful in the exercise of that talent
      on earth. Otherwise, that talent will be taken away and given to someone else.
              It is not the spiritual gift, as such, which is taken away since the stage of that capacity to serve
      as a gift already will have ceased. Rather, what is taken away and given to another as a reward is the
      capacity to perform that function (that was previously a gift but that has now become a reward after
      its allotted time is up). As to tongues, presumably there will be no need of this gift in heaven in order
      to talk to men or angels. However, what about talking to animals? Even if some animals are able to
      talk in heaven, it is unreasonable to think that all animals will be able to speak with equal clarity. Some
      animals may need an interpreter. Believers who have been faithful in their exercise of the gift of
      tongues or interpretation during their lifetimes on earth may find that they are able to talk to some
      animals in the new earth that no one else is able to talk with. Could it be that the stars actually sing
      and the creation really groans (Job 38:7; Rom 8:21)? Perhaps these believers will be able to commune
      with nature at a level no one else can fathom.

As to the oil in Mt 25:8-9, TOD also provided this explanation:

              Obviously, oil is not a reference to the Holy Spirit (at least not in regard to regeneration). The
      Holy Spirit is a person, not a substance of which you can have different degrees. You cannot have the
      Holy Spirit (or regeneration) in various degrees, but you can be filled with the Spirit to various degrees.
      Some of these regenerate believers were filled with the Spirit; some were not. What they lacked were
      reserves of spiritual oil. But what is indicated by their lacking spiritual reserves? They lack spiritual
      stamina. Some things that cannot be borrowed. For example, you cannot borrow someone’s fellowship
      with God. You hear people say, “I wish I had your faith and courage.” Yet it is impossible to give it to
      them. You must develop your own faith and courage. You can borrow someone’s lawn mower, but
      you cannot borrow his or her Christian maturity and strength of character. The oil represents Christian
      strength, growth, fruit, energy, and maturity—spiritual muscle so to speak. Some believers have
      spiritual reserves that they can draw upon; some do not.
              How does a believer obtain spiritual reservoirs? James said, “Consider it all joy, my brethren,
      when you encounter various trials, knowing that the testing of your faith produces endurance”
      (Jam 1:2-3). Only those believers who victoriously and joyfully draw upon the Lord’s strength in the
      midst of present difficulties will have the strength to endure victoriously and joyfully when the big
      trials come. Doing well on the various tests now will cause you to grow in spiritual maturity. You will
      produce the spiritual capacity that you need to endure. If you want to endure in the end (Mt 24:13),
      you should endure till the end. As believers, we should be developing our spiritual muscle. Believers
      are the ones who need to sing, “Give me oil in my lamp, keep me burning,” not unbelievers. Believers
      need to develop their spiritual muscle, not unbelievers. Unbelievers need spiritual life before they
      develop spiritual muscle. All these virgins had lamps and oil. They all had spiritual life and spiritual
      muscle. But some of them had more oil, more spiritual muscle, than others.
              Eight, the oil they lacked was not regeneration since they were told to “go...buy” (Mt 25:9).
      This is not talking about regeneration or salvation from hell because time and time again the Scripture
      tells us that salvation from hell is free. Those who are trying to buy their way into heaven with baptism,
      prayers, or church attendance are not going to make it. What the virgins lacked was not something that
      was free but something that was costly to them personally. The biblical distinction between a gift and
      a reward compels Marrowists to identify these foolish virgins as believers who had received the free
Page 90                                                                                      Dual Sealing and Security
       gift of eternal life but who did not fully follow the Lord in discipleship. They had been unwilling to
       pay the price. Wise virgins, on the other hand, are Marrowists who are willing to buy the gold, white
       garments, and eye salve necessary to enjoy the marriage feast (Rev 3:18).

       This argument might be expanded to say that the original oil was a gift, but the capacity to have oil until the
realization of the potential reward and beyond (and thus perform the torch dance) was dependent upon performance.
The sealing of the Spirit, in terms of the filling of the Spirit, is dependent upon not only having the Spirit but upon
having the Spirit’s energizing outpouring. Eaton’s argument has merit and has been anticipated with the discussion
of communion with the Spirit and Chitwood’s discussion of the oil of the virgins. Then again, one might pose
duality. Perhaps the sealing is not exclusively experiential. Perhaps it is legal as well and assures one’s unconditional
security. After all, even though Eaton insists that the inheritance is exclusively misthological, he nevertheless
affirms unconditional security. Our “justification, adoption and new birth...are eternally secure” (Glory, 95).
Moreover, Eaton hedges his statement regarding Eph 4:30: “The sealing of the Spirit gives us great assurance and
joy, but something of that joy will be lost if we grieve the Spirit by the way we walk” (Glory, 88). So, if we grieve
the Spirit to the point of failing to inherit the kingdom, do we lose the seal/joy entirely or only partially? Do we lose
all of it or only some of it? If we only lose some of the seal, then a soteric-mistholic duality is implied. The
soteriological aspect would be retained. In any event, in posing that the seal and down payment are gifts (and thus
soteriological benefits) while insisting that the inheritance is a reward (and thus a misthological benefit), Eaton
offers yet another soteric-mistholic model for duality.
                                                      Conclusion
      Various models by soteriologists and misthologists have been explored for the seal-pledge-inheritance
components of Eph 1:13-14. Arguments for both soteriological versus misthological exclusivity have been
evaluated, as well as for soteric-mistholic duality. The latter has been found more probable. Competing dualistic
models have been examined and the author’s preference stated. Yet allowance has been made for the competing
models as being worthy contenders. Just as a word has a semantic domain, the imagery Paul employs has a
hermeneutical range that makes it difficult, if not impossible, to pin Paul down to one dualistic model. Accordingly,
one must consider the plausibility that Paul did not intend to use the imagery in such a manner that would allow
him to be pinned down to one dualistic possibility.
                          Illustration 52. Soteric and Mistholic Options for Eph 1:13-14

          Interpreters                            Seal               Pledge/Earnest               Inheritance
 1.   Caulian\Dillowian                  S                          S                          S
 2.   Eaton                              S                          S                          M
 3.   Neo-Dillowian\Hollandsworth        S                          M                          M
 4.   Neo-Caulian1                       S                          S&M                        S&M
 5.   Neo-Caulian2                       S&M                        S&M                        S&M
 6.   Chitwood                           M                          M                          M
                                         S = soteric; M = mistholic
       The first interpretation is exclusively soteric and highlighted in blue accordingly. The last interpretation is
exclusively mistholic and consequently highlighted in yellow. Interpretations 2-5 are dualistic and thus highlighted
in a composite green. Of these dualistic models, interpretations 2 and 3 are offsetting in dualistic emphasis: number
2 is the most soteric; number 3 is the most mistholic. Number 4 is more dualistic. Number 5 is the most dualistic.
My preference is interpretation 5. Since the pledge/earnest is mistholic in interpretations 3-5, it is naturally
forfeitable in those models. Nevertheless, since it is simultaneously soteric in interpretations 4-5, it is also
nonforfeitable in some aspect. Similarly, the seal is concurrently forfeitable and nonforfeitable, in different aspects,
in interpretation 5. Notwithstanding the soteriological nature of the seal/pledge/earnest in interpretation 2, this
model poses that the soteriological seal/pledge/earnest can be lost without impinging unconditional security
because, according to this model, the function of the seal/pledge/earnest is not to assure unconditional security but
to make the misthological attainment of the inheritance experientially possible during the course of one’s life.
Naturally, the gift of this soteriological enablement expires when the course of one’s life is completed at the point
of death. The explanations offered by interpretations 2 and 5 concerning how the seal/pledge/earnest can be lost
need not be mutually exclusive.
             Excursus on Selective Timeline for Abraham
         The majority position is that Abraham was the eldest son, born when Terah was 70. The minority position
is that Abraham was the youngest son, born 60 years later when Terah was 130. The later position is based on two
factors. In terms of literary sequence, Moses placed Abraham’s departure form Haran after Terah’s death. Terah
died at 205. Abraham’s departure was at age 75. Thus, Terah would have been 130. However, since Moses and
Abraham and Paul considered a birth at age 100 to be miraculous (cp. Rom 4:19; Heb 11:12), the minority position
must be rejected. Abraham fathering a child at 100 would not be miraculous if his father had been 130 at the time
he fathered Abraham. Stephen’s seeming statement to the contrary in Acts 7:4 should be understood as referring to
the literary sequence. Stephen himself indicated as much when he said that God appeared to Abraham in Ur
(Acts 7:1). This deduction was made by Philo and Josephus and various commentators concur (LCHS; NICNT;
NAC; TNTC). Contextually, for the same reason, we must deduce that Moses is not giving the chronologically
sequence in Gen 12:1. Grammatically, there is reason to believe this to be the case also. Abraham departed from
Haran 60 years before Terah died.

                                              The 430 starts in UR:
      •   “And He said to him, ‘I am the Lord who brought you out of Ur of the Chaldeans, to give you this land to
