                                  Pivotal Point of Computation at Isaac’s Birth




                                                  Pivotal Questions:
       1. Was Stephen speaking of the chronological order or narrative order? If chronological, then “Abraham was
not born until Terah was 130 [205 - 75 = 130].” NICNT rightly says that this is “improbable.” This proposal runs
counter to the narrative since it would make Abraham having a child at 100 a natural rather than supernatural event.
Since the chronological order is not tenable, we must deduce that Stephen was simply relating the narrated order.
       2. Was Gen 12:1-3 originally spoken at this chronological location, thus in Haran? No. Originally, it was
spoken in Ur. Then reiterated in Haran. The narrative location supplies the reiteration at the point of the second call.
       3. How do we reconcile the 400 versus 430 years? Basically, there are two schools of thought. The Israelites
spent 400 years in slavery. Or the time is split between their preliminary sojourn in Canaan versus their slavery in
Egypt. The split hypothesis, and thus the LXX reading of Ex 12:40, is preferable.
       4. If we split the time, does the 400 years start at Isaac’s birth or five years later at Ismael’s mocking? Since
the 430 years apparently starts 30 years earlier and since it apparently starts at Ur rather than Canaan (per pivot
question 2), starting the 400 years at Isaac’s birth is preferable.
Page 94                                                                                        Dual Sealing and Security
                                                           Notes
      1. Ur (Abraham’s first calling):
             a. “After” (not necessarily “when”) Terah was 70, he had three sons (Abraham, Nahor, and Haran) in
                 the city of the Chaldeans in the land of Mesopotamia (Gen 11:26-29).
                       i. Heran became the father of Lot.
                      ii. Heran died while in Ur while his father Terah was still alive.
                     iii. Nahor married Heran’s daughter, Milkah.
                     iv. Abram married Sarai, who was Terah’s daughter by a different mother (Gen 20:11-12).
                      v. Terah and possibly his family were idolaters (Josh 24:2).154
             b. God appeared to Abraham while Abraham was still living in Ur (Gen 15:7; Neh 9:7-8; Acts 7:2)
                       i. At least by this point, Abraham became a believer (Heb 11:8).155
                      ii. God told Abraham: “Leave your country and your people...and go to the land I will show
                          you.” (Acts 7:3)
                     iii. Abraham “did not know where he was going” (Heb 11:8).
                     iv. Abraham left Ur and settled in Harran (Acts 7:4a).
      2. Haran (Abraham’s Second Calling):
             a. Terah left Ur and took Abram, Sarai, and Lot, and they set out together from UR for Canaan.
                 (Gen 11:31-32).
                       i. Instead of going all the way to Canaan, they settled at Harran when they came there.
                      ii. Terah was 205 when he died in Harran.
             b. In terms of the sequence of the narration, after Terah died, God sent Abraham to the land of Canaan
                 (Acts 7:4b).
             c. We have two translative options:
                       i. “The Lord had said to Abram, “Go from your country, your people and your father’s
                          household to the land I will show you” (Gen 12:1).
                               1. If this pluperfect tense translation is correct, then this must be the first call that
                                   Abraham had while still in Ur.
                               2. Stephen abbreviated it, and Abraham did not fully obey since he was to leave his
                                   father. Instead, Terah took Abraham.156

154
    Josh 24:2 seems to indicate that Torah, Nahor, and Abram were originally idolaters: “And Joshua said to all the
people, ‘Thus says the Lord, the God of Israel, ‘From ancient times your fathers lived beyond the River, namely,
Terah, the father of Abraham and the father of Nahor, and they served other gods.’” But Ross interprets Gen 31:53
as saying that the true God (Yahweh) was “the God of Abraham and the God of Nahor, the God of their father.”
Yahweh is invoked to judge. Ross suggests that, although Terah (and his two sons, Nahor and Abram) retained a
knowledge of Yahweh, they worshipped pagan gods until Yahweh called Abram. Allen P. Ross, Creation &
Blessing: A Guide to the Study and Exposition of Genesis (Grand Rapids, MI: Baker Books, 1998), 258. However,
WBC points out that the verb judge is plural, thus indicating plural gods. If this is the case, then Abram may have
worshiped Yahweh, while his father and brother were associated more so with pagan gods. But the LXX, though
admitting Terah, has a singular verb. Accordingly, one possibility suggested by NAC is that Terah and his ancestors
were the polytheists. K&D holds that Terah and Nahor were polytheistic but Abram was monotheistic. In short, the
biblical text leaves room for the proposition that Abram was a believer in Yahweh before he was called in Ur. This
proposition is probable. The elaboration provided by Jasher may well be an embellishment based on historical fact.
Even so, it provides plausible witness to the probable deduction that Abram was a believer before he was called in
Ur to go to Canaan. (Some of the quotations in this excurses will be from the NIV.)

          If Shem was Melchizedek, then the statement that he was without father or mother or genealogy in Heb 7:3
is speaking typologically, based on the details (and lack thereof) in the narrative. A typological understanding of
Melchizedek is common among commentators (GNTC, LCHS, NIBC, NICNT, WBC). The OT details do not
provide the background for Melchizedek. We can only derive the conclusion that Shem was Melchizedek from
logical inference and extra biblical material. This lack of explicit OT background for Melchizedek’s origin is useful
for typology and allows the writer of Hebrews to use Melchizedek legitimately as a type. Likewise, the lack of
explicit OT background for Abram’s original faith is useful for Paul’s assertion that Abraham’s original recorded
imputation of righteousness, based on his faith, was soteriological. Typologically, this is true. Nevertheless, through
logical inference, we may infer that this recorded faith was actually misthological. Even so, Paul’s point is still valid
historically in that Abram’s original faith, which is not recorded, would have been soteriological. In any case,
typologically, Abram’s recorded faith may be used typologically to draw the inference that would have been derived
historically if his original faith had been recorded.

                        Abraham departed since Tarah was 70 when Abraham was born and since Abraham
                        left Harran 75 years later (205 - 75 - 70 = 60).
      3. Canaan
                     i. Abraham “took his wife Sarai, his nephew Lot, all the possessions they had accumulated and
                        the people they had acquired in Harran, and they set out for the land of Canaan, and they
                        arrived there” (Gen 12:5).
                    ii. Hagar gives birth to Ishmael when Abraham was 86 (Gen 16:15-16).
                   iii. Abraham’s name is changed when he was 99 and he is circumcised (Gen 17:1-14).
                   iv. Isaac is born when Abraham is 100 (Gen 21:1-8).
                             1. The most plausible model is that the countdown of 400 years for Abraham’s
                                 descendants to be sojourners starts when Isaac was born in fulfillment and,
                                 parenthetically, Abraham’s subsequent generations would be enslaved until the
                                 exodus (Gen 15:13; Acts 7:6-7).
                             2. But they do not come out until 430 years later (Ex 12:40-41; Gal 3:17). So why the
                                 30 year discrepancy? Various options are possible.
                                     a. If the 430 started 30 years before the 400 years, it started at the time of
                                         Abraham’s first call in Haran (when he entered Canaan). JCPS: “His
                                         departure from Haran was 430 years before the children of Israel departed
                                         from Egypt (Exod. 12:40).”
                                                i. See https://www.harpscrossing.com/wp-
                                                   content/uploads/2014/04/Timeline-From-Abram-to-Exodus.pdf.
                                                   The chart at this site is good but wrongly believes that Abraham
                                                   was the youngest son.
                                               ii. This would make Abram 70 when God first appeared to Him.
                                             iii. See Classical Rabbinic Solution here: https://thetorah.com/how-
                                                   many-years-were-the-israelites-in-egypt/. The 400 starts from
                                                   Isaac’s birth (thus sojourn), and the 430 goes back 30 years before
                                                   that to Abram in Ur. Granted, this site objects to the classic rabbinic
                                                   view, but I do not find its objection convincing.
                                              iv. The competing view is attractive:
                                                   https://answersingenesis.org/bible-questions/how-long-were-the-
                                                   israelites-in-egypt. The 400 starts 5 years after Isaac’s birth when
                                                   Ishmael mocks him (thus affliction), and the 430 goes back 30
                                                   years before that to Abram in Haran.
                                               v. So do we date the 400 from the start of the sojourn or from the start
                                                   of the affliction? What gives the decisive edge to one view versus
                                                   the other? The Classical Rabbinic Solution is preferable. The 430
                                                   goes back to Ur per Gen 15:7; Neh 9:7; Acts 7:2.158
                                              vi. LCHS and GNTC say that Paul is using the LXX of Ex 12:40 which
                                                   adds and in Canaan. In that case, the classic view is best. This
                                                   reading is adopted herein.
                                             vii. WBC: “Paul here is probably not relying on Exod 12:40 versus
                                                   Gen 15:13, but only repeating the traditionally accepted number of
                                                   years for the time span between the Abrahamic covenant and the
                                                   Mosaic law.” But that traditional number is based on Ex 12:40. And
                                                   the LXX would start the clock ticking in Canaan.
                                     b. BKC thinks that the LXX views “conflicts with the clear statement in
                                         Exodus 12:40 that the Egyptian sojourn was 430 years.” It poses later starting
                                         points, either “the confirming of the Abrahamic Covenant with Jacob
